The Objection from Evil and Its Kalam Reply
An objection about evil and the kalam reply using logical symmetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical theological objection from evil and how the reply uses logical symmetry to defend the compatibility of divine knowledge, power, and mercy.

Core Idea
The problem of evil is met with a logical mirror: order implies knowledge, but consistency requires accepting both.

This lesson presents a classical objection about evil and suffering that challenges the Islamic concept of a merciful, wise, and powerful God. The objector sets up a dilemma: either God created for wisdom, which evil contradicts, or for mere power, which undermines the need for wisdom. The reply does not dismiss evil but uses the objector's own logical standard to defend divine attributes. By the end, you'll see how kalam reasoning turns the argument back on itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation together. Note how the objector first acknowledges order, then introduces evil, forming a dilemma. Then read the reply and see how it reframes the issue.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the logical moves.
2
Identify the objector's dilemma
Look for the two alternatives: creation for wisdom/benefit or for mere power. Understand why both seem to lead to a problem for divine attributes.
The objector claims evil disproves either wisdom or power, and if mere power suffices, order doesn't need knowledge.
3
Analyze the reply's first move
The reply clarifies that corrupt actions don't prove ignorance because a knower can do corrupt things. See why this is a preliminary clarification, not the main answer.
This prepares the ground for the deeper logical symmetry argument.
4
Grasp the logical symmetry argument
The reply argues: just as order implies knowledge, so goodness implies mercy/power. If you allow one inference, you must allow the other. Evil exists, but that doesn't negate attributes; it's a matter of interpretation.
This is the core of the kalam response: consistency in reasoning.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how this reply does not solve the problem of evil but shows that objections relying on logical inference can be turned around. Think about the limits of this approach.
This lesson is about the method, not a full theodicy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : إنا كما رأينا في هذا العالم أفعالاً محكمة متقنة منسقة منظمة وأنها توهم كون مدير هذا العالم عالماً حكيماً ، فقد نرى هذا العالم أيضاً مملوءاً من الآفات والمخافات والتشويهات في الخلقة والأحوال المنفرة مثل العمي والزمانة والفقر الشديد . ونرى الإنسان الكامل في العلم والعمل محروماً عن الخيرات الدنيوية ، ذليلاً مهيناً ، تحت تصرف الجهال والأراذل ونرى أخس الخلق مثل أرذل الصبيان والنسوان يحصل لهم الاستيلاء على أهل الدنيا والاستعلاء على الخلق وهذه الأحوال غير لائقة بالرحيم العليم الحكيم ، وتقريره إنه تعالى إما أن يقال : إنه خلق هذا العالم لرعاية حكمة ومصلحة ، أو خلقه لمحض القدرة من غير رعاية الحكمة والمصلحة. فإن كان الحق هو الثاني فحينئذ هذه الوجوه الحاصلة من المصالح ما قصد الفاعل خلق العالم لأجلها ، بل اتفق أنه خصص هذا القسم بالإيجاد والتكوين دون سائر الوجوه من غير أن كان كونها مصلحة ، علة لرجحان هذا القسم على سائر الأقسام ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : مجرد القدرة كافي في إخراج هذه الأشياء من العدم إلى الوجود ؟ وأما إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى خلق هذا العالم لأجل الرحمة والحكمة وإفاضة المنفعة . فنقول : لو كان الأمر كذلك لما خلق فيها أنواع الآفات والمخافات . وحيث حصلت هذه الأشياء ، علمنا أنها إنما حصلت إما لعدم العلم ، وإما لعدم القدرة . وأجيب عن هذا السؤال . فقيل : الإحكام والإتقان يدل على علم الفاعل، لأن الجاهل يمتنع أن يصدر عنه الفعل المحكم المتقن . أما الأفعال الفاسدة الخسيسة فإنها لا تدل على جهل الفاعل لأن العالم يمكنه أن يأتي بالأفعال الفاسدة الخسيسة . فعلم أنه ليس المقصود من السؤال المذكور ما توهمه هذا المجيب ، بل المقصود أنه كما قضى ظاهر العقل بأن الفعل المحكم المتقن لا يصدر إلا من العالم ، قضى ظاهر العقل بأن من كان موصوفاً بالقدرة وبالعلم وبالرحمة. فإنه لا يسلط على الضعفاء أنواع الآلام والأوجاع ، ولا يسلط القوي الظالم على الضعيف المظلوم. وحيث شاهدنا هذه الأحوال في العالم لزم القدح في أحد هذه الصفات الثلاثة . فإن جاز لكم أن تثبتوا كمال القدرة والرحمة مع أن العقل يفضي بأن حصول هذه الصفات الثلاثة يمنع من إيصال المضار والآلام إلى الضعفاء فلا يجوز لغيركم أن يقول بأن يحصل هذا القدر من الإحكام والإتقان في أجزاء العالم، من غير أن يكون الفاعل عالماً ، بل يكفي فيه كونه ظاناً .
The fifth question: Indeed, just as we see in this world actions that are precise, perfect, arranged, and organized, and that they give the impression that the manager of this world is knowing and wise, we also see this world filled with afflictions, fears, deformities in creation and repugnant conditions such as blindness, chronic illness, and extreme poverty. And we see the human perfect in knowledge and action deprived of worldly goods, humiliated, under the control of the ignorant and base. And we see the basest of creation, like the vilest of boys and women, gaining domination over the people of the world and ascendancy over creation. These conditions are not befitting the Merciful, the Knowing, the Wise. Its formulation: either it is said that He, exalted, created this world for the sake of wisdom and benefit, or He created it for mere power without regard to wisdom and benefit. If the truth is the second, then these beneficial aspects that occur—the agent did not intend to create the world for them, but rather it so happened that He specified this portion for existence and formation over other portions, without that benefit being the cause for the preponderance of this portion over other portions. And if this is the case, then why is it not permissible to say that mere power is sufficient to bring these things from non-existence into existence? And if we say that He, exalted, created this world for mercy, wisdom, and the bestowal of benefit, we say: if this were the case, He would not have created in it the various kinds of afflictions and fears. And since these things have occurred, we know that they occurred either due to lack of knowledge or lack of power. And this question was answered. It was said: perfection and precision indicate the knowledge of the agent, because the ignorant cannot produce a precise and perfect action. As for corrupt and base actions, they do not indicate the ignorance of the agent, because the knower can produce corrupt and base actions. Thus it is known that the intended meaning of the mentioned question is not what this responder imagined. Rather, the intended meaning is that just as the outward intellect decrees that a precise, perfect action only issues from the knower, so too the outward intellect decrees that whoever is described with power, knowledge, and mercy would not inflict upon the weak various kinds of pains and sorrows, nor would He empower the strong oppressor over the weak oppressed. Since we have witnessed these conditions in the world, it necessitates impugning one of these three attributes. If it is permissible for you to affirm complete power and mercy while the intellect holds that the possession of these three attributes prevents the delivery of harms and pains to the weak, then it is not permissible for others to claim that this degree of perfection and precision occurs in the parts of the world without the agent being knowing; rather, it suffices for it that He be an opinion-holder.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer concerning the problem of evil. The objector begins by noting that while the world shows precision and order suggesting a wise and knowing director, it is also full of afflictions, deformities, and injustices—such as the righteous suffering while the wicked prosper. These are incompatible with a merciful, all-knowing, wise God. The objector then poses a dilemma: either God created the world for wisdom and benefit, or for sheer power without wisdom. If the latter, then mere power suffices for creation, negating the need for wisdom. If the former, then why are there evils? Their presence would indicate either lack of knowledge or lack of power. The answer first clarifies that the presence of crookedness does not prove ignorance, since a knower can produce corrupt acts. The real point is that the same logical reasoning used to infer knowledge from order is also used to infer that a knowing, powerful, merciful God would not permit evil. Since evil exists, it seems one attribute must be denied. The answer refutes this by turning the argument back: if the objector accepts that God is powerful and merciful despite apparent contradictions, then he cannot deny that the order implies knowledge. The answer establishes that the objector's demand for consistency applies equally to both sides.

الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the reply simply denies the reality of evil or dismisses it as unimportant.
Correction: The reply actually accepts the existence of evil but uses logical symmetry to argue that if the objector accepts divine mercy and power despite evil, he cannot deny divine knowledge from order.

Checkpoint
According to the reply, why does the existence of corrupt actions not prove the agent's ignorance?
A) Because corrupt actions can only come from an ignorant agent.
B) Because a knowing agent is also capable of producing corrupt actions.
C) Because corrupt actions are not real actions.
D) Because the agent's knowledge is irrelevant to the quality of actions.

Key Takeaway
The kalam reply defends divine attributes by showing that the objection's logical inference—order implies knowledge—must be applied consistently to mercy and power as well, preserving the coherence of all three.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God is merciful. When you see suffering around you, do you doubt His mercy? Or do you rationalize it away without letting it touch your soul? Will you let this lesson's logical symmetry challenge your easy faith, or will you examine the inconsistency in your own heart?
?
Have you ever used the existence of evil as an excuse to distance yourself from God? This lesson shows the coherence of divine attributes. Will you now confront the problem of evil not as a logical puzzle but as a test of your trust in His wisdom?
?
You affirm God's knowledge from order, but do you affirm His mercy from your own life's hardships? This lesson demands consistency. What will you change today so that your belief is not selective?

Objection: The Problem of Evil and God's Attributes
An objection linking evil to doubt about God's attributes via parity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the problem of evil as a parity argument against the design argument for God's existence.

Core Idea
Evil and suffering challenge the inference to a perfect Creator just as order supports it.

The objection presented here is a classic challenge to the teleological argument: if the apparent order in the world points to a wise Creator, then the apparent evil and suffering should point to the absence of one or more divine attributes. This passage lays out that parity argument, forcing the theist to confront the problem of evil. As you read, notice how the objector uses the same rational principle the theist relies on.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the logical structure become clear.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the parity argument
Notice how the objector says 'just as... so too...' — that is the core: what applies to order also applies to evil.
The phrase 'كما قضى ظاهر العقل بأن... قضى ظاهر العقل بأن' signals the parallel.
3
Recognize the three attributes
The objector names power, knowledge, and mercy as attributes that would prevent evil if truly possessed. Find them in the text.
These three are the standard divine attributes in classical theism.
4
Understand the conclusion
The objector concludes that if the theist can use order to prove knowledge, then the atheist can use evil to prove the creator is not all-knowing (or not all-powerful, etc.).
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the design argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجيب ، بل المقصود أنه كما قضى ظاهر العقل بأن الفعل المحكم المتقن لا يصدر إلا من العالم ، قضى ظاهر العقل بأن من كان موصوفاً بالقدرة وبالعلم وبالرحمة. فإنه لا يسلط على الضعفاء أنواع الآلام والأوجاع ، ولا يسلط القوي الظالم على الضعيف المظلوم. وحيث شاهدنا هذه الأحوال في العالم لزم القدح في أحد هذه الصفات الثلاثة . فإن جاز لكم أن تثبتوا كمال القدرة والرحمة مع أن العقل يفضي بأن حصول هذه الصفات الثلاثة يمنع من إيصال المضار والآلام إلى الضعفاء فلا يجوز لغيركم أن يقول بأن يحصل هذا القدر من الإحكام والإتقان في أجزاء العالم، من غير أن يكون الفاعل عالماً ، بل يكفي فيه كونه ظاناً .
The respondent: Rather, the intended meaning is that just as the apparent judgment of the intellect ruled that a well-ordered, perfect act does not proceed except from a knowing (agent), so the apparent judgment of the intellect ruled that one who is described by power, knowledge, and mercy does not inflict upon the weak types of pains and sorrows, nor does he empower the strong oppressor over the weak oppressed. And when we observe these conditions in the world, it necessitates casting doubt upon one of these three attributes. For if it is permissible for you to affirm perfect power and mercy while reason dictates that the possession of these three attributes prevents bringing harms and pains to the weak, then it is not permissible for others to say that this amount of skill and perfection (ihkam wa itqan) occurs in the parts of the world without the agent being knowing; rather, it suffices for it to be conjectural.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the theist who argues from design (ihkam wa itqan) to a wise creator. The objector argues by parity of reasoning: if the apparent order in the world proves a wise creator, then the apparent evil and suffering in the world should prove that the creator lacks one of three attributes: power, knowledge, or mercy. The argument is that we see the weak suffering and the strong oppressing, which is inconsistent with a being who is all-powerful, all-knowing, and all-merciful. Therefore, if you (the theist) are allowed to use order to prove knowledge, then others are allowed to use evil to prove that the creator is not all-knowing or not all-powerful. This is a classic objection from the problem of evil against classical theism.

Key Terms
ظاهر العقل
الفعل المحكم المتقن
القدرة
العلم
الرحمة
الإحكام والإتقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is a denial of God's attributes or a statement of atheism.
Correction: The objector is not claiming that God lacks attributes, but using a logical parity argument to challenge the design argument. The point is that if order proves knowledge, then evil should disprove it, leading to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about the three attributes (power, knowledge, mercy) in light of observed evil?
A) They are all present perfectly despite evil.
B) The presence of evil casts doubt on at least one of them.
C) Evil disproves the existence of any of them.
D) Evil is an illusion and does not affect the attributes.

Key Takeaway
The problem of evil is used as a parity argument to challenge the design argument, asserting that evil casts doubt on at least one of God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this passage's parity argument force you to confront that doubt rather than ignore it? What specific act of trust will you commit to today in the face of this tension?
?
The objector says that if you use order to prove God's knowledge, then you must accept that evil casts doubt on His attributes. Have you ever used the beauty of creation to bolster your faith while avoiding the challenge of evil? What would it mean for you to stop avoiding that inconsistency and instead seek a deeper response from revelation?
?
This argument suggests that reason alone leads to a dead end. Do you actually rely on reason more than revelation when defending your faith? Will you humbly admit that your rational arguments are vulnerable, and turn back to the Qur'an and Sunnah for the only satisfying answer to the problem of evil? Why Ash'ari Atomism Undermines Design
The objector argues that Ash'ari's atomism renders design (ihkam wa itqan) meaningless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalist atomism appears to undermine the teleological argument from design, and how the objector uses a trilemma to argue that each atom could independently possess life.

Core Idea
If atoms can have life without bodies, then design—which depends on structure—is meaningless.

This passage presents a powerful internal critique of the Ash'ari position. The objector does not deny God's existence but argues that the Ash'ari denial of necessary causal connections between structure and function makes it impossible to speak of 'design' in the usual sense. The argument proceeds by first explaining Ash'ari's view that individual atoms can possess life and attributes, then using a trilemma to support that view, and finally concluding that if structure has no real effect, then the design argument collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's main claim
Begin by reading the opening lines where the objector states that 'ihkam wa itqan' is unintelligible on Ash'ari's school. Notice the bold assertion.
This sets the stage for the entire argument.
2
Understand Ash'ari's position on atoms and life
The passage explains that according to Ash'ari, bodily structure is not a condition for life. An individual atom can possess life, knowledge, power, hearing, and sight. This is a radical claim.
This is the key premise that leads to the problem.
3
Analyze the trilemma proof
The objector presents Ash'ari's proof: if a structure is composed of parts, either each part is receptive to life only if others are (circular), or some are receptive and others not (requires a distinguisher), or each is independent. The first two are invalid, so the third holds: each atom is independently receptive.
This is a formal logical argument.
4
Consider the reply about habit
When confronted with the fact that destruction of the body ends life, Ash'ari replies that this is merely a habitual occurrence (ʿada), not a necessary connection. This is the occasionalist position.
This is crucial: it denies any real causal link between structure and function.
5
Draw the conclusion for the design argument
If structure has no effect, then we cannot say that the eye is created for sight or the stomach for digestion. The very concept of design (ihkam) is lost. Therefore, the Ash'ari cannot use design as evidence for God's knowledge.
This is the final devastating implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : إن القول بالإحكام والإتقان على مذهب أبي الحسن الأشعري غير معقول . وبيانه ، أن عنده البنية ليست شرطاً لحصول الحياة ولسائر الصفات المشروطة بالحياة كالعلم والقدرة والسمع والبصر . فإن مذهبه : أن الجوهر الفرد لا يمتنع اتصافه بالحياة وبالعلم وبالقدرة حتى يكون ذلك الجوهر الفرد أفضل خلق الله في العلم وأقواهم في القدرة وفي سائر الكمالات من السمع والبصر . واحتج على صحة مذهبه : بأن هذه البنية مؤلفة من الأجزاء. فإما أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء قابلاً للحياة وللعلم وللقدرة مشروطاً يكون الآخر كذلك. وإما أن تحصل هذه الحاجة من أحد الجانبين دون الثاني . وإما أن يكون كل واحد منها غنياً عن الآخر في هذه القابلية والأول يوجب الدور ، والثاني يوجب امتياز أحد المثلين عن الآخر بحكم لازم ، ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت الثالث وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت أنه يصح في كل واحد من هذه الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ أن يكون موصوفاً بالحياة والعلم والقدرة والسمع والبصر فإذا قيل له : إنا نرى أنه متى اختلت هذه البنية واختلت هذه الأعضاء فإنه تبطل الحياة والعلم والقدرة قال : ذاك بحسب عادة أجراها الله تعالى . فإما أن يقال : إن حصول الحياة مفتقر إلى حصول هذه البنية . وحصول الإبصار والسماع مفتقر إلى خلقة العين والأذن . فليس الأمر كذلك . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن على مذهبه ليس لشيء من التركيبات والتصورات أثر في حصول شيء من المنافع والمصالح . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يمكننا أن نقول : إنه تعالى إنما خلق العين على هذه الخلقة لأجل أن يكمل بها الإبصار . وإنما خلق المعدة على هذا الوجه ، لأجل أن يكمل بها فعل الهضم. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يبطل ما يذكره أصحاب التشريح من منافع الأعضاء ونحن لا نفهم من لفظ الإحكام والإتقان إلا هذه الوجوه . فإذا بينا أن شيئاً منها لا يصح على مذهب الأشعري . فقد بطل الإحكام والإتقان على مذهبه ، فكيف يمكنه يستدل بالإحكام والإتقان على علم الفاعل القادر ؟
The fifth question: The statement of skill and perfection (ihkam wa itqan) according to the school of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is unintelligible. Its explanation: According to him, constitution (structure) is not a condition for the occurrence of life nor for the rest of the attributes conditioned by life, such as knowledge, power, hearing, and sight. For his doctrine is that the individual atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is not prevented from being qualified by life, knowledge, and power, such that that individual atom would be the best of God's creation in knowledge and the strongest of them in power and in the rest of perfections like hearing and sight. And he argued for the correctness of his doctrine: that this structure is composed of parts. Either every one of those parts is receptive to life, knowledge, and power, conditioned by the other being likewise; or this need occurs from one side without the second; or every one of them is independent of the other in this receptivity. The first necessitates circularity, and the second necessitates the distinction of one of the two similar things from the other by a necessary judgment. And when these two divisions were invalidated, the third is established. And when this is established, it is established that it is valid for each one of these indivisible parts to be qualified by life, knowledge, power, hearing, and sight. So if it is said to him: 'We see that when this structure is disturbed and these organs are disturbed, life, knowledge, and power are nullified,' he says: 'That is according to a habit (ʿada) that God has caused to run.' So it is not to be said that the occurrence of life is dependent on the occurrence of this structure, and the occurrence of sight and hearing is dependent on the creation of the eye and ear. It is not so. When you understand this, we say: According to his doctrine, there is no effect for any of the compositions and forms in the occurrence of any of the benefits and interests. And on this assumption, we cannot say: He, exalted, created the eye in this form in order to perfect sight through it, and created the stomach in this manner in order to perfect the act of digestion through it. And on this assumption, what the anatomists mention about the benefits of organs is invalidated. We do not understand from the expression 'skill and perfection' (ihkam wa itqan) except these aspects. So when we show that none of them is valid according to Ash'ari's doctrine, then skill and perfection are invalid according to his doctrine. So how can he use skill and perfection to deduce the knowledge of the powerful agent?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a fifth objection against the Ash'ari school's ability to use the argument from design. The objector begins by stating that the concept of 'skill and perfection' (al-ihkam wa al-itqan) is unintelligible on Ash'ari's principles. He explains that Ash'ari denies that bodily structure (al-binya) is a necessary condition for life and its attendant attributes (knowledge, power, hearing, sight). Instead, Ash'ari holds that a single indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) can possess these attributes, making it the most perfect creature. Ash'ari's proof is based on a trilemma: if a structure is composed of parts, then either (1) each part is receptive to life only if the others are, leading to circularity; or (2) some parts are receptive and others not, requiring a distinguishing factor between similar things; or (3) each part is independent in receptivity. Since (1) and (2) are invalid, (3) is established, implying each atom can have life independently. To the obvious objection that destruction of the body ends life, Ash'ari replies that this is merely a habitual occurrence (ʿada) ordained by God, not a necessary connection. The objector then draws the devastating conclusion: if structure and organs have no real effect on functions, then terms like 'for the sake of' (li-ajl) lose meaning—the eye is not created for seeing, the stomach not for digestion. This negates the very concept of design (ihkam), which depends on the fitting of means to ends. Hence, the Ash'ari cannot use design as evidence for God's knowledge. This is a powerful internal critique of occasionalist atomism.

Key Terms
الإحكام والإتقان
الجوهر الفرد
البنية
العادة
التركيبات والتصورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'ari occasionalism is compatible with the design argument because God still creates the world with order.
Correction: The objector argues that if structure has no causal role, then the order is merely coincidental and cannot be used as evidence of design, because the concept of 'for the sake of' presumes a genuine means-end relationship.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does Ash'ari's denial of structural conditions for life make the design argument invalid?
A) Because design requires a necessary connection between structure and function, which Ash'ari rejects.
B) Because Ash'ari believes the world is eternal.
C) Because Ash'ari denies the existence of atoms.
D) Because Ash'ari believes God is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari denial of necessary causal connections between structure and function undermines the very concept of design, making the design argument self-defeating for an Ash'ari.

Alternative Proof for God's Conceptual Knowledge
New proof for divine knowledge based on God's free agency, starting with conceptual knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternative proof for God's conceptual knowledge based on His nature as a free agent by choice.

Core Idea
Intention to create requires prior conception.

In this passage, the author presents a new, stronger proof for God's knowledge. Instead of looking at the perfection of creation, the proof focuses on God's agency: He creates by choice. This simple yet profound argument shows that if God is a free agent, He must have conceptual knowledge of everything He creates. Let's examine the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: starting with a division of knowledge, then focusing on conception.
The text is from a chapter on divine knowledge.
2
Identify the key premise
The proof hinges on the claim that a free agent acts by intention, and intention requires conception. Identify this logical link.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that God must possess conceptual knowledge of all essences. Ask yourself: does this proof also cover assent?
The passage explicitly separates conception and assent; only conception is proved here.
4
Reflect on the proof's strength
The author claims this proof is stronger than the argument from design. Why? Consider that it does not rely on observation of contingent things.
This connects to the broader debate on divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث
في
تقرير طريقة أخرى سوى طريقة الإتقان والإحكام تدل على كونه تعالى عالماً بناء على كونه تعالى فاعلاً بالاختيار لا مجبراً بالذات

اعلم أن ههنا طريقاً آخر أقوى من دليل الإحكام والإتقان

وتقريره: أن نقول: قد عرفت أن العلم إما تصور وإما تصديق. فنقول: أما تصور الحقائق والماهيات فحاصل لله تعالى. والدليل عليه: هو أنه قد ثبت أنه قادر مختار، والقادر المختار إنما يفعل بواسطة القصد إلى التكوين والتخليق، والقصد إلى التكوين والتخليق مشروط بتصور تلك الحقائق، فإن لم يكن متصوراً لماهية من الماهيات امتنع منه القصد إلى تكوينها وتخليقها. والعلم بذلك ضروري فيثبت أنه تعالى متصور لهذه الماهيات.
The third chapter: On establishing another method besides the method of perfection and precision that indicates that He, exalted, is knowing, based on His being an agent by choice, not compelled by essence. Know that there is another way, stronger than the proof of perfection and precision. Its exposition: We say: You have known that knowledge is either conception (تصور) or assent (تصديق). We say: As for the conception of realities and essences, it is present for God, exalted. The evidence for it is that it has been established that He is a powerful, free agent (قادر مختار), and the free agent only acts by means of intention to bring about and create. And intention to bring about and create is conditional upon the conception of those realities. For if He is not a conceiver of an essence of the essences, it is impossible for Him to intend its bringing about and creation. And knowledge of that is necessary, so it is established that He, exalted, is a conceiver of these essences.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter presenting a different proof for God's knowledge, claiming it is stronger than the argument from design. The proof is based on God's nature as an agent by choice (فاعل بالاختيار). The author first reminds that knowledge is divided into conception (تصور) and assent (تصديق). Here, the proof focuses on conceptual knowledge. The argument proceeds: it has been established that God is a powerful free agent (قادر مختار). A free agent acts by intending (قصد) to bring something into existence. Intention presupposes conception of that thing. Therefore, God must have conceptual knowledge of every essence (ماهية) He creates. This is presented as necessary knowledge (ضروري). Thus, God's conceptual knowledge of all essences is proved.

Key Terms

تصديق
قادر مختار
قصد
تكوين وتخليق
ماهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only shows God knows things He created, not all possible essences.
Correction: The proof actually demonstrates that God possesses conceptual knowledge of all essences (ماهيات) because He is the creator by choice; the essences are the objects of His creative power.

Checkpoint
What is the essential link between God's being a free agent and His conceptual knowledge?
A) Free agency requires perfect knowledge of all possible worlds.

C) Free agents must know the future of their actions.
D) God's knowledge is necessary, not dependent on creation.

Key Takeaway
God's conceptual knowledge of all essences is proven by the necessity of intention in free agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you start your next day by asking God to align your intentions with the conception He has for you?
?

When you look in the mirror, do you see a creature God conceived with intentional love, or do you see a product of random chance? How will you change your self-talk today to reflect this truth?

God's Conceptual Knowledge (Tasawwur)
Proof that God has conceptual knowledge from His free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must have conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of all essences based on His being a free agent.

Core Idea
God's free agency requires intention, and intention requires conception.

The author presents a stronger proof for God's knowledge, starting with the simplest kind: conceptual knowledge. He argues that because God is a free agent, He must intend to create, and intention presupposes conception of what is created. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on tasawwur
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from the premise of free agency to the conclusion of conception.
The author calls this proof stronger than the previous one.
2
Identify the key premise
Locate the statement that God is qādir mukhtār (powerful chooser). This is the foundational premise for the entire argument.
The premise is established earlier in the book.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: free agent acts by intention (qaṣd), intention requires conception of what is intended. Conclude that God must have conception of all essences He creates.
The author calls this knowledge 'necessary'.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof is 'stronger and farther from objections'. Write down one reason based on the passage.
Think about the directness of the connection between free agency and conception.

اعلم أن ههنا طريقاً آخر، أقوى من دليل الإحكام والإتقان. وتقريره : أن نقول : قد عرفت أن العلم إما تصور وإما تصديق. فنقول : أما تصور الحقائق والماهيات فحاصل لله تعالى. والدليل عليه : هو أنه قد ثبت أنه قادر مختار، والقادر المختار إنما يفعل بواسطة القصد إلى التكوين والتخليق، والقصد إلى التكوين والتخليق مشروط بتصور تلك الحقائق، فإن لم يكن متصوراً لماهية من الماهيات امتنع منه القصد إلى تكوينها وتخليقها. والعلم بذلك ضروري فيثبت أنه تعالى متصور لهذه الماهيات. ثم جئنا إلى التصديقات فنقول : إنها أيضاً حاصلة له تعالى. والدليل عليه، هو أن هذه الحقائق منها ما هي متلازمة، ومنها ما هي متعاندة، ومنها ما لا متلازمة ولا متعاندة. أما المتلازمات فهي على قسمين: منها ما يكون لازماً بغير واسطة، ومنها ما يكون لازماً بوسائط. واللوازم التي تكون بوسائط، أشياء متلاصقة ويكون لزوم كل واحد منها لملاصقه، لزوماً بغير واسطة، واستلزام كل واحد منها بملاصقه المتصل به، القريب منه إنما يكون لذاته، فإذا حصل تصور تلك الماهية ثم إن تلك الماهية لذاتها توجب استلزام ذلك اللازم المتصل به، فحينئذ يلزم من حصول ذلك المتصور، حصول العلم بكون ذلك التصور مستلزماً لذلك اللازم، ثم يلزم من العمل بحصول ذلك اللازم الثاني العلم بحصول اللازم الثالث. وهلم جرا إلى آخر المراتب. فلما كانت التصورات حاضرة كانت لوازمها معلومة، فيكون لزوم بعضها لبعض معلوماً، ويكون منافاة بعضها لبعض معلوماً. والأشياء التي لا تكون متلازمة ولا متعاندة تكون هذه الأحوال منها أيضاً معلومة. والحاصل : أنه لما ثبت حصول التصورات، وثبت أن التصورات إما علل للملازمات أو للمعاندات أو لانتفاء اللزوم والعناد، وثبت أن تصور ذات العلة يوجب العلم بلوازمها يثبت أن الكل يصير معلوماً. وهذا الطريق أضبط، وعن الإشكالات أبعد.
Know that here there is another way, stronger than the proof of perfection and precision. Its formulation: we say: you have known that knowledge is either conception (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). So we say: as for conception (tasawwur) of realities and essences, it is obtained for God Almighty. The evidence for it is: that it has been established that He is a powerful chooser (qādir mukhtār), and the powerful chooser only acts by means of intention (al-qaṣd) to bring into being and to create, and intention to bring into being and to create is conditioned by conception of those realities. For if He were not conceiving an essence among essences, intention to bring it into being and create it would be impossible from Him. And knowledge of that is necessary, so it is established that He Almighty is conceiving of these essences. Then we come to assents (al-taṣdīqāt) and we say: they are also obtained for Him Almighty. The evidence for it is that these realities: among them are concomitant with each other (mutalāzima), among them are opposed to each other (mutāʿānida), and among them are neither concomitant nor opposed. As for the concomitants, they are of two kinds: among them is what is concomitant without mediation, and among them is what is concomitant with mediations. The concomitants that are with mediations are things that are contiguous, and the concomitance of each one of them to its contiguous is a concomitance without mediation, and the implication of each one of them by its contiguous that is connected to it, the near one, is only by its essence. So when that essence is conceived, then that essence by its very self necessitates the implication of that concomitant connected to it. Then at that point, from the obtaining of that conception follows the obtaining of knowledge that that conception is implicative of that concomitant. Then from the knowledge of obtaining that second concomitant follows knowledge of obtaining the third concomitant, and so on to the end of the ranks. Since the conceptions are present, their concomitants are known, so the concomitance of some to others is known, and the mutual opposition of some to others is known. And the things that are neither concomitant nor opposed—these states from them are also known. The conclusion: when the obtaining of conceptions is established, and it is established that conceptions are either causes for concomitants or for oppositions or for the negation of concomitance and opposition, and it is established that conception of the essence of the cause necessitates knowledge of its concomitants, it is established that all becomes known. And this way is more precise and farther from objections.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the first alternative proof for God's knowledge. The author begins by stating that knowledge is divided into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). He then proves that God has conception of all essences because He is a free agent (qādir mukhtār); free agency requires intention (qaṣd), and intention requires prior conception of what is to be created. This is a necessary truth. Next, he proves that God has assent (judgmental knowledge) regarding the relations between essences. He classifies essences into three types: necessary concomitants (mutalāzima), mutual opposites (mutāʿānida), and those that are neither. The necessary concomitants are further divided into immediate (without mediation) and mediate (with mediations). For mediate concomitants, the chain of knowledge proceeds stepwise: from conception of the essence, one knows its immediate necessary concomitant, then from that one knows the next, and so on. Since the conceptions are present, their concomitants are known. The same applies to oppositions and neutral relations. Thus, once conceptions are established, all judgmental knowledge about them—concomitance, opposition, or their negation—follows. The author concludes that this method is more precise and less susceptible to objections.

Key Terms
تصور
قادر مختار
القصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is only general and does not include particular essences.
Correction: The proof shows that because God freely creates each essence, He must have a conception of each one individually.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why must God have conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of all essences?
A) Because He is omniscient by nature.
B) Because He is a free agent and must intend to create, which requires conception.
C) Because essences are eternal.
D) Because tasawwur is a necessary attribute.

Key Takeaway
God's conceptual knowledge of all essences is necessitated by His free agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that God's intention to create requires conception. Since every detail of your existence is intentionally created, what does it mean for you to know that God has conceived of every moment of your life before it happens? How does that change the way you view your struggles and successes?
?
If God's free agency necessitates that He conceives every essence, then nothing in your life is random or unknown to Him. Are you living as if every thought and action is within His conception? What would change if you fully internalized that God's knowledge of you is perfect and detailed?
?
The author says that knowledge of this is 'necessary' (ḍarūrī). Is your belief in God's complete knowledge of you merely theoretical, or does it press you to live with greater accountability? What specific area of your life would you need to transform to truly honor His detailed knowledge of you?

God's Judgmental Knowledge (Tasdiq): Concomitants and Oppositions
Proof that God has judgmental knowledge of relations between essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's judgmental knowledge (tasdiq) of relations between essences is derived from His conceptual knowledge.

Core Idea
From conception of essences, all their relations become known.

After establishing God's conceptual knowledge, the author turns to judgmental knowledge—the knowledge of relations like concomitance and opposition. He classifies essences into three categories and shows that once conceptions are present, all such relations are necessarily known. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on tasdiq
Read the second part of the Arabic passage from 'ثم جئنا إلى التصديقات' to the end. Follow the translation carefully.
The argument builds on the conclusion that God has conception of all essences.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three types of essences: concomitant (mutalāzima), opposed (mutāʿānida), and neither. Understand that these are relations between essences.
The author focuses first on concomitants.
3
Understand the chain of knowledge for mediate concomitants
Trace the stepwise process: from conception of an essence, knowledge of its immediate necessary concomitant follows, then from that knowledge the next concomitant, and so on.
This chain works because the concomitance is based on the essence itself.
4
Apply the reasoning to all relations
Note that the same logic applies to oppositions and neutral relations. Since conceptions are present, all relations are known.
The conclusion is that all becomes known.
5
Reflect on the strength of this method
Consider why the author says this way is 'more precise and farther from objections'. Write down one advantage based on the passage.
Compare with the earlier proof from perfection and precision.

?

?

Is there a relationship in your life—with Allah, with others, with yourself—that you treat as 'neither concomitant nor opposed,' believing it has no necessary consequences? What would it mean to see that even neutral-seeming states are fully known and accounted for in God's judgmental knowledge?

Knowledge of Existence Leads to Knowledge of All Things
A proof for God's knowledge through analysis of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of existence itself provides a proof for God's comprehensive knowledge, without relying on direct observation of particulars.

Core Idea
If God knows existence, He knows all its divisions and details.

The author presents a second, more abstract approach to proving God's knowledge. Instead of starting from the order of the universe, this argument begins with God's own nature as a free agent and the logical implications of His intention to create. The key insight is that knowing the universal concept of existence automatically entails knowledge of all its necessary subdivisions and particulars. This lesson walks through that logical chain step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly. Then read the English translation. Let the overall argument sink in before breaking it down.
Notice the author's phrase 'فاعل مختار' (free agent) – this is the starting point.
2
Identify the starting premise
The argument begins with God being a free agent who acts by intention. Ask yourself: What must God know in order to intend to bring something into existence?
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: God knows existence → existence divides into necessary and possible → He knows the division → He knows the universal → He knows the particulars through their necessary concomitants. Write down each step.
The chain relies on the principle that knowing a quiddity leads to knowing its necessary concomitants.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author calls this a 'good way' and mentions it can be strengthened by logic. Consider why this argument does not claim God knows every particular directly, but through their connection to universals.
This addresses a common misunderstanding: God's knowledge is not piecemeal but based on essential relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا طريق آخر في تقرير هذا الكلام : وهو أن نقول : لما ثبت أنه فاعل مختار وهو إنما يفعل بواسطة القصد إلى الإيجاد، فلا بد وأن يحضر عنده تصور معنى الموجود، لأنه لو لم يعرف أن الموجود ما هو ؟ امتنع منه القصد إلى الإيجاد، وإذا عرف أن الموجود ما هو ؟ والموجود من حيث أنه موجود يقبل الإنقسام إلى الواجب والممكن، وجب أن تعرف هذا الانقسام، وإذا عرفت هذا الانقسام، فقد عرفت أن الواجب ما هو ؟ وأن الممكن ما هو ؟ وبهذا الطريق عرفت كلياً، ومتى عرفت كلياً، فقد عرفت انقسامه إلى جزئياته، لما ثبت أن تصور كل ماهية من حيث هي هي، موجب لانتقال الذهن منها إلى لوازمها، ثم من تلك اللوازم الى لوازم تلك اللوازم بالغة ما بلغت فهذا طريق حسن. يمكن تقويته بالمباحث المنطقية، المفرعة على أن العلم بالماهية يوجب العلم بلازمه القريب. والله أعلم
And here is another way in formulating this speech: that we say: since it is established that He is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) and He only acts by means of intention to bring into existence (al-ījād), then must be present with Him a conception (taṣawwur) of the meaning of existence (al-mawjūd). Because if He did not know what existence is, intention to bring into existence would be impossible from Him. And if He knows what existence is, and existence insofar as it is existence admits division into the necessary (al-wājib) and the possible (al-mumkin), then it is necessary that He know this division. And if He knows this division, then He knows what the necessary is and what the possible is. And by this way, He knows a universal (kullī), and when He knows a universal, He knows its division into its particulars (juzʾiyyāt), because it is established that conception of every quiddity (māhiyya) in terms of its very self necessitates the mind's transition from it to its concomitants (lawāzim), then from those concomitants to the concomitants of those concomitants, however far they reach. So this is a good way. It can be strengthened by logical investigations, branched on the fact that knowledge of the quiddity necessitates knowledge of its near concomitant. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a second alternative proof for God's knowledge. The argument begins again from God's free agency: God is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) and acts through intention (qaṣd) to bring things into existence. This intention requires a conception of what existence means. Therefore, God must have a concept of existence (maʿnā al-mawjūd). Once He knows existence in general, He recognizes that existence admits a division into necessary (wājib) and possible (mumkin). Hence, He knows each of these categories. Because He knows this universal (kullī) division, He knows its particulars (juzʾiyyāt), since the conception of any quiddity leads the mind to its necessary concomitants, and from them to further concomitants, ad infinitum. The author notes that this argument can be strengthened through logical investigations which establish that knowledge of a quiddity entails knowledge of its near concomitant. He concludes with 'And God knows best.'

الواجب

الكلي
الجزئي
تصور كل ماهية
اللوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof claims God knows every particular directly, as if He observes each one individually.
Correction: The argument does not assert direct observation of each particular; rather, it shows that knowledge of universals and their necessary concomitants entails knowledge of particulars in a structural, essential way.

Checkpoint
How does the author argue that God knows the division of existence into necessary and possible?
A) By revelation.
B) By analyzing the concept of existence.
C) By observing creation.
D) By innate knowledge.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of the single universal 'existence' unfolds to encompass all necessary and possible beings through their essential relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of a single universal 'existence' unfolds to include every detail of your life, how does that change the way you approach your private thoughts and actions today?
?
This argument shows that God's knowledge is necessary and comprehensive. Do you truly believe that He knows every intention behind your deeds? If so, what will you do differently from this moment?
?
The proof relies on the principle that knowledge of a quiddity necessitates knowledge of its concomitants. When you reflect on your own existence, do you see it as a 'quiddity' that necessarily points to its Creator? What does that demand from you?

The Argument from God's Free Agency to His Knowledge
Author argues that God's free agency entails His knowledge of universals and particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's status as a free agent implies that He must have conceptual knowledge, including knowledge of universals and particulars.

Core Idea
A free agent must know what He intends to create, so God necessarily knows existence itself.

This lesson presents a second argument for God's knowledge, rooted in His being a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). The reasoning shows that intentional action requires conceptualization of the object of intention, leading to knowledge of both universals and particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'fa-' and 'idhā'.
2
Trace the logical steps
Follow the reasoning: free agent acts by intention → must have concept of existence → existence divides into necessary and possible → knows both → knows universal and particulars.
The argument moves from God's action to His knowledge of universals.
3
Reflect on the chain of concomitants
Note the claim that conceptualizing an essence leads to knowing its necessary concomitants in a chain. This underpins the move from the universal to particulars.
This principle can be strengthened by logic.

وههنا طريق آخر في تقرير هذا الكلام : وهو أن نقول : لما ثبت أنه فاعل مختار وهو إنما يفعل بواسطة القصد إلى الإيجاد، فلا بد وأن يحضر عنده تصور معنى الموجود ، لأنه لو لم يعرف أن الموجود ما هو ؟ امتنع امتنع منه أن إلى الإيجاد ، وإذا عرف أن الموجود ما هو ؟ والموجود من حيث أنه موجود يقبل الإنقسام إلى الواجب والممكن ، وجب أن تعرف هذا الانقسام ، وإذا عرفت هذا الانقسام ، فقد عرفت أن الواجب ما هو ؟ وأن الممكن ما هو ؟ وبهذا الطريق عرفت كليا ، ومتى عرفت كليا ، فقد عرفت انقسامه إلى جزئياته ، لما ثبت أن تصور كل ماهية من حيث هي هي ، موجب لانتقال الذهن منها إلى لوازمها ، ثم من تلك اللوازم الى لوازم تلك اللوازم بالغة ما بلغت فهذا طريق حسن . يمكن تقويته بالمباحث المنطقية ، المفرعة على أن العلم بالماهية يوجب العلم بلازمه القريب . والله أعلم
And here is another way in presenting this discussion: it is that we say: since it is established that He is a free agent, and He only acts through intention to bring into existence, then it is necessary that a conceptualization of the meaning of existence should be present with Him. For if He did not know what existence is, it would be impossible for Him to intend to bring into existence. And if He knows what existence is, and existence insofar as it is existence admits division into necessary and possible, then this division must be known. And if this division is known, then He has known what the necessary is and what the possible is. And by this way He has known a universal, and whenever He knows a universal, He has known its division into its particulars, because it is established that conceptualization of every essence in itself necessitates the mind's transition from it to its concomitants, then from those concomitants to the concomitants of those concomitants, however far they reach. So this is a good way; it can be strengthened by logical investigations based on the principle that knowledge of the essence necessitates knowledge of its immediate concomitant. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument for God's knowledge, based on His being a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). The reasoning proceeds step by step: God acts by intention (qaṣd) to bring things into existence. Therefore, He must have a concept of existence itself; otherwise He could not intend to create. Once He knows existence, He knows that existence divides into necessary (wājib) and possible (mumkin). Hence He knows both categories. Knowing the universal (existence) entails knowing its particulars, because conceptualizing any essence leads to knowing its necessary concomitants (lawāzim) in a chain. The author notes this argument can be strengthened by logical principles about knowledge of essences entailing knowledge of their immediate concomitants.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار (fāʿil mukhtār): free agent
قصد (qaṣd): intention
تصور (taṣawwur): conceptualization
واجب (wājib): necessary
ممكن (mumkin): possible
لازم (lāzim): concomitant

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God learns about existence and its divisions through a process of reasoning or acquisition.
Correction: The argument does not imply that God acquires knowledge; rather, it shows that His nature as a free agent entails that He has always known existence, universals, and particulars.

Checkpoint
Why must God have a concept of existence according to this argument?
A) Because existence is self-evident
B) Because He acts by intention and must know what He intends to create
C) Because He is omniscient
D) Because the universe exists

Key Takeaway
God's free agency logically implies His comprehensive knowledge of existence, universals, and particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God must have a concept of existence to intend to create, then He knows every intention you harbor. When was the last time you acted as if your hidden plans were unknown to Him? Will you bring that secret before Him now in sincere repentance?
?
God's knowledge of existence and its divisions is perfect and all-encompassing. What area of your life have you compartmentalized as 'too small' or 'too worldly' for His concern? Write down one specific habit you've ignored, and commit to bringing it into alignment with His command tonight.
?
The argument shows that knowing a universal entails knowing its particulars. Yet you often make general promises to obey Allah without specifying how. What is one concrete act of worship you have been delaying? Set a day and time to perform it, and do not let yourself forget.

Ibn Sina's Three Proofs for Divine Knowledge
Author summarizes Ibn Sina's three proofs for divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs Ibn Sina used to establish that God knows all things, including the complementary relationship between knowledge of self and knowledge of others, and the metaphysical argument from immateriality.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's three proofs show that God's knowledge is necessary, simultaneous, and inherent to His immaterial essence.

Ibn Sina, the great philosopher, advanced several arguments for divine knowledge. In this passage, he summarises three key proofs that appear throughout his works. These proofs are interconnected: the first establishes knowledge of self, the second knowledge of others, and the third grounds both in God's immaterial nature. As you read, notice how each proof builds on the previous one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three ways
Read the passage carefully and locate the three distinct ways (وجوه) that Ibn Sina presents. Mark each one's starting point and note the key claim of each.
2
Understand the first way
The first way shows that God knows His essence, and that this knowledge necessarily entails knowledge of everything else. Consider why knowledge of the cause implies knowledge of all effects.
3
Understand the second way
The second way argues from God's knowledge of other things back to knowledge of His essence. Notice how it reverses the direction of the first way.
4
Understand the third way
The third way is based on immaterial substances: when an immaterial form is apprehended, the subject becomes intellect, intelligible, and intelligent. Since God is immaterial by essence, He is most worthy of this triune status.
5
Reflect on interconnection
Consider how the three proofs complement each other. The first two are symmetrical; the third provides a metaphysical foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع تقرير الوجوه التي استدل بها الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي بن سينا في سائر كتبه على كونه تعالى عالما بالمعلوميات اعلم . أنه ذكر في هذا الباب وجوها ثلاثة : فالطريق الأول : بين فيه كونه تعالى عالما بذاته . ثم بين فيه : أن علمه بذاته يوجب كونه عالما بما سواه. والطريق الثاني : بين فيه كونه تعالى عالما بغيره . ثم بين أنه متى كان عالما بما سواه، فإنه يجب أن يكون عالما بذاته . والطريق الثالث : يذكر فيه أن الجوهر المجرد إذا الحد بالصورة المجردة وحصل التعقل . فالذي يكون مجردا لذاته أولى أن يكون عقلا ومعقولا وعاقلا . فهذا مجموع الوجوه التي عول عليها .
The Fourth Goal: Presentation of the proofs by the chief master Abū ʿAlī ibn Sīnā in his various books for His being, exalted, knowing of known things. Know that he mentioned in this chapter three ways: The first way: he established in it that He, exalted, is knowing of His essence. Then he established in it that His knowledge of His essence necessitates His being knowing of what is other than Him. The second way: he established in it that He, exalted, is knowing of other than Him. Then he established that when He is knowing of what is other than Him, then it is necessary that He be knowing of His essence. The third way: he mentions in it that the immaterial substance when it is united with the immaterial form and intellection occurs, then that which is immaterial by its essence is more worthy to be intellect, intelligible, and intelligent. So this is the sum of the proofs he relied upon.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the fourth goal (al-maqṣid al-rābiʿ) of the work. The author sets out to present the proofs used by Avicenna (Ibn Sīnā) in various books to establish that God is knowing of all things known. He summarizes three ways: The first way shows that God is knowing of His own essence, and this knowledge necessitates knowledge of everything else. The second way shows that God is knowing of other things, and from that follows knowledge of His own essence. The third way is based on the nature of immaterial substance: when an immaterial substance attains intellection by being united with an immaterial form, it becomes an intellect, an intelligible, and an intelligent. Since God is immaterial by essence, He is most worthy of being all three simultaneously. The author notes that these are the proofs Avicenna relied upon.

Key Terms

عالم بذاته (ʿālim bi-dhātihi): knowing of His essence
جوهر مجرد (jawhar mujarrad): immaterial substance

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three proofs are separate and independent arguments with no connection.
Correction: They are interconnected: the first two are complementary (knowledge of self implies knowledge of others and vice versa), and the third provides the metaphysical grounding in immateriality.

Ibn Sina's three proofs for divine knowledge rely on the relationship between essence and others, and on immateriality.

The First Way: Self-Knowledge Leads to All Knowledge
Ibn Sina's first way: knowledge of self necessitates knowledge of all necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's first proof that God's self-knowledge necessitates knowledge of all necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
God's self-knowledge unlocks all other knowledge.

This passage presents Ibn Sina's first proof for God's omniscience. The argument is elegant: if God knows His own essence, He must know everything that necessarily follows from it. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its first foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice the concise style of Ibn Sina's argument.
2
Understand the chain of knowledge
The argument builds a chain: from self-knowledge to knowledge of the immediate concomitant, then to the second, and so on until all concomitants are known.
This chain is key to the proof.
3
Identify the two foundations
The author says the statement rests on two foundations. The first is given: God is knowing of His essence. The second is not in this excerpt.
Ask why these foundations are needed.
4
Focus on the first foundation
The first foundation is that God is knowing of His essence. The evidence given is simply that He is existent.
Consider why existence implies self-knowledge for God.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الطريق الأول : فتقريره : أن نقول إنه عالم بذاته ، وكل من كان عالما بذاته ، وجب أن يكون عالما بلازمه القريب ، ويلزم من علمه باللازم الأول علمه باللازم الثاني . وعلى هذا الترتيب حتى تصير جميع اللوازم معلومة . وهذا الكلام مبني على أصلين : الأصل الأول : إنه تعالى عالم بذاته. والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى موجود

And this statement is based on two foundations: The first foundation: He, exalted, is knowing of His essence. And the evidence for it is that He, exalted, is existent—
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage elaborates the first of Ibn Sina's three proofs. The author states: 'He (God) is knowing of His essence.' The argument is that whoever knows his own essence necessarily knows its immediate logical concomitant. From that first concomitant, he gains knowledge of the second, and so forth in a chain until all concomitants are known. This reasoning rests on two foundations (aṣlān). The present text only gives the first foundation: that God is knowing of His essence, and the evidence for that is simply that He is existent. The text cuts off here, but the idea is that existence itself implies self-knowledge for a necessary being. The second foundation is not provided in this excerpt.

Ibn Sina's Three Arguments for God's Knowledge
The author summarizes Ibn Sina's three arguments for God's knowledge of all things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways Ibn Sina used to argue that God knows all things, and how they are interconnected.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's three ways show that God's self-knowledge and knowledge of others are inseparable.

Ibn Sina presented three arguments for God's knowledge of all things, each starting from a different premise but leading to the same conclusion. In this passage, the author summarizes these three ways, showing how they build on each other. Let's examine each one.

Guided Path
1
Read the First Way: Self-Knowledge
Read the description of the first way: God's knowledge of His essence necessitates knowledge of other things.
Notice how this argues from self-knowledge outward.
2
Read the Second Way: Knowledge of Others
Read the second way: starting from knowledge of others, it concludes that God must know His own essence.
This is the reverse of the first way.
3
Read the Third Way: Abstract Substance
Read the third way: an abstract substance united with an abstract form becomes intellect, intelligible, and intellectual. God, being purely abstract, is most deserving of this.
This uses metaphysical principles about abstraction.

The third way: he mentions in it that the abstract substance when it unites with the abstract form and intellection occurs, then that which is abstract by itself is more deserving to be intellect, intelligible, and intellectual. So this is the totality of the ways upon which he relied.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents a summary of Avicenna's (Ibn Sina's) three arguments for God's knowledge of all things. The first way proceeds from God's self-knowledge to knowledge of everything else by necessity of consequences. The second way starts from knowledge of others and concludes to self-knowledge. The third way uses the nature of abstract substance: when an abstract substance unites with an abstract form, intellection occurs; God, being purely abstract, is most deserving of being intellect, intelligible, and intellection. The author presents these as the three ways that Ibn Sina relied upon.

The First Principle: God's Self-Knowledge
Author states the first way and the first principle with its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first principle of God's self-knowledge and its proof based on God's self-subsistence and abstraction from matter.

Core Idea
Knowledge of self is necessary for a self-subsistent, immaterial being.

In this lesson, we examine the first building block of Ibn Sina's argument for God's knowledge: the claim that God knows His own essence. This is established through a proof that any self-subsistent, abstract being must know itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing the first way
Read the Arabic passage once to see the overall structure of the argument and the two principles.
The passage is dense; focus on the flow.
2
Identify the first principle and its proof
The first principle is 'He is knowing of His essence'. Its proof uses two premises: God is existent, self-subsistent, abstract; and anything like that knows itself.
The second premise is to be proven later.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how the argument moves from God's attributes to self-knowledge. The proof relies on the definition of knowledge as presence, which will be detailed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الطريق الأول : فتقريره : أن نقول إنه عالم بذاته ، وكل من كان عالماً بذاته ، وجب أن يكون عالماً بلازمه القريب ، ويلزم من (۱) علمه باللازم الأول ( علمه باللازم) (۲) الثاني . وعلى هذا الترتيب حتى تصير جميع اللوازم معلومة . وهذا الكلام مبني على أصلين : الأصل الأول : إنه تعالى عالم بذاته. والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى موجود قائم بنفسه مجرد عن الجسمية ، وكل من كان كذلك فإنه يجب أن يكون عالماً بذاته . أما المقدمة الأولى فقد سبق إثباتها . وأما المقدمة الثانية . وهي أن كل من كان قائماً بنفسه غنياً عن المادة فإنه لا بد وأن يعلم نفسه . فالدليل على صحتها . وجهان : الوجه الأول : إنه قد ثبت أنه لا معنى للعلم إلا حضور ماهية المعلوم عند العالم ، فإذا حضرت ماهية مجردة عند ماهية مجردة صارت الماهية القائمة بنفسها عالمة بذلك الشيء . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الشيء إذا كان قائما بنفسه لم تكن حقيقة وجوده حاصلة لغيره ، بل كانت حقيقته حاصلة لنفسه . فإذا كان حصول ماهية مجردة الماهية أخرى مجردة يقتضي كون ذلك الموجود القائم بنفسه عالماً ، فههنا لما حصلت حقيقة ذلك الشيء بنفسها ، وجب كون ذلك الشيء عالماً بنفسه . وذلك هو المطلوب . الوجه الثاني : إنه لا شك في أن الواحد منا يعلم نفسه ، وقد بينا (٢) أن العلم عبارة عن حضور ماهية المعلوم في العالم. فإذا علمنا أنفسنا ، فإما أن يكون ذلك لأجل أن صورة متساوية أنفسنا ، حضرت عند نفسنا . أو لأجل أن نفس نفسنا حضرت عند نفسنا ، والأول باطل ، لأنه يلزم اجتماع المثلين ، ولأنه ليست إحداهما بالمختلفة ، والثانية بالحالة أولى من العكس . ولما بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت أن علمنا بنفسنا ليس إلا حضور نفسنا عند نفسنا ، وإذا كان كذلك وجب في كل شيء حضرت نفسه عند نفسه أن يكون عالماً وواجب الوجود كذلك ، فيجب أن يكون عالماً بنفسه .
As for the first way: its formulation is that we say: He is knowing of His essence, and everyone who is knowing of His essence must be knowing of its immediate necessary consequence, and from His knowledge of the first necessary consequence follows His knowledge of the second, and so on in this order until all necessary consequences become known. And this statement is based on two principles: The first principle: He, exalted, is knowing of His essence. The proof of it is that He, exalted, is existent, self-subsistent, abstract from corporeality, and everyone who is like that must be knowing of His essence. As for the first premise, its proof has preceded. As for the second premise, which is that everyone who is self-subsistent and independent of matter must necessarily know himself. The proof of its correctness has two aspects: The first aspect: It has been established that there is no meaning of knowledge except the presence of the quiddity of the known thing to the knower. So when an abstract quiddity is present to an abstract quiddity, the self-subsistent quiddity becomes knowing of that thing. If this is established, we say: if a thing is self-subsistent, the reality of its existence is not obtained from another, but its reality is obtained for itself. So if the obtaining of an abstract quiddity to another abstract quiddity necessitates that the self-subsistent existent be knowing, then here, when the reality of that thing is obtained by itself, it is necessary that that thing be knowing of itself. And that is what is sought. The second aspect: There is no doubt that one of us knows himself, and we have explained that knowledge is an expression of the presence of the quiddity of the known in the knower. So when we know ourselves, it is either because a form equal to ourselves is present to ourselves, or because our own soul itself is present to ourselves. The first is false, because it would require the coexistence of two similars, and because neither one is more entitled to be the state than the other. Since this part is invalid, it is established that our knowledge of ourselves is nothing but the presence of ourselves to ourselves. And if it is so, then it is necessary for everything whose self is present to itself to be knowing, and the Necessary Existent is like that, so He must be knowing of Himself.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the first way of Ibn Sina. The author first restates the core argument: God knows His essence, and knowing one's essence entails knowledge of its necessary consequences, leading to knowledge of all consequences. This argument rests on two principles: (1) God knows His own essence, and (2) knowledge of the essence leads to knowledge of consequences. The proof of the first principle is given: God is existent, self-subsistent, and abstract from matter; anything with these attributes must know itself. The second principle is supported by two proofs. The first proof defines knowledge as the presence of the quiddity of the known to the knower. If an abstract quiddity is present to another abstract quiddity, the latter becomes knowing. In God's case, His own reality is present to itself, so He is knowing of Himself. The second proof uses human self-knowledge as an analogy. When we know ourselves, it is not through a separate form of ourselves (which would lead to infinite regress or duplication), but through the direct presence of our soul to itself. Similarly, God, being self-subsistent, has His essence present to itself, and thus He knows Himself. The argument concludes that the Necessary Existent must be knowing of Himself.

Two Proofs for Self-Knowledge of the Self-Subsistent
Author presents two proofs for the second premise that self-subsistent beings know themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two detailed proofs that a self-subsistent, immaterial being must know itself.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge is the presence of the essence to itself.

This lesson delves into the second principle: that any self-subsistent being independent of matter necessarily knows itself. The author provides two proofs: one based on the definition of knowledge as presence, and another using the analogy of human self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the two proofs carefully
Read the Arabic passage for both proofs. Note the logical steps in each.
The first proof is more abstract, the second uses analogy.
2
Understand the first proof (presence of quiddity)
Knowledge is defined as presence of the known's quiddity to the knower. If an abstract quiddity is present to itself, it becomes knowing.
This applies directly to God's self-subsistent essence.
3
Understand the second proof (human self-knowledge)
We know ourselves. This cannot be through a separate form (would require two similars), so it must be direct presence of the soul to itself. The same applies to God.
This proof uses elimination of alternatives.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Both proofs lead to the conclusion that the Necessary Existent, being self-subsistent and abstract, must know Himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الطريق الأول : فتقريره : أن نقول إنه عالم بذاته ، وكل من كان عالماً بذاته ، وجب أن يكون عالماً بلازمه القريب ، ويلزم من (۱) علمه باللازم الأول ( علمه باللازم) (۲) الثاني . وعلى هذا الترتيب حتى تصير جميع اللوازم معلومة . وهذا الكلام مبني على أصلين : الأصل الأول : إنه تعالى عالم بذاته. والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى موجود قائم بنفسه مجرد عن الجسمية ، وكل من كان كذلك فإنه يجب أن يكون عالماً بذاته . أما المقدمة الأولى فقد سبق إثباتها . وأما المقدمة الثانية . وهي أن كل من كان قائماً بنفسه غنياً عن المادة فإنه لا بد وأن يعلم نفسه . فالدليل على صحتها . وجهان : الوجه الأول : إنه قد ثبت أنه لا معنى للعلم إلا حضور ماهية المعلوم عند العالم ، فإذا حضرت ماهية مجردة عند ماهية مجردة صارت الماهية القائمة بنفسها عالمة بذلك الشيء . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الشيء إذا كان قائما بنفسه لم تكن حقيقة وجوده حاصلة لغيره ، بل كانت حقيقته حاصلة لنفسه . فإذا كان حصول ماهية مجردة الماهية أخرى مجردة يقتضي كون ذلك الموجود القائم بنفسه عالماً ، فههنا لما حصلت حقيقة ذلك الشيء بنفسها ، وجب كون ذلك الشيء عالماً بنفسه . وذلك هو المطلوب . الوجه الثاني : إنه لا شك في أن الواحد منا يعلم نفسه ، وقد بينا (٢) أن العلم عبارة عن حضور ماهية المعلوم في العالم. فإذا علمنا أنفسنا ، فإما أن يكون ذلك لأجل أن صورة متساوية أنفسنا ، حضرت عند نفسنا . أو لأجل أن نفس نفسنا حضرت عند نفسنا ، والأول باطل ، لأنه يلزم اجتماع المثلين ، ولأنه ليست إحداهما بالمختلفة ، والثانية بالحالة أولى من العكس . ولما بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت أن علمنا بنفسنا ليس إلا حضور نفسنا عند نفسنا ، وإذا كان كذلك وجب في كل شيء حضرت نفسه عند نفسه أن يكون عالماً وواجب الوجود كذلك ، فيجب أن يكون عالماً بنفسه .
As for the first way: its formulation is that we say: He is knowing of His essence, and everyone who is knowing of His essence must be knowing of its immediate necessary consequence, and from His knowledge of the first necessary consequence follows His knowledge of the second, and so on in this order until all necessary consequences become known. And this statement is based on two principles: The first principle: He, exalted, is knowing of His essence. The proof of it is that He, exalted, is existent, self-subsistent, abstract from corporeality, and everyone who is like that must be knowing of His essence. As for the first premise, its proof has preceded. As for the second premise, which is that everyone who is self-subsistent and independent of matter must necessarily know himself. The proof of its correctness has two aspects: The first aspect: It has been established that there is no meaning of knowledge except the presence of the quiddity of the known thing to the knower. So when an abstract quiddity is present to an abstract quiddity, the self-subsistent quiddity becomes knowing of that thing. If this is established, we say: if a thing is self-subsistent, the reality of its existence is not obtained from another, but its reality is obtained for itself. So if the obtaining of an abstract quiddity to another abstract quiddity necessitates that the self-subsistent existent be knowing, then here, when the reality of that thing is obtained by itself, it is necessary that that thing be knowing of itself. And that is what is sought. The second aspect: There is no doubt that one of us knows himself, and we have explained that knowledge is an expression of the presence of the quiddity of the known in the knower. So when we know ourselves, it is either because a form equal to ourselves is present to ourselves, or because our own soul itself is present to ourselves. The first is false, because it would require the coexistence of two similars, and because neither one is more entitled to be the state than the other. Since this part is invalid, it is established that our knowledge of ourselves is nothing but the presence of ourselves to ourselves. And if it is so, then it is necessary for everything whose self is present to itself to be knowing, and the Necessary Existent is like that, so He must be knowing of Himself.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the first way of Ibn Sina. The author first restates the core argument: God knows His essence, and knowing one's essence entails knowledge of its necessary consequences, leading to knowledge of all consequences. This argument rests on two principles: (1) God knows His own essence, and (2) knowledge of the essence leads to knowledge of consequences. The proof of the first principle is given: God is existent, self-subsistent, and abstract from matter; anything with these attributes must know itself. The second principle is supported by two proofs. The first proof defines knowledge as the presence of the quiddity of the known to the knower. If an abstract quiddity is present to another abstract quiddity, the latter becomes knowing. In God's case, His own reality is present to itself, so He is knowing of Himself. The second proof uses human self-knowledge as an analogy. When we know ourselves, it is not through a separate form of ourselves (which would lead to infinite regress or duplication), but through the direct presence of our soul to itself. Similarly, God, being self-subsistent, has His essence present to itself, and thus He knows Himself. The argument concludes that the Necessary Existent must be knowing of Himself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge requires a separate object (e.g., a mental image) distinct from the self.
Correction: The proofs show that self-knowledge is the direct presence of the essence to itself, not through a separate form (which would lead to infinite regress or duality).

Checkpoint

Objection to the definition of knowledge as presence, with the start of the response.
