Objection from Different Realities
Objection from different realities against analogical self-knowledge argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector argues that a ruling established for one reality does not automatically apply to a different reality, specifically regarding self-knowledge.

Core Idea
A ruling for one reality does not transfer to a different reality.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against analogical reasoning from human self-knowledge to divine self-knowledge. The objector concedes certain premises for argument but then challenges the transfer of a ruling across different realities. Pay close attention to how the argument distinguishes between the nature of things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Note that the objector makes two separate sub-arguments.
The passage begins with 'الوجه الرابع من الوجوه' and ends with 'والله أعلم'.
2
Identify the two sub-arguments
The first sub-argument says that a ruling in one reality does not imply the same ruling in a different reality. The second sub-argument uses the difference in essence between humans and God to block the analogy.
The objector concedes for argument that self-presence implies knowledge, but only for that specific reality.
3
Reflect on the overall point
Ask yourself: Why does the objector not simply deny self-presence? Instead, he accepts it and then uses the 'different realities' principle to reject the conclusion. This shows the nuance of Islamic theological debate.
This passage does not deny that God knows Himself; it only refutes a particular proof for it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع من الوجوه: هب أنه يصح في العقل كون الشيء عند نفسه، وثبت أيضاً أن حضور الشيء عند نفسه يقتضي حصول معنى العالمية، إلا أنا نقول: الشيء الذي حضر عند شيء آخر حقيقة مخصوصة. وهذا الذي يقولون إنه حضر عند نفسه حقيقة أخرى، ولا يلزم من ثبوت حكم في حقيقة ثبوت مثل ذلك الحكم في حقيقة مخالفة للحقيقة الأولى. فهذا هو الكلام على الوجه الأول. وأما الوجه الثاني، فهو أيضاً بناء على أن إدراك الشيء عبارة عن حضور صورة المعلوم عند العالم. وقد أبطلناه. ثم نقول: لو سلمنا ذلك، إلا أنا نقول: هب أن حضور نفسنا عند نفسنا كوننا عالمين بنفسنا. فلم قلتم: إن حضور ذات الله تعالى عند ذاته يوجب كونه عالماً بنفسه؟ وذلك محال. ذلك لأن نفوسنا مخالفة بالماهية لذاته، ولا يلزم من حصول حكم في شيء، حصول مثل ذلك الحكم في شيء يخالفه. والله أعلم.
The fourth of the faces: Suppose it is valid in reason that a thing is present to itself, and it is also established that the presence of a thing to itself necessitates obtaining the meaning of being knowing, except that we say: The thing that is present to another thing is a particular reality. And that which they say is present to itself is another reality, and it does not follow from the establishment of a ruling in a reality the establishment of the like of that ruling in a reality opposite to the first reality. So this is the discussion concerning the first face. As for the second face, it is also based on the idea that perception of a thing is the presence of the form of the known thing to the knower. And we have already refuted it. Then we say: Even if we grant that, except that we say: Suppose that our self being present to our self makes us knowers of ourselves. Then why do you say that the presence of the essence of God, may He be exalted, to His essence necessitates that He is knowing of Himself? And that is impossible. That is because our souls are different in essence from His essence, and it does not follow from the obtaining of a ruling in a thing the obtaining of the like ruling in a thing that differs from it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth face (الوجه الرابع), which is a counter-argument to the earlier claims. It concedes for argument's sake that self-presence is possible and that it implies being knowing (العالمية), but then objects that this conclusion only applies to the particular reality in which it is found. The advocate for the opposition argues: what is present to itself has a certain nature; what is present to another has a different nature. A ruling established for one nature does not automatically transfer to a different nature. Then the passage addresses the second face (the linguistic argument). It notes that the second face rests on the definition of perception as the presence of the form of the known to the knower—a definition that has already been refuted earlier. Even if that definition is accepted, the objector continues: granted that our self-presence makes us knowers of ourselves, why should we conclude that God's self-presence makes Him know Himself? This is not necessary because our souls and God's essence are different in reality (مخالفة بالماهية). Therefore, the ruling for us does not automatically apply to God. The passage ends with 'والله أعلم' (And God knows best).

Key Terms
حضور الشيء عند نفسه
العالمية
مخالفة بالماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage denies that God knows Himself.
Correction: The passage only refutes a specific proof for God's self-knowledge, not the attribute itself. It uses the principle that different realities may have different rulings.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Are you willing to submit to what He reveals, even when it does not fit your human categories?

The Causal Argument for God's Omniscience and Its Condition
Argument that self-knowledge entails knowledge of all things, conditional on necessitarianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof from God's self-knowledge to knowledge of all things depends on viewing God as a necessitating agent rather than a free agent.

Core Idea
Divine omniscience from self-knowledge only follows if God is a necessitating cause.

This passage presents a key argument for God's exhaustive knowledge: if God knows His own essence and that essence is the cause of everything else, then He must know everything. But the author adds a crucial condition: this reasoning only holds if one assumes God is a necessitating agent. Let's examine the argument and its limitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional nature of the argument from the start.
2
Identify the causal chain
The argument moves from knowledge of essence to knowledge of being a cause, then to knowledge of effects, cascading through all effects. Trace this chain in the text.
3
Note the condition on divine agency
The author explicitly states that this proof only works for those who hold God is a necessitating agent (موجب بالذات), not for free agent (فاعل مختار). Ask yourself why this distinction matters.
This is the key insight of the passage.
4
Reflect on the incomplete analysis
The passage ends as the author begins to analyse the relationship between a cause and its nature. Consider what this analysis might aim to show.
The text is cut off, but the main argument is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني: سلمنا أنه تعالى عالم بذاته المخصوصة: فلم قلتم: إنه يلزم أن يكون عالماً بسائر الأشياء؟ وقد احتجوا عليه بأنه تعالى علة لما سواه لذاته. فإذا علم ذات نفسه، وجب أن يعلم كونها علة لما عداه. وإذا علم ذلك فقد علم ما عداه. لأن من المحال أن يعلم كونه علة لما عداه ولا يعلم ما عداه. فيثبت أنه يلزم من علمه بذاته المخصوصة علمه بمعلوله القريب، ثم يلزم من علمه بمعلوله الأول علمه بمعلوله الثاني. لأن المعلول الثاني معلول للمعلول الأول. وهكذا يلزم من علمه بالمعلول الثاني علمه بالمعلول الأول على الترتيب النازل من عنده طولاً وعرضاً. واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال إنما يتم على مذهب من يقول: إنه تعالى موجب بالذات لوجود هذه الممكنات. أما من يقول: إنه تعالى فاعل مختار، فإن هذه الحجة لا تتمشى على قوله. ثم نقول: إنا إذا قلنا إن الألف علة للباء، فإما أن يكون نفس كونه ألفاً هو نفس كونه علة للباء، وإما أن يكون .
The second principle: We grant that He, exalted, is knowing of His particular essence: then why do you say that it necessarily follows that He is knowing of all other things? And they have argued for it that He, exalted, is the cause of what is other than Him by His essence. So if He knows His own essence, He must know that it is the cause of what is other than Him. And if He knows that, then He knows what is other than Him. Because it is impossible that He knows that He is the cause of what is other than Him but does not know what is other than Him. So it is established that from His knowledge of His particular essence, His knowledge of His proximate effect follows, then from His knowledge of His first effect, His knowledge of His second effect follows. Because the second effect is an effect of the first effect. And so on, from His knowledge of the second effect, His knowledge of the first effect follows in descending order from Him, lengthwise and breadthwise. And know that this inference is only valid according to the doctrine of whoever says: He, exalted, is a necessitating agent by essence (موجب بالذات) for the existence of these contingent things. As for whoever says: He, exalted, is a free agent (فاعل مختار), then this argument does not hold on his view. Then we say: If we say that 'alif' is the cause of 'ba', then either its being 'alif' is itself its being a cause of 'ba', or it is ...
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage introduces the second principle (الأصل الثاني): even if we grant that God knows His own particular essence, does it necessarily follow that He knows all other things? The proponents of this view argue that since God is the cause of everything else by His essence, knowledge of His essence necessitates knowledge that it is the cause of others, and that in turn necessitates knowledge of those others (because it is impossible to know something is a cause without knowing the effect). Then, from knowledge of the proximate effect, knowledge of the next effect follows, and so on through the entire causal chain descending from God. The author then notes a crucial condition: this reasoning only works for those who hold that God is a necessitating agent (موجب بالذات), meaning He necessarily produces the universe by His essence. Those who hold that God is a free agent (فاعل مختار) cannot use this argument, because a free agent's knowledge of His essence does not entail that He must create or know creation in the same deterministic way. The passage ends mid-sentence as the author begins to analyze the relationship between the cause and its nature (e.g., whether being 'alif' is identical to being the cause of 'ba'). The text is incomplete, but the main point is clear: the proof from causality to exhaustive divine knowledge depends on a specific theological assumption about the nature of divine agency.

Key Terms
علة
موجب بالذات
فاعل مختار
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof from self-knowledge to omniscience is absolute and independent of how we view divine action.
Correction: The author explicitly states that this proof only holds if God is a necessitating agent; it fails under a free agent view.

Checkpoint
Under which view of divine action does the proof from self-knowledge to knowledge of others hold?
A) God as free agent (فاعل مختار)
B) God as necessitating agent (موجب بالذات)
C) Both views equally
D) Neither view

Key Takeaway
The causal proof for God's omniscience depends on the premise that God is a necessitating agent; it does not hold under a free agent view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The argument shows that if God is a necessitating cause, then His self-knowledge compels creation. Do you want a God who had to create you, or a God who wanted to? What does your answer reveal about your relationship with Him?

Refuting God's Self-Knowledge by Presence
Refutation of two aspects of the claim that God knows Himself by presence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between human self-knowledge by presence and God's self-knowledge fails due to the difference in quiddity (māhiyya) and the logical principle that judgments do not transfer across different realities.

Core Idea
Human souls and God differ in essence, so the rule of presence does not transfer.

This passage tackles a sophisticated argument: that God knows Himself by a direct presence (ḥuḍūr), similar to how human souls are aware of themselves. The author dismantles this analogy by first clarifying what presence means and then showing that even if we accept the human case, it does not apply to God because the two essences are fundamentally different. This lesson walks you through the two aspects of the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structured argument with 'first aspect' and 'second aspect'.
2
Understand the two aspects
Identify the two distinct arguments presented. The first argues that a judgment in one reality does not transfer to a different reality. The second challenges the theory of perception as presence and then argues by analogy from human souls to God.
3
Grasp the key logical principle
Focus on the statement: 'It does not follow from the establishment of a judgment in a reality the establishment of a similar judgment in a reality that differs from the first.' This is the core of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that because our souls differ in quiddity from God's essence, the same rule of self-knowledge by presence does not apply. This safeguards divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى العالمية، إلا أنا نقول الشيء الذي حضرت عند شيء آخر حقيقة مخصوصة. وهذا الذي يقولون إنه حضر عنه نفسه حقيقة أخرى، ولا يلزم من ثبوت حكم في حقيقة ثبوت مثل ذلك الحكم في حقيقة مخالفة للحقيقة الأولى فهذا هو الكلام على الوجه الأول. وأما الوجه الثاني، فهو أيضاً بناء على أن إدراك الشيء عبارة عن حضور صورة المعلوم عند العالم. وقد أبطلناه. ثم نقول: لو سلمنا ذلك إلا أنا نقول: هب أن حضور نفسنا عند نفسنا كوننا عالمين بنفسنا. فلم قلتم: إن حضور ذات الله تعالى عند ذاته يوجب كونه عالماً بنفسه؟ وذلك محال ذلك لأن نفوسنا مخالفة بالماهية لذاته، ولا يلزم من حصول حكم في شيء، حصول مثل ذلك الحكم فيها يخالفه. والله أعلم.
The meaning of 'universality', except that we say: the thing that is present at another thing is a specific reality. And this is what they say: it is present from itself as another reality. And it does not follow from the establishment of a judgment in a reality the establishment of a similar judgment in a reality that differs from the first reality. So this is the speech regarding the first aspect. As for the second aspect, it is also based on the idea that perception of a thing is the presence of the form of the known thing at the knower. And we have refuted that. Then we say: even if we grant that, yet we say: suppose that the presence of our soul at itself constitutes our being knowers of ourselves. Then why do you say that the presence of God's essence at His essence necessitates His being knower of Himself? And that is impossible. That is because our souls differ in quiddity (al-māhiyya) from His essence, and it does not follow from the obtaining of a judgment in a thing the obtaining of a similar judgment in that which differs from it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two aspects of an argument claiming that God knows Himself by presence (ḥuḍūr). The author first defines the first aspect: the claim that a thing present at another thing is a specific reality, and when that self-same reality is present to itself, it constitutes another reality—and that a rule from one reality does not transfer to a different reality. The second aspect is based on the theory that perception is the presence of the form of the known in the knower. The author says this theory has already been refuted. Then, even if granted, the author argues: suppose our soul's presence to itself makes us knowers of ourselves—why would God's presence to Himself necessitate His being knower of Himself? That is impossible because our souls differ in quiddity (māhiyya) from God's essence. A judgment that holds for one thing does not necessarily hold for something that differs from it. Thus the analogy fails.

Key Terms
حضور
صورة المعلوم
الماهية
عالم بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's self-knowledge is exactly analogous to a human soul's self-awareness by presence.
Correction: The essences are different (human souls are contingent, God is necessary), so the rule does not transfer.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that God knows Himself by presence like human souls?
A) Because presence is impossible for God.
B) Because human souls and God differ in quiddity (māhiyya), so the same judgment does not apply.
C) Because God's essence is not present to itself.
D) Because perception is not by presence.

Key Takeaway
A judgment established for a contingent being (like the human soul) does not automatically apply to the necessary being (God) because of the difference in quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author refutes the analogy between human self-knowledge and God's self-knowledge. Are there areas in your life where you have made God in your own image, expecting Him to think or act like a human? What specific step can you take this week to actively affirm God's absolute otherness and transcendence?

Condition for the Opponent's Argument: Necessitating Agent vs. Free Agent
The author presents the opponent's argument and identifies its conditional validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent's argument that God's knowledge of His essence implies knowledge of all things only holds if God is a necessitating agent (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt), not a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār).

Core Idea
The opponent's causal argument only works if God is a necessitating agent, not if He is a free agent.

This lesson presents the second principle of the debate: assuming God knows His essence, the opponent claims this necessitates knowledge of all things because God is the cause of everything. The author immediately notes a crucial condition: this inference depends on whether God is a necessitating agent or a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Read the initial statement of the second principle and the opponent's reasoning that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect.
Notice the chain of effects mentioned.
2
Identify the condition
The author states that the argument only holds on the view that God is a necessitating agent (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt). Understand why it fails for a free agent.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why the type of agency matters. A free agent can choose not to act, so knowing His essence does not automatically mean He knows the effects by necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني: سلمنا أنه تعالى عالم بذاته المخصوصة: فلم قلتم: إنه يلزم أن يكون عالماً بسائر الأشياء؟ وقد احتجوا عليه بأنه تعالى علة لما سواه. لذاته. فإذا علم ذات نفسه، وجب أن يعلم كونها علة لما عداه. وإذا علم ذلك فقد علم ما عداه. لأن من المحال أن يعلم كونه علة لما عداه أنه لا يعلم ما عداه. فيثبت أنه يلزم من علمه بذاته المخصوصة علمه بمعلوله القريب، ثم يلزم من علمه بمعلوله الأول علمه بمعلوله الثاني. لأن المعلول الثاني معلول أول، للمعلول الأول. وهكذا يلزم من علمه بالمعلول الثاني علمه بالمعلول الأول على الترتيب النازل من عنده طولاً وعرضاً. واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال إنما يتم على مذهب من يقول: إنه تعالى موجب بالذات لوجود هذه الممكنات. أما من يقول: إنه تعالى فاعل مختار، فإن هذه الحجة لا تتمشى على قوله. ثم نقول: إنا إذا قلنا إن الألف علة للباء - فإما أن يكون نفس كونه ألفاً، هو نفس كونه علة للباء. وإما أن يكون كون الألفاً مغايراً لكونه علة للباء، إلا أنه من لوازمه. والأول باطل. لأنا بالضرورة تعلم التفرقة بين أن يقال: الألف ألف. وبين أن يقال الألف علة للباء. ولولا التغاير لما حصلت هذه التفرقة. وأيضاً: فالألف إشارة إلى تلك الذات المخصوصة وكونه علة لكذا حالة نسبية إضافية والحقيقة المخصوصة التي ليست هي في نفسها نسبة وإضافة، لا بد وأن تكون مغايرة للنسبة وللإضافة: فإنه يصح العلم بتلك الحقيقة المخصوصة مع الذهول عن كونها علة للباء، ويصح أيضاً تعقل المفهوم من علة الباء، مع الذهول عن تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة. وذلك يوجب التغاير. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: ثبت أن كون الألف ألفاً مغاير لكونه علة للباء. وإذا كانت هذه المغايرة حقة لم يكن علمه بنفسه المخصوصة وذاته المعينة عين العلم بكونه علة للباء. اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن علمه بذاته يوجب العلم بلوازمه، إلا أن هذا نفس المطلوب. فإذا جعلناه مقدمة في إثبات هذا المطلوب، لزم إثبات الشيء بنفسه، وأنه باطل. وتمام الكشف أنا قلنا: الدليل على أنه يلزم من علمه لذاته المخصوصة علمه بمعلوله الأول: هو أنه لذاته علة لذلك المعلول القريب. فإذا علم ذاته فقد علم كونه علة لذلك المعلول القريب. ونقول: موضع المغالطة هذا هو الكلام. فإنكم إن أردتم أن ذاته نفس كونه علة للمعلول القريب. فهذا باطل. وإن سلمتم التغاير. فلم قلتم: إنه يلزم من علمه بتلك الذات المخصوصة علمه بكونه علة للمعلول القريب؟ وهل النزاع وقع إلا في قولكم: إن العلم بالشيء يوجب العلم بلوازمه؟ فهذا تمام البحث عن هذه المقدمة. ثم نقول: الذي يدل على أن هذه المقدمة ليست حقة وجوه: الأول: إنا نعلم ماهيات كثيرة، وحقائق كثيرة. وكل حقيقة يشير
The second principle: We grant that He, exalted, is knower of His specific essence: then why do you say that it necessarily follows that He is knower of all things? They have argued for it by [saying] that He, exalted, is the cause of what is other than Him. By His essence. So if He knows His own essence, He must know that it is the cause of what is other than Him. And if He knows that, then He has known what is other than Him. Because it is impossible that He knows that He is the cause of what is other than Him while not knowing what is other than Him. So it is established that from His knowledge of His specific essence necessarily follows His knowledge of His proximate effect, then from His knowledge of His first effect necessarily follows His knowledge of His second effect. Because the second effect is a first effect of the first effect. And so on, from His knowledge of the second effect necessarily follows His knowledge of the first effect in descending order from Him, lengthwise and breadthwise. And know that this inference is only valid according to the doctrine of one who says that He, exalted, is a necessitating agent (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt) for the existence of these possibles. As for one who says that He, exalted, is a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār), this argument does not hold on his view. Then we say: if we say that the alif is the cause of the bā’, then either its being an alif is identical to its being a cause of the bā’, or its being an alif is distinct from its being a cause of the bā’, but it is among its concomitants (lawāzim). The first is false, because we necessarily know the distinction between saying 'the alif is an alif' and saying 'the alif is the cause of the bā’'. Were there no distinction, this differentiation would not occur. Also: the alif is an indication of that specific essence, and its being a cause of something is a relative, additional state. The specific reality which is not in itself a relation and addition must be distinct from the relation and addition. For it is possible to know that specific reality while being heedless of its being a cause of the bā’, and it is also possible to conceive the meaning of 'cause of the bā’' while being heedless of that specific reality. And that necessitates distinction. If this is established, we say: it is established that the alif's being an alif is distinct from its being a cause of the bā’. And when this distinction is real, His knowledge of His specific self and determinate essence is not identical to knowledge of His being a cause of the bā’. Unless it is said that His knowledge of His essence necessitates knowledge of its concomitants, but that is precisely the desired conclusion. If we make it a premise in proving this desired conclusion, that would be proving the thing by itself, and that is invalid. The full disclosure is that we said: the proof that from His knowledge of His specific essence necessarily follows His knowledge of His first effect is that He is, by His essence, the cause of that proximate effect. So if He knows His essence, He has known His being a cause of that proximate effect. And we say: the location of the fallacy is this speech. For if you intend that His essence is identical to its being a cause of the proximate effect, that is false. And if you grant the distinction, then why do you say that from His knowledge of that specific essence necessarily follows His knowledge of His being a cause of the proximate effect? And has the dispute occurred except over your statement that knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of its concomitants? So this is the complete investigation of this premise. Then we say: that which indicates that this premise is not true are [several] aspects. The first: we know many quiddities (māhiyyāt) and many realities. And every reality indicates—
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about whether God's knowledge of His own essence entails knowledge of all other things. It begins by stating the second principle (al-aṣl al-thānī): assuming God knows His specific essence, the opponent claims this necessarily implies knowledge of everything else because God is the cause of all. The author first notes that this argument only works if God is a necessitating agent (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt), not a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār). Then, using the example of 'alif' and 'bā’', the author examines the relationship between a cause and its effect. Two possibilities: either the essence of the cause is identical to being a cause, or it is distinct but a concomitant. The first is false because we can distinguish between the essence and the causal relation—they are conceptually distinct. The author supports this by noting that we can know the essence without knowing its causal role, and vice versa. Therefore, knowledge of the essence does not automatically give knowledge of being a cause. If one says that knowledge of essence necessitates knowledge of its concomitants, that is exactly what needs to be proven—thus circular. The author then exposes the fallacy: if the opponent claims identity, it's false; if they admit distinction, they still need to prove that knowledge of essence entails knowledge of its causal property, which is the very point in dispute. Finally, the author begins listing proofs that the premise is false, starting with the first proof that we know many quiddities without knowing their concomitants (but the text cuts off).

Key Terms
علة
موجب بالذات
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God knows His essence as the cause, He must automatically know all effects regardless of His agency.
Correction: The author shows that this only holds if God is a necessitating agent. If He is a free agent, knowledge of essence does not necessitate knowledge of effects because effects depend on His will.

Checkpoint
According to the author, on which view does the opponent's argument that God's knowledge of His essence implies knowledge of all things hold?
A) It holds on both views of God's agency.
B) It only holds if God is a necessitating agent (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt).
C) It only holds if God is a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār).
D) It does not hold on any view.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's causal argument depends on the assumption that God is a necessitating agent; it fails if God is a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as though God were a necessitating agent, expecting that your obedience automatically guarantees a specific outcome in your life? How does recognizing His free agency change the way you pray and trust His response?
?
The opponent assumed that knowing the cause gives certain knowledge of effects. In your own struggles, do you sometimes assume you fully understand your situation because you know its cause? How might God's freedom—and His hidden wisdom—humble that assumption?
?
If God is a free agent, then His actions are not compelled by His essence. Does the thought that God could have chosen otherwise for your life disturb you or bring you hope? Spend a moment in gratitude that His choices are guided by perfect wisdom and mercy, not necessity.

The Distinction Between Essence and Causality: The Alif and Bā' Example
The author performs a conceptual analysis of causality using the alif-bā' example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essence of a thing is distinct from its being a cause, and why this undermines the opponent's argument.

Core Idea
The essence of a thing is not identical to its causal relation, so knowing the essence does not automatically give knowledge of causality.

In this lesson, the author uses the simple example of 'alif' and 'bā'' to analyze the relationship between a cause and its effect. He considers two possibilities: identity or concomitance, and shows that identity is false. This leads to a crucial insight about the fallacy in the opponent's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the alif-bā' analysis
Read the passage where the author examines whether the essence of a cause is identical to its being a cause or merely a concomitant.
The terms 'alif' and 'bā'' are placeholder letters for cause and effect.
2
Understand the falsehood of identity
The author argues that we intuitively distinguish between 'the alif is an alif' and 'the alif is the cause of bā’', so they are not identical.
3
Grasp the circularity objection
If the opponent says the causal property is a concomitant, then knowing the essence necessitates knowing the concomitant. But that is exactly what needs to be proved, leading to circular reasoning.
4
Connect to God's knowledge
Apply this analysis to God: His essence and His being a cause are distinct. Therefore, knowing His essence does not entail knowing His causal role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني: سلمنا أنه تعالى عالم بذاته المخصوصة: فلم قلتم: إنه يلزم أن يكون عالماً بسائر الأشياء؟ وقد احتجوا عليه بأنه تعالى علة لما سواه. لذاته. فإذا علم ذات نفسه، وجب أن يعلم كونها علة لما عداه. وإذا علم ذلك فقد علم ما عداه. لأن من المحال أن يعلم كونه علة لما عداه أنه لا يعلم ما عداه. فيثبت أنه يلزم من علمه بذاته المخصوصة علمه بمعلوله القريب، ثم يلزم من علمه بمعلوله الأول علمه بمعلوله الثاني. لأن المعلول الثاني معلول أول، للمعلول الأول. وهكذا يلزم من علمه بالمعلول الثاني علمه بالمعلول الأول على الترتيب النازل من عنده طولاً وعرضاً. واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال إنما يتم على مذهب من يقول: إنه تعالى موجب بالذات لوجود هذه الممكنات. أما من يقول: إنه تعالى فاعل مختار، فإن هذه الحجة لا تتمشى على قوله. ثم نقول: إنا إذا قلنا إن الألف علة للباء - فإما أن يكون نفس كونه ألفاً، هو نفس كونه علة للباء. وإما أن يكون كون الألفاً مغايراً لكونه علة للباء، إلا أنه من لوازمه. والأول باطل. لأنا بالضرورة تعلم التفرقة بين أن يقال: الألف ألف. وبين أن يقال الألف علة للباء. ولولا التغاير لما حصلت هذه التفرقة. وأيضاً: فالألف إشارة إلى تلك الذات المخصوصة وكونه علة لكذا حالة نسبية إضافية والحقيقة المخصوصة التي ليست هي في نفسها نسبة وإضافة، لا بد وأن تكون مغايرة للنسبة وللإضافة: فإنه يصح العلم بتلك الحقيقة المخصوصة مع الذهول عن كونها علة للباء، ويصح أيضاً تعقل المفهوم من علة الباء، مع الذهول عن تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة. وذلك يوجب التغاير. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: ثبت أن كون الألف ألفاً مغاير لكونه علة للباء. وإذا كانت هذه المغايرة حقة لم يكن علمه بنفسه المخصوصة وذاته المعينة عين العلم بكونه علة للباء. اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن علمه بذاته يوجب العلم بلوازمه، إلا أن هذا نفس المطلوب. فإذا جعلناه مقدمة في إثبات هذا المطلوب، لزم إثبات الشيء بنفسه، وأنه باطل. وتمام الكشف أنا قلنا: الدليل على أنه يلزم من علمه لذاته المخصوصة علمه بمعلوله الأول: هو أنه لذاته علة لذلك المعلول القريب. فإذا علم ذاته فقد علم كونه علة لذلك المعلول القريب. ونقول: موضع المغالطة هذا هو الكلام. فإنكم إن أردتم أن ذاته نفس كونه علة للمعلول القريب. فهذا باطل. وإن سلمتم التغاير. فلم قلتم: إنه يلزم من علمه بتلك الذات المخصوصة علمه بكونه علة للمعلول القريب؟ وهل النزاع وقع إلا في قولكم: إن العلم بالشيء يوجب العلم بلوازمه؟ فهذا تمام البحث عن هذه المقدمة. ثم نقول: الذي يدل على أن هذه المقدمة ليست حقة وجوه: الأول: إنا نعلم ماهيات كثيرة، وحقائق كثيرة. وكل حقيقة يشير
The second principle: We grant that He, exalted, is knower of His specific essence: then why do you say that it necessarily follows that He is knower of all things? They have argued for it by [saying] that He, exalted, is the cause of what is other than Him. By His essence. So if He knows His own essence, He must know that it is the cause of what is other than Him. And if He knows that, then He has known what is other than Him. Because it is impossible that He knows that He is the cause of what is other than Him while not knowing what is other than Him. So it is established that from His knowledge of His specific essence necessarily follows His knowledge of His proximate effect, then from His knowledge of His first effect necessarily follows His knowledge of His second effect. Because the second effect is a first effect of the first effect. And so on, from His knowledge of the second effect necessarily follows His knowledge of the first effect in descending order from Him, lengthwise and breadthwise. And know that this inference is only valid according to the doctrine of one who says that He, exalted, is a necessitating agent (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt) for the existence of these possibles. As for one who says that He, exalted, is a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār), this argument does not hold on his view. Then we say: if we say that the alif is the cause of the bā’, then either its being an alif is identical to its being a cause of the bā’, or its being an alif is distinct from its being a cause of the bā’, but it is among its concomitants (lawāzim). The first is false, because we necessarily know the distinction between saying 'the alif is an alif' and saying 'the alif is the cause of the bā’'. Were there no distinction, this differentiation would not occur. Also: the alif is an indication of that specific essence, and its being a cause of something is a relative, additional state. The specific reality which is not in itself a relation and addition must be distinct from the relation and addition. For it is possible to know that specific reality while being heedless of its being a cause of the bā’, and it is also possible to conceive the meaning of 'cause of the bā’' while being heedless of that specific reality. And that necessitates distinction. If this is established, we say: it is established that the alif's being an alif is distinct from its being a cause of the bā’. And when this distinction is real, His knowledge of His specific self and determinate essence is not identical to knowledge of His being a cause of the bā’. Unless it is said that His knowledge of His essence necessitates knowledge of its concomitants, but that is precisely the desired conclusion. If we make it a premise in proving this desired conclusion, that would be proving the thing by itself, and that is invalid. The full disclosure is that we said: the proof that from His knowledge of His specific essence necessarily follows His knowledge of His first effect is that He is, by His essence, the cause of that proximate effect. So if He knows His essence, He has known His being a cause of that proximate effect. And we say: the location of the fallacy is this speech. For if you intend that His essence is identical to its being a cause of the proximate effect, that is false. And if you grant the distinction, then why do you say that from His knowledge of that specific essence necessarily follows His knowledge of His being a cause of the proximate effect? And has the dispute occurred except over your statement that knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of its concomitants? So this is the complete investigation of this premise. Then we say: that which indicates that this premise is not true are [several] aspects. The first: we know many quiddities (māhiyyāt) and many realities. And every reality indicates—
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about whether God's knowledge of His own essence entails knowledge of all other things. It begins by stating the second principle (al-aṣl al-thānī): assuming God knows His specific essence, the opponent claims this necessarily implies knowledge of everything else because God is the cause of all. The author first notes that this argument only works if God is a necessitating agent (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt), not a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār). Then, using the example of 'alif' and 'bā’', the author examines the relationship between a cause and its effect. Two possibilities: either the essence of the cause is identical to being a cause, or it is distinct but a concomitant. The first is false because we can distinguish between the essence and the causal relation—they are conceptually distinct. The author supports this by noting that we can know the essence without knowing its causal role, and vice versa. Therefore, knowledge of the essence does not automatically give knowledge of being a cause. If one says that knowledge of essence necessitates knowledge of its concomitants, that is exactly what needs to be proven—thus circular. The author then exposes the fallacy: if the opponent claims identity, it's false; if they admit distinction, they still need to prove that knowledge of essence entails knowledge of its causal property, which is the very point in dispute. Finally, the author begins listing proofs that the premise is false, starting with the first proof that we know many quiddities without knowing their concomitants (but the text cuts off).

Key Terms

معلول
تغاير
لوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the essence of a cause and its being a cause are the same thing.
Correction: The author shows they are distinct concepts; we can know one without the other.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the problem with the opponent's argument that knowledge of God's essence entails knowledge of being a cause?
A) It assumes identity between essence and causality, which is false.
B) It assumes distinction, which leads to circularity.
C) Both A and B are problems identified.
D) There is no problem; the argument is valid.

Key Takeaway
The essence of a cause and its causal property are distinct, so knowing the essence does not directly give knowledge of causality; claiming otherwise leads to circular reasoning.

?
The author exposes a fallacy: conflating essence with causal property. In your own spiritual journey, have you mistakenly thought that a particular practice (like prayer or charity) is identical to your faith, rather than being a concomitant? How will you reexamine your actions to ensure they flow from essence but are not confused with it?
?
This passage forces a distinction between what something is and what it does. Is there an area where you have blurred that line regarding God—perhaps expecting that because He is good, He must always act in a way you understand? How will you surrender to the fact that His causality is distinct from His essence, and trust His wisdom even when you cannot see the link?

الأصل الثاني: سلمنا أنه تعالى عالم بذاته المخصوصة: فلم قلتم: إنه يلزم أن يكون عالماً بسائر الأشياء؟ وقد احتجوا عليه بأنه تعالى علة لما سواه. لذاته. فإذا علم ذات نفسه، وجب أن يعلم كونها علة لما عداه. وإذا علم ذلك فقد علم ما عداه. لأن من المحال أن يعلم كونه علة لما عداه أنه لا يعلم ما عداه. فيثبت أنه يلزم من علمه بذاته المخصوصة علمه بمعلوله القريب، ثم يلزم من علمه بمعلوله الأول علمه بمعلوله الثاني. لأن المعلول الثاني معلول أول، للمعلول الأول. وهكذا يلزم من علمه بالمعلول الثاني علمه بالمعلول الأول على الترتيب النازل من عنده طولاً وعرضاً. واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال إنما يتم على مذهب من يقول: إنه تعالى موجب بالذات لوجود هذه الممكنات. أما من يقول: إنه تعالى فاعل مختار، فإن هذه الحجة لا تتمشى على قوله. ثم نقول: إنا إذا قلنا إن الألف علة للباء - Essence Distinct from Causal Property: Refuting Necessary Entailment
The author argues that God's essence is distinct from His being a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's essence is distinct from His attribute of being a cause and why knowledge of the essence does not automatically entail knowledge of its effects.

Core Idea
Knowledge of an essence does not automatically give knowledge of its relational properties.

This passage tackles a key question in Islamic theology: does God's knowledge of His own essence directly imply knowledge of everything He causes? The author argues that being a cause is a relational property distinct from the essence itself. He uses a logical example to clarify this distinction and then refutes the opposing view by showing it leads to circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the author repeats the example of 'alif' and 'ba'.
2
Identify the distinction being made
Focus on where the author argues that being a cause is different from the essence itself. Look for signs like 'مغاير' (different) and the argument about possible knowledge of one without the other.
3
Examine the objection and refutation
Find where the author addresses the claim that knowledge of essence necessitates knowledge of its consequences. Note how he labels this as circular.
The author calls it 'إثبات الشيء بنفسه' (establishing the thing by itself).
4
Reflect on the fallacy revealed
Understand the author's final point: if the essence were identical to being a cause, that is false; if distinct, entailment is not self-evident. The real dispute is about whether knowledge of a thing requires knowledge of its necessary consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً كونه الفاً مغايراً لكونه علة للباء ، إلا أنه من لوازمه . والأول باطل . لأنا بالضرورة تعلم التفرقة بين أن يقال (١) : الألف ألف . وبين أن يقال الألف علة للباء . ولولا التغاير لما حصلت هذه التفرقة . وأيضاً : فالألف إشارة إلى تلك الذات المخصوصة وكونه علة لكذا حالة نسبية إضافية والحقيقة المخصوصة التي ليست هي في نفسها نسبة وإضافة ، لا بد وأن تكون مغايرة للنسبة وللإضافة : فإنه يصح العلم بتلك الحقيقة المخصوصة مع الذهول عن كونها علة للباء ، ويصح أيضاً تعقل المفهوم من علة الباء ، مع الذهول عن تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة . وذلك يوجب التغاير . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : ثبت أن كون الألف ألفاً مغاير لكونه علة للباء . وإذا كانت هذه ( المغايرة حقة )(٢) لم يكن علمه بنفسه المخصوصة (٣) وذاته المعينة عين العلم بكونه علة للباء . اللهم إلا أن يقال : إن علمه بذاته يوجب العلم بلوازمه ، إلا أن هذا نفس المطلوب . فإذا جعلناه مقدمة في إثبات هذا المطلوب ، لزم إثبات الشيء بنفسه ، وأنه باطل . وتمام الكشف أنا قلنا : الدليل على أنه يلزم من علمه لذاته المخصوصة علمه بمعلوله الأول : هو أنه لذاته (٤) علة لذلك المعلول القريب . فإذا علم ذاته فقد علم كونه علة لذلك المعلول القريب. ونقول : موضع المغالطة هذا هو الكلام . فإنكم إن أردتم أن ذاته نفس كونه علة للمعلول القريب . فهذا باطل . وإن سلمتم التغاير . فلم قلتم : إنه يلزم من علمه بتلك الذات المخصوصة علمه بكونه علة للمعلول القريب ؟ وهل النزاع وقع إلا في قولكم : إن العلم بالشيء يوجب العلم بلوازمه ؟ فهذا تمام البحث عن هذه المقدمة
And also, its being an 'alif' is different from its being a cause for the 'ba'', except that it is among its necessary consequences. And the first is false. Because we necessarily know the distinction between saying (1): 'the alif is an alif' and saying 'the alif is a cause for the ba''. And were there no difference, this distinction would not have occurred. And also: the 'alif' indicates that specific essence, and its being a cause for something is a relative, additional state. And the specific reality which is not in itself a relation and addition must be different from the relation and addition. For it is possible to know that specific reality while being heedless of its being a cause for the ba'', and it is also possible to conceive the meaning of 'cause of the ba'' while being heedless of that specific reality. And that necessitates difference. If this is established, we say: it is established that its being an 'alif' is different from its being a cause for the ba''. And if this difference is real (2), then His knowledge of His specific self (3) and His designated essence is not identical to knowledge of His being a cause for the ba''. Unless it is said that His knowledge of His essence necessitates knowledge of its necessary consequences, but this is the very thing sought. If we make it a premise in establishing this sought thing, then it would be establishing the thing by itself, which is false. And the full disclosure: we said: the proof that from His knowledge of His specific essence follows knowledge of His first effect is that He is essentially (4) a cause for that proximate effect. So if He knows His essence, then He knows His being a cause for that proximate effect. And we say: the place of fallacy is this discourse. For if you intend that His essence is identical to His being a cause for the proximate effect, then this is false. And if you grant the difference, then why do you say that from His knowledge of that specific essence follows knowledge of His being a cause for the proximate effect? And has the dispute occurred except in your statement: 'knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of its necessary consequences'? This concludes the investigation of this premise.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God's knowledge of His essence entails knowledge of His first effect. The author first argues that being a cause is a relative property distinct from the essence itself, using the example of 'alif' and 'ba''. He shows that we can conceive the essence without conceiving its causal relation, and vice versa, establishing their difference. Then he addresses the potential objection that knowledge of the essence necessarily implies knowledge of its necessary consequences. He points out that this objection would be circular because it assumes what is to be proved. He further clarifies the fallacy: if one claims that the essence is identical to being a cause, that is false; if one admits difference, then the entailment is not self-evident. The passage concludes that the entire dispute reduces to whether knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of its necessary consequences.

Key Terms
مغايرة
علة
لوازم
ذات
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that if God knows His essence, He must automatically know all its consequences because the essence is identical to its causal properties.
Correction: The author shows that the essence is distinct from its causal property (being a cause is a relational attribute), so knowledge of the essence does not automatically entail knowledge of the effects. Claiming otherwise leads to circular reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the main point the author is making about God's knowledge of His essence and His being a cause?
A) God's knowledge of His essence is identical to knowledge of His being a cause.
B) Knowledge of an essence always entails knowledge of its necessary consequences.
C) Being a cause is a relative property distinct from the essence, so knowledge of one does not automatically give knowledge of the other.
D) God cannot know His own essence.

Key Takeaway
God's essence is distinct from His being a cause, so knowledge of one does not automatically entail knowledge of the other; otherwise, it leads to circular reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that knowing someone's character automatically tells you how they will act in every situation. How does the author's distinction between essence and causal property challenge that assumption, and what does that mean for your expectation of God's actions?
?

?
If knowledge of God's essence does not automatically give you knowledge of all His causal properties, how much humility is required when you try to interpret God's actions in your life? In what area do you need to admit that you don't know the deeper reasons behind what He does?

Ibn Sina's Argument that Knowledge of Others Implies Self-Knowledge
The author presents Ibn Sina's second proof that God's knowledge of others implies knowledge of His own essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's reasoning for establishing that God's knowledge of other things necessitates knowledge of His own essence, including the circularity argument against requiring the presence of forms in the mind.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of others logically implies self-knowledge; requiring presence leads to circularity.

Ibn Sina presents a subtle proof that God's knowledge of other things must include knowledge of His own essence. He first establishes that God knows others, then argues that this knowledge cannot be conditional on the presence of forms in the mind, on pain of circularity. This lesson walks through the logic step by step, even though the passage is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its structure
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice that the argument has a first premise (God knows others) and then a proof that this premise leads to self-knowledge through a conditional analysis.
The passage is cut off; the conclusion is implied.
2
Understand the first premise and its proof
The first premise is that God knows things other than Himself. The proof is that knowledge of any thing requires the form of that thing in the knower, and it is not impossible for God's essence to be known together with another thing.
Focus on the concept of conjunction (مقارنة) of two essences in the mind.
3
Follow the conditional analysis
The author considers whether the validity of this conjunction is conditional on the form being present in the mind. He argues that if it were, then presence itself is a conjunction, leading to a circular condition.
Circularity (دور) would make the condition impossible.
4
Grasp the circularity argument
If the conjunction's validity depended on presence, then we would need the conjunction to occur before it could be valid, but possibility must precede occurrence. That is circular and impossible.
Thus the conjunction is not conditional on presence; it is always valid.
5
Consider the intended conclusion
The argument shows that the conjunction is valid whether the form is present or not. The incomplete text likely concludes that since God knows others, this conjunction applies, so He knows His own essence.
Think about how this completes Ibn Sina's proof.

الطريق الثاني من الطرق التي ذكرها الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي بن سينا قال : دل الدليل على أنه تعالى عالم بغيره، وإذا كان العالماً بغيره، وجب كونه عالماً بذاته. أما الأصل الأول : وهو قولنا : إنه عالم بغيره . فالدليل عليه . هو أنه لا يمتنع أن تعلم ذات الله تعالى مع أي معلوم كان. وقد ثبت بالدليل أن العلم بالشيء لا يحصل إلا عند انطباع صورة المعلوم في العالم ، فإذا حصل العلم بذات الله تعالى مع العلم بغيره ، فقد حضرت هاتان الماهيتان في الذهن معاً ، فحصول المقارنة بين هاتين الماهيتين غير ممتنع . فإما أن تكون صحة هذه المقارنة مشروطة بكون هذه الصورة حاضرة في الذهن وأما أن لا تكون . والأول باطل ، لأن حضور هذه الصورة في الذهن مقارنة لهذه الماهية مع الذهن، فلو كانت صحة هذه المقارنة مشروطة بكون هذه الصورة حاضرة في الذهن وقد دللنا على أن حضورها في الذهن مقارنة بينها وبين الذهن لزم أن تكون صحة هذه المقارنة مشروطة بحصول هذه المقارنة، لكن بشرط سابق على المشروط فيلزم أن يكون وقوع الشيء سابقاً بالرتبة على إمكان وقوعه، لكن الإمكان سابق على الوقوع فيلزم الدور وهو محال ، فيثبت بهذا الدليل : أن صحة هذه المقارنة غير مشروطة بكون هذه الصورة حاضرة في الذهن ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فسواء حضرت تلك الماهية في الذهن أو في الخارج، وجب أن يصح عليها هذه المقارنة ، فكما أن الصورة المعقولة من ذات الله تعالى كان يجوز أن تقارنها صور...
The second way among the ways mentioned by the Chief Master Abu Ali ibn Sina: He said: The proof indicates that He, exalted, is knower of other than Himself. And if He is knower of other than Himself, then necessarily He is knower of His own essence. As for the first premise—that is, our statement that He is knower of other than Himself—the proof for it is that it is not impossible that the essence of Allah, exalted, be known along with any known thing. And it has been established by proof that knowledge of a thing does not occur except when the form of the known thing is impressed in the knower. So if knowledge of the essence of Allah, exalted, occurs together with knowledge of other than Him, then these two essences become present in the mind together; so the occurrence of conjunction between these two essences is not impossible. Now either the validity of this conjunction is conditional upon this form being present in the mind, or it is not. The first is invalid, because the presence of this form in the mind is a conjunction of this essence with the mind. So if the validity of this conjunction were conditional upon this form being present in the mind, and we have demonstrated that its presence in the mind is a conjunction between it and the mind, then it would follow that the validity of this conjunction is conditional upon the occurrence of this conjunction—but by a condition prior to the conditioned. Hence it would entail that the occurrence of a thing is prior in rank to the possibility of its occurrence; but possibility is prior to occurrence, so circularity (al-dawr) would be entailed, which is impossible. Thus it is established by this proof that the validity of this conjunction is not conditional upon this form being present in the mind. And if the matter is so, then whether that essence is present in the mind or in the external world, this conjunction must be valid for it. So just as the intelligible form of the essence of Allah, exalted, is permissible for it to be accompanied by forms...
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's second argument that God's knowledge of other things necessitates His knowledge of His own essence. The argument begins with the first premise: God knows things other than Himself. The proof for this premise is based on the principle that knowledge occurs through the impression (انطباع) of the form of the known object in the knower (العالم). It is not impossible for the essence of Allah to be known together with any other known thing. If such simultaneous knowledge occurs, then both essences (ماهيتان) are present in the mind together, and their conjunction (مقارنة) is not impossible. Now, the validity of this conjunction is either conditional upon the form of the known object being present in the mind, or it is not. The first option leads to a contradiction: the presence of the form in the mind is itself a conjunction between that essence and the mind. If the validity of the conjunction were conditional on that presence, then it would be conditional on itself—a circular priority (دور). This would require the occurrence of a thing to be prior in rank to its possibility, which is impossible because possibility precedes occurrence. Therefore, the validity of the conjunction is not conditional on the form being present in the mind. Hence, whether the object's form is present in the mind or externally, the conjunction remains valid. This establishes that God's essence can be known together with other forms. The argument is cut off here, but the intended conclusion is that since God knows others and the conjunction is valid, He also knows His own essence.

Key Terms
ذات (essence)
عالم (knower)
صورة (form)
مقارنة (conjunction)
انطباع (impression)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: For two forms to be known together, the second form must be present in the mind as a condition for the conjunction.
Correction: The passage shows that such a condition leads to circularity, because presence in the mind is itself a conjunction. Therefore, the conjunction does not require presence; it is valid independently.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that the validity of the conjunction is conditional on the form being present in the mind?
A) Because it leads to an infinite regress.
B) Because if it were conditional, it would be conditional on itself, causing circularity.
C) Because the form of God's essence is always present in the mind.
D) Because the conjunction is impossible without presence.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's argument shows that God's knowledge of others cannot be conditional on forms being present in the mind, thereby paving the way to conclude that God knows His own essence as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Ibn Sina argues that circularity is impossible—God's knowledge is unconditionally true. If you truly believed that God knows you completely without any condition, what would you change about how you pray, repent, or obey?
?
The argument shows that God's self-knowledge is inseparable from His knowledge of creation. Are you living as if your relationship with Him is separate from the rest of your life, or are you seeking to integrate your actions with the reality that He knows you intimately and wholly?

Ibn Sina's Second Path: Proving Allah's Knowledge of Others
Ibn Sina's second path: proving God's knowledge of others via comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's second path to prove that God knows other-than-Himself, based on the possibility of comparison between essences and the rejection of circularity.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of others is proven by the unconditional validity of comparison.

This passage introduces Ibn Sina's second proof for divine knowledge of others. Al-Razi presents a careful argument based on the structure of intellection and the rejection of a conditional that would lead to circularity. The proof establishes that God, existing externally, can be compared with other intelligibles, meaning He knows them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and initial statement
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the initial claim: if God knows others, He must know Himself. Then focus on the first principle: He knows others.
The passage is dense, so read carefully.
2
Understand the first principle: God knows another
The proof for God knowing others starts with the possibility of God's essence being known along with any known thing. Since knowledge requires the imprinting of forms, both essences are present in the mind. The key question is whether the validity of comparing these essences is conditioned on one form being present.
3
Follow the conditional argument and its rejection
Al-Razi argues that if validity were conditioned on presence, then the presence of the form itself is a comparison, leading to circularity (the condition would require itself). This is impossible, so the condition is false.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Conclude that comparison is unconditional
Since the condition is false, the comparison is valid regardless of whether the essence is in the mind or externally. Thus, God's external essence can be compared with other forms, meaning He knows them. Therefore, God is knowledgeable of things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني من الطرق التي ذكرها الشيخ الرئيس (أبو علي بن سينا) : قال : دل الدليل على أنه تعالى عالم بغيره ، وإذا كان العالماً بغيره ، وجب كونه عالماً بذاته .

أما الأصل الأول : وهو قولنا : إنه عالم بغيره . فالدليل عليه . هو أنه لا يمتنع أن تعلم ذات الله تعالى مع أي معلوم كان. وقد ثبت بالدليل أن العلم بالشيء لا يحصل إلا عند انطباع صورة المعلوم في العالِم . فإذا حصل العلم بذات الله تعالى مع العلم بغيره ، فقد حضرت هاتان الماهيتان في الذهن معاً ، فحصول المقارنة بين هاتين الماهيتين غير ممتنع . فإما أن تكون صحة هذه المقارنة مشروطة بكون هذه الصورة حاضرة في الذهن وإما أن لا تكون . والأول باطل ، لأن حضور هذه الصورة في الذهن مقارنة لهذه الماهية مع الذهن، فلو كانت صحة هذه المقارنة مشروطة بكون هذه الصورة حاضرة في الذهن وقد دللنا على أن حضورها في الذهن مقارنة بينها وبين الذهن لزم أن تكون صحة هذه المقارنة مشروطة بحصول هذه المقارنة ، لكن بشرط سابق على المشروط فيلزم أن يكون وقوع الشيء سابقاً بالرتبة على إمكان وقوعه، لكن الإمكان سابق على الوقوع فيلزم الدور وهو محال ، فيثبت بهذا الدليل : أن صحة هذه المقارنة غير مشروطة بكون هذه الصورة حاضرة في الذهن ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فسواء حضرت تلك الماهية في الذهن أو في الخارج، وجب أن يصح عليها هذه المقارنة ، فكما أن الصورة المعقولة من ذات الله تعالى كان يجوز أن تقارنها صور سائر المعقولات ، وجب أن يصح على ذات الله حال كونها موجوداً في الأعيان أن يقارنها صور سائر المعقولات ، لكنا قد بينا أنه لا معنى للتعقل والإدراك إلا هذه المقارنة ، فلما دل الدليل على أن هذه المقارنة جائزة على ذات الله تعالى . وجب القطع بأنه يصح على ذات الله تعالى أن يكون عالماً بالأشياء
The second path among the paths mentioned by the Chief Master (Abu Ali ibn Sina). He said: The proof indicates that He, exalted, is knowing by another, and if He is knowing by another, then He must be knowing of Himself.

As for the first principle: it is our statement: He is knowing by another. The proof for it is that it is not impossible that the essence of Allah, exalted, be known along with any known thing. And it has been established by proof that knowledge of a thing only occurs upon the imprinting of the form of the known in the knower. So if knowledge of the essence of Allah, exalted, occurs with knowledge of another, then these two essences are present in the mind together. So the occurrence of comparison between these two essences is not impossible. Then either the validity of this comparison is conditioned upon this form being present in the mind, or it is not. The first is false, because the presence of this form in the mind is a comparison of this essence with the mind. So if the validity of this comparison were conditioned upon this form being present in the mind, and we have shown that its presence in the mind is a comparison between it and the mind, it would follow that the validity of this comparison be conditioned upon the occurrence of this comparison. But this is a condition prior to the conditioned, so it follows that the occurrence of a thing is prior in rank to its possibility. But possibility is prior to occurrence, so circularity follows, which is impossible. Thus it is established by this proof that the validity of this comparison is not conditioned upon this form being present in the mind. And if the matter is so, then whether that essence is present in the mind or in the external world, this comparison must be valid for it. So just as the intelligible form of the essence of Allah, exalted, is permissible for it to be compared with forms of other intelligibles, it must be valid for the essence of Allah, as it exists externally, that forms of other intelligibles compare with it. But we have explained that there is no meaning to intellection and perception except this comparison. So when the proof indicates that this comparison is permissible upon the essence of Allah, exalted, it is necessary to decide that it is valid for the essence of Allah, exalted, to be knowledgeable of things.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's second path to prove that God knows other-than-Himself. It begins by stating the conclusion: if God knows others, He must know Himself. Then it establishes the first principle: God knows others. The proof is based on the possibility of comparing two essences in the mind. The argument is that it is not impossible for God's essence to be known along with any other known thing. Since knowledge requires the form of the known to be imprinted in the knower, if God's essence is known along with another, both essences are present in the mind. The key question is whether the validity of comparing these two essences is conditioned on one form being present in the mind. Al-Razi argues that if it were conditioned, it would lead to circularity because the presence of the form in the mind is itself a comparison with the mind. Therefore, the condition is not required. Consequently, whether the essence is in the mind or externally, the comparison is valid. Since intellection is nothing but such comparison, God's essence, existing externally, can be compared with other forms, meaning He can know them. Thus, it is proven that God is knowledgeable of things.

مقارنة
انطباع صورة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on a circular argument.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if the validity of comparison were conditional, it would lead to circularity, so the condition must be rejected. The proof itself is not circular; it exposes circularity in the opposing view.

C) It would lead to a contradiction.
D) It would lead to the impossibility of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's second path proves God's knowledge of others by showing that the validity of comparison between essences is not conditioned on presence in the mind, thus God's external essence can compare with other forms, and intellection is nothing but such comparison.

The argument shows that God's knowledge is not conditioned on anything. Are you living as if God's knowledge makes a difference in your daily choices? Think of a specific decision you made today. How would you have acted differently if you were fully aware that God's knowledge of you is immediate and complete?

The Necessity Argument: Whatever is Valid for Allah Must Occur
Al-Razi proves that change is impossible for Allah, so any valid attribute must be necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that any attribute valid for Allah must necessarily be realized, because change is impossible for Him.

Core Idea
For Allah, anything possible must be actual; otherwise change would be possible.

Al-Razi now presents a second proof that Allah must know all things, based on the impossibility of change. He argues that if an attribute could be absent when it is valid, that would imply that Allah could change, which leads to a contradiction. This lesson unpacks that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that al-Razi starts with a strong claim: anything valid for Allah must occur. Underline that claim.
This claim is the conclusion he wants to prove.
2
Understand the three cases of attributes
Al-Razi then considers every possible attribute in relation to Allah's essence. He lists three cases: the essence is independent in requiring it, independent in repelling it, or neither. Understand each case and its consequence.
The first two cases lead to perpetuity (always or never). The third is the problematic one.
3
Follow the dependency chain showing impossibility
For the third case, al-Razi shows that if the essence is not independent with respect to an attribute, then both affirmation and negation require an external cause. This leads to a chain of dependencies that would make the Necessary of Existence dependent on another, which is impossible.
This is the heart of the proof: reducing to contradiction.
4
Connect to the conclusion that Allah must know all things
Once change is ruled out, any attribute that is valid for Allah must be actual. Since it is already established that Allah can know things (from earlier proof), it follows that He must know them. This completes the argument.
The explicit conclusion is about knowledge, but the principle applies to all attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كل ما صح على ذات الله تعالى وجب حصوله له ، لأنه لو صح عليه أمر من الأمور ، مع أنه لا يجب حصوله . فحينئذ يكون التغير جائزاً عليه . وذلك محال ، لأن كل صفة تفرض فإما أن تكون ذات الله مستقلة باقتضائها ، وإما أن تكون مستقلة بدفعها . وإما أن لا تكون مستقلة لا بالاقتضاء ولا بالدفع . والأولان يوجبان إما دوام الإيجاب ، أو دوام السلب وذلك يمنع من جواز التغير . والثالث يوجب المحال ، لأن تلك الذات لا تنفك عن ثبوت تلك الصفة وسلبها ، لكن ثبوتها وسلبها لا بد فيها من سبب منفصل عن الذات. فعلى هذا : الذات موقوفة التحقق على ثبوت تلك الصفة أو سلبها . وذلك الثبوت وذلك السلب موقوف التحقق على ثبوت شيء منفصل أو سلبه ، والموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء موقوف على الشيء . فذات واجب الوجود موقوفة في تحققها على الغير، والموقوف على الغير ممكن لذاته. فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود . وهو محال . فيثبت أن التغير على ذات الله تعالى وصفاته محال ، وإذا كان كذلك فكل ما صح في حق الله تعالى ، وجب أن يكون حاصلاً . وقد ثبت أنه يصح كونه تعالى عالماً بالمعلومات ، فوجب أن يكون عالماً بها . فهذا تمام الكلام في بيان أن الله تعالى يجب أن يكون عالماً بما سواه
If this is established, we say: Everything that is valid for the essence of Allah, exalted, must necessarily occur for Him. Because if an affair among affairs were valid for Him without it being necessary to occur, then change would be permissible for Him. And that is impossible. Because every attribute that is supposed: either the essence of Allah is independent in its requirement, or it is independent in its repulsion, or it is not independent either in requirement or repulsion. The first two necessitate either perpetuity of affirmation or perpetuity of negation, and that prevents the possibility of change. The third necessitates impossibility, because that essence is not free from affirmation or negation of that attribute, but its affirmation and negation inevitably require a cause separate from the essence. So on this basis: the essence is dependent in its realization on the affirmation or negation of that attribute. And that affirmation and that negation are dependent in realization on the affirmation or negation of something separate. And that which is dependent on that which is dependent on a thing is dependent on the thing. So the essence of the Necessary of Existence is dependent in its realization on another. And that which is dependent on another is possible in itself. So it follows that the Necessary of Existence in itself be possible of existence, which is impossible. Thus it is established that change upon the essence of Allah, exalted, and His attributes is impossible. And if it is so, then everything that is valid in the right of Allah, exalted, must necessarily occur. And it has been established that it is valid for Him to be knowledgeable of things, so He must be knowledgeable of them. This completes the discussion in explaining that Allah, exalted, must be knowledgeable of other-than-Himself.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof that God must know things, based on the impossibility of change in Allah. It begins by stating that anything valid for Allah must necessarily occur; otherwise change would be possible. Al-Razi then classifies every possible attribute in relation to God's essence: either the essence is independent in requiring it (i.e., it is essentially necessary), independent in repelling it (i.e., essentially negated), or neither. The first two lead to perpetuity—the attribute is always present or always absent—and thus no change. The third case leads to impossibility: the essence cannot be devoid of either affirmation or negation, so both require an external cause. This makes the essence dependent on that cause for its own realization, and through a chain of dependencies, the Necessary of Existence would become dependent on another, making it possible in itself, which is a contradiction. Therefore, change is impossible, and any valid attribute must be actual. Since it has been proven that God can know things, He must know them. This concludes the first principle.

تغير
دوام الإيجاب
دوام السلب
موقوف التحقق
واجب الوجود
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's attributes could be contingent, meaning He could acquire or lose them without contradiction.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that contingency implies change, which would require an external cause for God's essence, contradicting His necessary existence. Therefore, all valid attributes must be permanent and actual.

B) It would be subject to change.
C) It would be dependent on an external cause.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's argument shows that Allah cannot change, so every attribute that is valid for Him must be actual, including knowledge.

Will you live differently knowing that nothing in your life can surprise Him or alter His eternal decree?

What would change if you fully internalized that His attributes are necessary and not contingent?

Second Principle: Allah Must Know Himself
Al-Razi begins to argue that from knowing others, Allah must also know Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial argument for why Allah must know Himself based on the capacity for reflexive knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowing implies the ability to know that one knows.

Al-Razi now turns to a second foundational principle: that Allah must know Himself. The argument is straightforward: anyone who knows something has the potential to be aware of that knowledge. Since Allah knows all things, He must also know that He knows, which is equivalent to knowing Himself. This lesson unpacks that initial claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style of Al-Razi's argument.
The passage ends abruptly; the argument continues beyond this page.
2
Identify the logical structure
Find the premise (the established matter) and the conclusion (Allah must know Himself). The connecting reason is given: every knower can know that he knows.
The premise refers back to the first principle that Allah knows all things.
3
Understand the key term
Focus on the phrase 'ʿāliman bidhātih' (knowledgeable of Himself). Reflect on what it means for Allah to know His own essence.
This term is central to the principle.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this principle is important for tawḥīd. Knowing Himself is part of Allah's perfection and distinguishes Him from creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل الثاني : أنه إذا ثبت أن الأمر كذلك . فنقول : يجب أيضاً أن يكون عالماً بذاته، لأن كل من علم شيئاً فإنه يمكنه أن يعلم كونه عالماً به
And the second principle: that if it is established that the matter is so, we say: He must also be knowledgeable of Himself, because whoever knows something, then it is possible for him to know that he is knowledgeable of it.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage introduces the second principle: God must know Himself. The initial argument is that anyone who knows something can also know that he knows it. This reflexive awareness is a capacity inherent in knowledge. The passage ends here, but the implication is that since God knows all things, He must also know that He knows, i.e., He knows Himself. The argument is incomplete due to the page break, but the direction is clear.

What is the initial argument given for God knowing Himself?
A) Because He knows all things.
B) Because every knower can know that he knows.
C) Because His essence is simple.
D) Because He is the Necessary Being.

Key Takeaway
The ability to know one's own knowledge is inherent in all knowledge, and since Allah's knowledge is perfect, He must know Himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah knows even your hidden thoughts? If so, why do you still commit sins in secret? What would change if you acted as if every moment were witnessed by the All-Knowing?
?
You have the capacity to know that you know. How often do you reflect on your own knowledge of Allah? How does this reflection deepen your conviction?
?
If every knower can know that they know, then consider that Allah, the Perfect Knower, knows Himself perfectly. How does this reality make you feel about your own ignorance? What will you do today to seek knowledge of your Creator?
