The First Objection: Human Inability to Know God's Essence
Objector denies human knowledge of God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the argument from degrees, which denies that humans can know God's essence.

Core Idea
Humans can only know God through His attributes, not His essence.

In this passage, the objector challenges the very possibility of knowing God's essence. He invokes the philosophers' distinction between what is knowable about God—negative and relational attributes—and the unknowable essence itself. This objection, if valid, would undermine the argument's assumption that we can conceive of God's essence. However, note that the argument is about God's own knowledge, not human knowledge, so the objection may be misplaced.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the objector's central assertion: that we cannot know God's essence at all.
The objector starts with 'la nusallimu' (we do not accept).
2
Understand the philosophers' doctrine
The objector attributes to the philosophers the view that the inner reality (kunn) of God's essence is unintelligible to humans. Grasp what 'unintelligible' means here.
This is a key concept in Islamic philosophy and theology.
3
Distinguish between attributes and essence
The objector says only negative and relational attributes are known, not the essence in itself. Reflect on the difference between knowing about something and knowing its essence.
Negative attributes: what God is not. Relational: His relation to creation.
4
Reflect on the objection's target
Ask yourself: does this objection actually refute the earlier argument about God's self-knowledge? The objector talks about human knowledge, but the argument concerned God's knowledge of His own essence.
This distinction is crucial for evaluating the objection's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : الاعتراض على هذا الكلام من وجوه : السؤال الأول : لا نسلم أنه يمكننا أن نعقل ذات الله فضلا عن أن يقال إنه يمكننا أن نعقل ذاته مع تعقل سائر الذوات. وتقرير هذا الكلام : هو أن مذهب الحكماء : أن كنه ذات الله غير معقول للبشر . وإنما المعلوم عند الخلق من الله تعالى الصفات السلبية والصفات الإضافية . فأما الذات المخصوصة من حيث هي هي فغير معلومة للخلق . وإذا ثبت أن تلك الذات المخصوصة غير معلومة البتة ، كان القول بأنه يصح العلم بها حال حصول العلم بغيرها : خطا
One might say: There are objections to this discourse from several aspects: The first question: We do not accept that we can intellect the essence of God, let alone it be said that we can intellect His essence along with intellecting other essences. The exposition of this statement: It is the doctrine of the philosophers (al-hukama’) that the inner reality (kunn) of God's essence is unintelligible to humans. What is known among creatures of God most high are only negative attributes (al-sifat al-salbiyya) and relational attributes (al-sifat al-idafiyya). As for the particular essence in itself (al-dhat al-makhsusa min haythu hiya hiya), it is entirely unknown to creatures. And if it is established that that particular essence is absolutely unknown, then the claim that it is possible to have knowledge of it while having knowledge of other things is an error.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the previous proof. The objector denies the very possibility of humans knowing God's essence, let alone knowing it along with other essences. The objector invokes the philosophers' distinction between what is known of God—namely, negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) and relational attributes (sifat idafiyya)—and the unknowable essence itself. Since the essence is absolutely unknown to creatures, the objector argues that the premise of the earlier argument (that God's self-knowledge is possible) is flawed because it implicitly assumes that we can conceive of God's essence. However, note that the original argument was about God's knowledge of His own essence, not human knowledge. The objector's shift to human knowledge may be a misunderstanding. The answer, which is not provided in this window, would likely clarify that the argument does not rely on human knowledge of God's essence, but on the logical possibility of God's self-intellection.

Misconception: The objection proves that God cannot know His own essence.
Correction: The objection is about human knowledge of God's essence, not God's self-knowledge. The argument's original premise concerns God's knowledge of His own essence, which is not addressed by this objection.

Checkpoint

B) It is known to humans only partially.
C) Its inner reality (kunn) is unintelligible to humans.
D) It is known by intuition.

Key Takeaway
The first objection claims that humans cannot know God's essence, only His attributes, but this may not affect the argument about God's self-knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you pray, do you focus on God's attributes (His mercy, power, etc.) or do you secretly try to imagine His essence? Will you accept that His essence is unknowable and learn to love Him through what He has revealed of Himself?
?
If God's essence is completely unknown to you, what does that mean for your relationship with Him? Are you content to know Him through His attributes, or does this limitation trouble you? Commit to one specific way you will embrace this truth in your daily devotion.

The Proof for God's Knowledge of Himself and All Things
Summary proof for divine knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if God knows anything other than Himself, He must know Himself, and how this establishes His all-encompassing knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge of another implies the possibility of self-knowledge; with possibility, necessity is removed.

This lesson presents a concise argument for God's knowledge, central to Islamic theology. The author begins by defining knowledge as a judgment requiring prior conceptualization. From this, he deduces that if God knows things other than Himself, He can know His own essence, and this possibility, combined with earlier proofs, establishes it as actual.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of knowledge
The passage defines knowledge as a judgment on an essence. Notice that every judgment (taṣdīq) requires a prior conceptualization (taṣawwur) of its terms.
2
Follow the argument from other-knowledge to self-knowledge
The author argues that if a knower knows something else, he can know himself because the same intellectual capacity is involved. For God, since He knows all other things, He can know Himself.
3
See how possibility leads to actuality
Once possibility is established, the earlier proof that negates necessity applies. Thus, God's self-knowledge is proven.
This is the 'preceding proof' mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعلم بكونه عالماً ، بكذا ) حكم على ذاته بكونه عالما بكذا )(۱) وكل تصديق فإنه مسبوق بتصور طرفيه، فالعالم بغيره يمكنه أن يعلم ذاته ، ومتى ثبت الإمكان فقد نفي (٢) الوجوب بالدليل المتقدم، فيثبت أنه تعالى عالم بكل ما سواه (۳) ، وأنه عالم بذاته المخصوصة . هذا تلخيص هذا الكلام على أحسن الوجوه ، وهو المراد من قول الشيخ الرئيس في كتاب الإشارات في النمط الثالث في النفس الأرضية (٤) : «كل من يعقل شيئاً ، فإنه يمكنه أن يعقل بالقوة القريبة من الفعل إلى آخر هذا الفصل .(٤) نص عبارة ابن سينا : ( إنك تعلم أن كل شيء يعقل شيئاً ، فإنه يعقل بالقوة القريبة من الفعل أن يعقل ، وذلك عقل منه لذاته ، فكل ما يعقل شيئاً فله أن يعقل ذاته . وكل ما يعقل فمن شأنه ماهيته أن تقارن معقولاً آخر ، ولذلك يعمل أيضاً مع غيره . وإنما تعقله القوة العاقلة بالمقارنة لا محالة . فإن كان مما يقوم بذاته ، فلا مانع له من حقيقته . أن يقارن المعنى المعقول . اللهم إلا أن تكون ذاته ممنوة في الوجود بمقارنة أمور مانعة من ذلك من مادة أو شيء آخر إن كان . فإن كانت حقيقته مسلمة لم يمتنع عليها مقارنة الصورة العقلية إياها فكان لها ذلك بالإمكان . وفي ضمن ذلك إمكان عقله لذاته ، ( فصل ۱۷ خط ۳ ) .
And the knowledge that He is knowing, of such-and-such, (is) a judgment upon His essence that He is knowing of such-and-such. And every affirmation (taṣdīq) is preceded by a conceptualization (taṣawwur) of its two terms. Thus, the one who knows other than himself can know his own essence. And when possibility is established, necessity has been negated by the preceding proof, so it is established that He, exalted, is knowing of everything other than Him, and that He is knowing of His own particular essence. This is a summary of this discourse in the best manner. And it is what is intended by the statement of the Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'īs) in the Book of Indications (Kitāb al-Ishārāt), in the third pattern, on the earthly soul: 'Whoever intellectually apprehends something, he can intellectually apprehend, in a potentiality close to actuality... to the end of this section.' (Footnote: Text of Ibn Sina's statement: 'You know that everything that intellectually apprehends something, it intellectually apprehends in a potentiality close to actuality that it intellects, and that is an intellection of its own essence. So everything that intellects something has the ability to intellect its own essence. And everything that intellects, it is in the nature of its quiddity to be associated with another intelligible, and therefore it also acts along with other things. And the intellective power only intellects by association necessarily. If it is something subsistent by itself, there is no hindrance from its reality to associate with the intelligible meaning, unless its essence is in existence hindered by the association of preventing things, from matter or something else if there be. If its reality is unimpeded, then the association of the intelligible form with it is not prevented, so it has that by possibility. And within that is the possibility of its intellection of its own essence. (Chapter 17, line 3).')
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for God's knowledge of Himself and all other things. It begins by defining knowledge (ʿilm) as a judgment of the intellect about the essence of something. For any judgment (taṣdīq) or affirmation to occur, it must be preceded by a conceptualization (taṣawwur) of the two terms involved (the subject and predicate). The argument then proceeds: if a knowing subject can know something other than itself, then it can also know its own essence. Since God knows all things other than Himself (as established by earlier proofs), the possibility of His knowing Himself is affirmed. With that possibility established, the earlier proof (presumably of God's necessity) negates any necessity that would prevent it, so it is established that God knows His own particular essence and everything else. The author presents this as a summary of a longer discourse, citing Ibn Sina's statement from the Ishārāt that whoever intellects something has the potential to intellect his own essence. The footnote provides the full text of Ibn Sina's argument, which elaborates that any intellective being can by its nature associate with other intelligibles unless hindered by matter, and thus can potentially intellect its own essence.

Key Terms
علم
تصديق
تصور
ذات
إمكان
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes what it proves.
Correction: It is not circular because it starts from an accepted premise that God knows other things and uses the nature of intellection to deduce self-knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the summary argument, what must precede every affirmation (taṣdīq)?
A) A proof from scripture
B) A conceptualization (taṣawwur) of its two terms
C) A sensory experience
D) A direct intuition of God

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of Himself follows logically from His knowledge of other things, using the principle that intellection of another implies potential for self-intellection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of His own essence is so certain, how confident are you that your own knowledge of God is truly a tasdiq (firm affirmation) rather than just a vague concept? What step must you take today to move from mere concept to genuine affirmation?
?
The proof shows that God's self-knowledge is logically necessitated by His knowledge of all things. Does this truth change how you relate to God's knowledge of your own innermost thoughts? Will you live today as if every thought is known by Him, or will you forget?
?
You have just learned that knowledge of another implies the capacity for self-knowledge. Consider your own limited knowledge: do you know yourself as God knows you? What specific aspect of your soul are you avoiding knowing? Ask God to grant you the courage to face it.

Ibn Sina's Argument on the Nature of Intellection and Self-Knowledge
Ibn Sina citation on intellection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's explanation of how any intellective being, if not hindered by matter, can potentially intellect its own essence, and how this supports the proof of God's self-knowledge.

Core Idea
Intellection of an other implies the potential to intellect oneself, unless hindered by matter.

This lesson delves into the philosophical underpinning of the earlier argument. The author cites Ibn Sina's Ishārāt, which elaborates on the nature of intellection. Ibn Sina argues that any being that intellects something has the potential to intellect its own essence, provided there is no material hindrance. This reinforces the conclusion that God, being immaterial, necessarily intellects Himself.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's statement
Read the quoted text from the Ishārāt carefully. Notice the key terms: 'intelligible', 'potentiality', 'essence'.
2
Identify the conditions for self-intellection
Ibn Sina explains that if a thing's essence is not hindered by matter or other impediments, it can associate with the intelligible form of itself. This possibility is inherent in any intellective being.
3
Apply to God
Since God is pure intellect without matter, the possibility becomes actual. This supports the earlier argument that God knows Himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Second Objection: Can We Know God’s Essence Alongside Other Knowledge?
The opponent presents the second objection with two supporting arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against simultaneous knowledge of God's essence and other things, including its two supporting arguments based on psychological experience and sensory analogy.

Core Idea
The mind cannot hold two strong knowledges at once—this is the objection’s core claim.

The author now presents the second objection from the opponent. Based on psychological experience and sensory analogy, the opponent argues that knowledge of God's essence is so immense that it blocks all other knowledge. Let us examine these arguments carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Start by reading the opponent's question: why can't we know God's essence along with other things?
2
Identify the first argument
The opponent claims we cannot direct the mind to two items of knowledge at once, using the example of logical versus geometrical questions.
This is a psychological argument about mental focus.
3
Identify the second argument
The opponent analogizes to sensory perception: strong sensations overwhelm weak ones, so God's essence overwhelms all other knowledge.
The sensory analogy uses the sun and lamp, thunder and mosquito.
4
Understand the conclusion
Both arguments lead to the conclusion that simultaneous knowledge of God and other things is impossible. Reflect on why the opponent thinks so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا أنه يصح أن تصير ذات الله تعالى معلومة . فلم قلتم : إنه يصح العلم بها ، مع العلم بغيرها ؟ وتقريره : أنا نجد من أنفسنا وجداناً ضرورياً، أنا متى وجهنا الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم فإنه لا يمكننا في ذات الوقت توجيه الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم آخر . والعلم بذلك ضروري. ولذلك فإن من كان مشتغل الفكر في مسألة منطقية فإنه في تلك اللحظة لا يمكنه توجيه الذهن نحو مسألة هندسية . والعلم بذلك ضروري . وهذا يدل على أن الجمع بين علمين في الذهن دفعة واحدة محال ، أو نقول إنه وإن كان ذلك ممكناً (٣) في الجملة ، إلا أنه ههنا غير ممكن ، لأنه ثبت بالاستقرار : أن القوى الحاسة حال شعورها بالمحسوسات القوية ، لا يمكنها الشعور بالمحسوسات الضعيفة . فالسراج إذا وضع في مقابلة قرص الشمس لا نحس بذلك السراج، والبعوضة إذا طنت عند ظهور الرعد لا نحس بطنين البعوضة ، فكذلك هنا ذات الله تعالى أعظم الموجودات . وعليه فإنه أعلم المعارف ، وعند حضور عرفانه في العقل ، يمتنع حضور عرفان غيره . فلم قلتم : إن الأمر ليس كذلك ؟ فإن قالوا : إنا إذا عرفنا أن هذا العالم مفتقر في وجوده إلى إيجاد واجب الوجود . فهذا علم بإضافة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين واجب الوجود ، والعلم بإضافة أمر إلى أمر مشروط بالعلم بالمضافين ، فحال حصول العلم بهذه الإضافة وجب أن يكون العلم بالعلم ، والعلم بواجب الوجود حاضراً . وذلك يقتضي (٣) جواز حصول العلم بواجب الوجود مع العلم بغيره. فنقول : هذا مغالطة محضة ، وذلك لأن العلم بكونه واجب الوجود علم بصفة من صفاته ، وليس هذا علماً بذاته المخصوصة ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يلزم من علمنا بمعنى كونه واجب الوجود ، علمنا بذاته المخصوصة . فزال هذا السؤال .
The second question: We grant that it is valid for the essence of God, exalted, to become known. So why do you say that knowledge of it is valid along with knowledge of other than it? Its explication: We find in ourselves by necessary intuition that when we direct the mind to recall an item of knowledge, we cannot at the same time direct the mind to recall another item of knowledge. And knowledge of that is necessary. Therefore, one who is occupied in thought with a logical question cannot in that moment direct the mind to a geometrical question. And knowledge of that is necessary. This indicates that the combination of two knowledges in the mind at one time is impossible. Or we say that even if that is possible in general, yet here it is not possible, because it is established by induction: that the sensory faculties, when they perceive strong sensations, cannot perceive weak sensations. So if a lamp is placed opposite the disk of the sun, we do not perceive that lamp, and if a mosquito buzzes at the time of thunder, we do not perceive the buzzing of the mosquito. Similarly here: the essence of God, exalted, is the greatest of existents, and therefore it is the greatest of knowledges, and when its knowledge is present in the intellect, the presence of the knowledge of other than it is impossible. So why do you say that the matter is not so? Then if they say: We, when we know that this world is dependent in its existence on the bringing into existence of the Necessary Existent, this is a knowledge of a specific relation between the world and the Necessary Existent, and knowledge of a relation of one thing to another is conditional on knowledge of the two relata. So when knowledge of this relation occurs, it must be that knowledge of the knowledge and knowledge of the Necessary Existent are present. And that requires the permissibility of obtaining knowledge of the Necessary Existent along with knowledge of other than it. So we say: This is pure sophistry, because knowledge that He is the Necessary Existent is knowledge of one of His attributes, and this is not knowledge of His particular essence. And if the matter is so, then our knowledge of the meaning of His being the Necessary Existent does not entail our knowledge of His particular essence. So this question is removed.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the possibility of knowing God's essence along with other things, followed by its refutation. The objector grants that God's essence can be known but argues that it cannot be known simultaneously with other knowledge. The argument is based on psychological experience: we cannot focus on two topics at once. Strong sensations overwhelm weak ones. Similarly, God's essence is the greatest, so when known, it blocks all other knowledge. The author then presents a counterargument from the opponent: knowledge that the world depends on the Necessary Existent involves a relation, which requires knowledge of both relata, thus showing that knowledge of God can coexist with knowledge of the world. The author refutes this by distinguishing between knowledge of God's essence and knowledge of His attributes. Knowing that He is the Necessary Existent is knowledge of an attribute, not the essence itself. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Takeaway
The second objection claims that knowing God's essence blocks all other knowledge, supported by psychological and sensory arguments.

Refutation by Distinguishing Essence from Attribute
The author refutes the second objection by countering a counter-argument and distinguishing attribute from essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the second objection by distinguishing between knowledge of God's essence and knowledge of His attributes, specifically that knowing God as the Necessary Existent is an attribute, not essence knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowing God is the Necessary Existent is an attribute, not His essence—so the objection fails.

After presenting the objection, the author now considers a counter-argument from the opponent and delivers a crisp refutation. The key move is a simple but powerful distinction: knowledge that God is the Necessary Existent is knowledge of one of His attributes, not of His particular essence. Let's see how this dissolves the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-argument
The opponent claims that knowing the world depends on the Necessary Existent involves a relation between two things, requiring knowledge of both, so we must have simultaneous knowledge of God and the world.
2
Identify the author's refutation
The author calls this sophistry and distinguishes: knowing that God is the Necessary Existent is knowledge of an attribute, not the essence itself.
The key distinction: attribute vs. essence.
3
Understand why the refutation works
If we only know an attribute, we don't necessarily know the essence. So the objection's conclusion that we cannot know essence with other things remains intact.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
This shows the importance of precise conceptual distinctions in theological arguments.

Refuting Similarity Arguments about God
Sanusi refutes opponent's mental image argument by parity of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the opponent's claim that mental images of God imply God's essence can accompany other essences, by showing the logic leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Similarity in one aspect does not imply similarity in all aspects.

Sanusi presents the fourth argument against the view that God can be mentally pictured. The opponent argues that if a mental image of God can coexist with other images, then God's essence itself should be able to coexist with other essences. Sanusi turns the tables: he applies the same logic to dependence, forcing the opponent to accept an absurd conclusion—that God would be dependent on a locus and become an accident.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent says: if a mental image of God can accompany other mental images, then God's essence itself must also be able to accompany other essences, because the same rule applies to similar things.
This is the view Sanusi rejects.
2
Identify Sanusi's counter-logic
Sanusi points out that the mental image is dependent on a 'locus' (the mind or the image-bearer). By the same principle of similarity, God's essence would then also need a locus, making it dependent and an accident—which is impossible for God.
3
Recognize the refutation strategy
Sanusi shows that the opponent's own principle leads to a contradiction, so the principle cannot be applied selectively. If the opponent refuses to accept the consequence for God, then his original argument also fails.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : إن حاصل كلامكم هو أنه إذا كانت الصورة العقلية من ذات الله تعالى لا يمتنع عليها مقارنة سائر الصور وجب أن لا يمتنع على ذات الله مقارنة سائر الصور لأن حكم الشيء يجب أن يكون مساوياً لحكم مثله . فنقول : فعلى هذا لما صح في تلك الصورة العقلية أن يكون محتاجاً إلى ذلك المحل ، وجب أن يصح على ذات الله تعالى أن تكون محتاجة إلى ذلك المحل وأن يكون حالا فيه . وإذا لم يلزم من التماثل المذكور هذا ، فكذلك لا يلزم ما ذكرتم .
The fourth aspect: The gist of your statement is that if the mental image of God's essence is not prevented from accompanying other images, then it must be that God's essence is not prevented from accompanying other images, because the ruling of a thing must be equal to the ruling of its like. So we say: Accordingly, since it is valid for that mental image to be in need of that locus, it must be valid for God's essence to be in need of that locus and to be an accident in it. And if this does not follow from the mentioned similarity, then what you mentioned also does not follow.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument. The opponent claims that if God's mental image can coexist with other images, then God's essence can also coexist with other images, by the principle of similarity. The author turns the argument around: if the mental image is dependent on a locus (mahall), then by the same logic God's essence would be dependent and be an accident, which is absurd. Therefore, the opponent's argument fails because the similarity does not hold in a crucial respect.

حكم الشيء

Misconception: If two things are similar in one respect, they are similar in all respects.
Correction: Sanusi shows that similarity (as used by the opponent) is not absolute; the opponent's own principle would lead to an unacceptable consequence, so it cannot be applied selectively.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the opponent by showing that the similarity principle, when applied consistently, leads to an absurd consequence—God being dependent—thus undermining the opponent's argument.

Eighth Objection: Knowledge as a Specific Relation
Objection and answer about knowledge as a relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's mere accompaniment of mental images does not prove He is knowing, because knowledge is a specific relation requiring additional conditions.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not just images; it is a relation with extra conditions.

This passage presents the eighth objection against the argument that God's essence can be accompanied by mental images and thus be knowing. The objector concedes the accompaniment but questions the inference, and the answer clarifies that knowledge is not the mere presence of images but a specific relation that requires further conditions, which are absent here.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection and the counter-answer structure.
The objector starts with a concession: 'we grant that...'
2
Identify the objector's challenge
The objector asks: why does the mere possibility of accompaniment imply that God is knowing? Find the exact question in the text.
3
Analyze the answer's redefinition
The answer states that knowledge is not the mental images themselves but a specific relation (nisba makhsusa) conditioned by the images and other conditions. Highlight this distinction.
The key phrase is 'بل عن نسبة مخصوصة مشروطة'.
4
Connect to the missing conditions
The answer concludes that because those extra conditions are lacking in God's case (according to the opponent's view), knowledge does not follow. Reflect on why this refutes the objection.
The ruling of knowledge is lost due to the loss of those conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثامن : سلمنا أن الذات عند كونها موجودة في خارج الذهن ، يصح عليها أن تقارنها سائر الصور. لكن لم قلتم : إنه يلزم من هذا القدر صحة كون تلك الذات عالمة ؟ وبيانه : أنه لو كان العلم نفس هذه المقارنة ، لكان ما ذكرتم لازماً ، إلا أنا بينا أن بتقدير صحة القول بالصور الذهنية ، فإن الحق أنه ليس العلم عبارة عن نفس تلك الصور الذهنية ، بل عن نسبة مخصوصة مشروطة بحصول تلك الصور الذهنية . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، لم يلزم من حصول هذه الصور، حصول العلم . لاحتمال أنه حصول النسبة المسماة بالعلم ، وكما أنه مشروط بحضور الصورة الذهنية ، فكذلك مشروط بشرائط أخرى ، وتلك الشرائط الأخرى مفقودة ههنا . ففات الحكم بفوات تلك الشرائط
The eighth question: We grant that when the essence is outside the mind, it is valid for it that it be accompanied by other images. But why do you say that from this fact follows the validity of that essence being knowing? The explanation: If knowledge were this very accompaniment, then what you mentioned would be necessary. But we have shown that, assuming the validity of the doctrine of mental images, the truth is that knowledge is not the same as those mental images, but rather a specific relation conditioned by the occurrence of those mental images. If that is the case, then the occurrence of these images does not entail the occurrence of knowledge, because it is possible that the occurrence of the relation called knowledge, just as it is conditioned by the presence of the mental image, is also conditioned by other conditions, and those other conditions are absent here. So the ruling is lost due to the loss of those conditions.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage contains the eighth objection and its answer. The objector concedes that God's essence can accompany mental images but questions why that implies knowledge. The answer explains that knowledge is not merely the presence of images but is a specific relation (nisba makhsusa) conditioned by the images and by other conditions. Since those extra conditions are lacking in the case of God's essence (according to the opponent's view), knowledge does not follow from the mere accompaniment.

شروط
مقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Knowledge is a specific relation (nisba) that requires the images plus other conditions; mere accompaniment is insufficient.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Tenth Objection: From Knowledge of Others to Self-Knowledge
Objection to inference of self-knowledge from knowledge of others

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tenth objection against the claim that God's knowledge of other things necessitates knowledge of His own essence, and the beginning of the reply.

Core Idea
Knowledge of others does not automatically imply self-knowledge.

This passage presents a critical objection to the inference from God's knowledge of creation to knowledge of His own essence. The objector concedes that God knows other things but questions why that must entail self-knowledge. The opponent's reasoning is given, and the reply begins by pointing out a flaw in the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the dialectical structure.
Notice the concession 'سلمنا'.
2
Identify the concession and the question
The objector grants that God knows other things. Find the question that follows and understand what is being challenged.
3
Analyze the opponent's argument
The opponent argues that whoever knows something can know that they know it, and this leads to self-knowledge. Understand the reasoning.
The argument relies on the idea that affirmation requires conception of the two extremes.
4
Examine the first objection
The reply begins by raising a problem: the same reasoning would allow us to judge that a person is human and not a horse. Consider why this undermines the opponent's inference.
The passage cuts off, but this is the first of two aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال العاشر : سلمنا أنه تعالى عالم بغيره. فلم قلتم : إنه يجب أن يكون عالماً بذاته ؟ قوله : لأن كل من علم شيئاً أمكنه أن يعلم كونه عالماً بذلك الشيء . وذلك الشيء . يوجب كونه عالماً بذاته لأن كل تصديق فإنه مسبوق بتصور الطرفين . قلنا : السؤال عليه من وجهين : الأول : إن هذا مشكل بم أنه يمكننا أن نحكم على هذا الشخص بأنه إنسان ، وليس بفرس ؟

His statement: 'Because whoever knows something can know that he is knowing of that thing, and that thing necessitates that he is knowing of his essence, because every affirmation is preceded by a conception of the two extremes.' We said: The objection to it is from two aspects: The first: This is problematic because we can judge that this person is a human and not a horse?
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth objection and the beginning of its answer. The objector challenges the claim that God's knowledge of other things implies knowledge of His own essence. The opponent's reasoning is that knowing something allows one to know that one knows it, and that leads to self-knowledge. The answer begins by pointing out a problem: the same reasoning would allow us to judge that a person is a human and not a horse, but that judgment does not necessarily follow from the premise. The answer is cut off but sets up a refutation of the opponent's inference.

The Ninth Objection: Actuality Requires a Cause
An objection arguing that possibility does not entail actuality; a cause is required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ninth objection argues that possibility alone does not make God actually knowing without a specific cause.

Core Idea
Possibility is not enough; actuality demands a cause.

The ninth objection challenges the earlier claim that God can be knowing. The objector introduces a key principle: that for any attribute to be actual (not just possible), there must be a cause that brings it into existence. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's own reasoning come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like 'muqtaḍā' (cause) and 'qāʾilīyah' (speakership).
2
Identify the main claim
The objector grants that God can be knowing (possibility) but argues that actuality requires a cause. Find the sentence that states 'the ruling is not sufficient'.
The key is the distinction between possibility and actuality.
3
Analyze the cause argument
The objector explains that the cause is either the essence itself or something else. Follow his reasoning: if the essence is not a cause and nothing else is, then the effect is absent.
This is a disjunctive argument: either A or B, both fail, so no cause.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the attribute remains impossible due to the absence of a cause, not due to any inherent impossibility in the attribute itself.
This refines the notion of impossibility: it may be conditional on the lack of a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال التاسع: هب أنه ثبت أنه يصح على ذات الله تعالى كونه عالماً بالأشياء. إلا أن الحكم لا يكفي في حصول كون القائل قائلاً، بل لا بد معه من حصول المقتضى. فهذه الحالة، وإن كانت ممكنة بالنظر إلى القائل إلا أنه لم يوجد المقتضي لذلك، فيبقى على الامتناع لفوات المقتضى، لا لعدم القائلية. وبيان فوات المقتضى: أن المقتضى لحصول تلك القائلية، إما تلك الذات المخصوصة أو غيرها، فإذا لم تكن تلك الذات المخصوصة مقتضية لهذا الأثر ولم يكن غيرها صالحاً لهذا الاقتضاء، فحينئذ قد فات المقتضى، فات الأثر لفقدان المقتضى، وإن كانت القائلية حاصلة.
Ninth Question: Suppose it is established that it is permissible for the essence of God, exalted, to be knowing of things. However, the ruling is not sufficient for the actualization of the speaker's being a speaker; rather, the cause must be present along with it. So this state, even if possible with respect to the speaker, yet the cause has not existed for it, so it remains in impossibility due to the absence of the cause, not due to the absence of speakership. And the explanation of the absence of the cause: that the cause for the actualization of that speakership is either that specific essence or something else. So when that specific essence is not productive for this effect, and nothing else is suitable for this productivity, then the cause has been absent, so the effect is absent due to the loss of the cause, even if speakership were possibly existent.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth objection (السؤال التاسع) against the claim that God's knowledge is actual. The objector argues that even if it is established that God can be knowing (i.e. it is possible for Him), that does not make Him actually knowing. There must be a cause (المقتضى) that brings about the attribute. The objector explains that the cause for God's being a speaker (القائلية) is either His essence or something else. If His essence itself is not a sufficient cause for that effect, and nothing else can cause it, then the effect (the knowledge) does not exist. The objector emphasizes that the impossibility here is due to the absence of a cause, not due to any inherent impossibility in the attribute itself. This objection aims to show that possibility alone does not entail actuality; a cause is necessary.

القائلية
الأثر
الاقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is possible for God, then it is automatically actual.
Correction: The objector shows that possibility does not entail actuality; a cause is necessary to bring the possible into existence.

Checkpoint

B) A cause (al-muqtaḍā)
C) The attribute of existence

The ninth objection establishes that divine attributes, even if possible, require a cause for actuality; without a cause, they remain unrealized due to the absence of a cause, not inherent impossibility.

Tenth Objection: Infinite Regress in God's Knowledge of Self
Objection that knowledge of objects does not necessitate self-knowledge, with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational objection against the necessary knowledge of God's essence and the two supporting arguments focused on perception duality and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Knowledge of something does not automatically entail knowledge of self without absurdity.

This passage presents the tenth objection in a series of challenges to the claim that God must know His essence. The objector argues that even if God knows other things, there is no necessary connection to self-knowledge. Two powerful arguments are presented: one based on the impossibility of a single faculty perceiving both particulars and universals, and the other on the impossibility of an infinite regress of knowledge levels. Understanding this objection is key to grasping the subtleties of divine knowledge in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the passage fully. Note that the objector concedes God knows other things but questions why He must know Himself. The objection cites the principle that knowing something implies knowing that one knows, which is said to necessitate self-knowledge.
2
Analyze the first argument
Focus on the first counterargument (الوجه الأول). It examines a simple judgment 'This person is human' and shows that the subject is particular and the predicate is universal. According to the Master, perceiving particulars requires a corporeal faculty, and universals an immaterial one—so a single faculty cannot do both, challenging the claim.
This argument targets the idea that knowledge of self follows from knowledge of objects.
3
Understand the second argument
The second counterargument (الوجه الثاني) points out that if knowing something requires knowing that one knows, then an infinite chain of levels of knowledge emerges. Since all possible ranks must be actual for the Necessary Being, this leads to infinite simultaneous knowledges and causal chains, which is impossible.
This is the infinite regress argument, a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Connect to the Necessary Being
Consider why the infinite regress is specifically impossible for God: because in the Necessary Being, every possible state must be actual, so the regress would be actualized, leading to absurdity.
This shows the objection is tailored to the nature of wājib al-wujūd.
5
Reflect on the overall objection
Step back and see how both arguments together undermine the claim that knowledge of objects implies self-knowledge. The objector does not deny God's knowledge but questions the necessity of self-knowledge based on these rational difficulties.
This prepares the ground for the answer (not included here).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال العاشر: سلمنا أنه تعالى عالم بغيره. فلم قلتم: إنه يجب أن يكون عالماً بذاته؟ قوله: لأن كل من علم شيئاً أمكنه أن يعلم كونه عالماً بذلك الشيء. وذلك الشيء يوجب كونه عالماً بذاته لأن كل تصديق فإنه مسبوق بتصور الطرفين. قلنا: السؤال عليه من وجهين: الأول: إن هذا مشكل بأنه يمكننا أن نحكم على هذا الشخص بأنه إنسان، وليس بفرس؟ فقولنا: هذا الشخص إنسان قضية. موضوعها: قولنا هذا الشخص ومحمولها: قولنا إنسان. والأول شخصي، والثاني كلي، فلو كان الحاكم على شيء بشيء يجب كونه متصوراً لهما، لزم وجود قوة واحدة تكون مدركة للشخص وللمعنى الكلي. وهذا عند الشيخ محال، لأن مدرك الأشخاص يجب أن يكون جسمانياً، ومدرك الكليات يجب أن يكون مجرداً عند الشيخ. الثاني: إن من علم شيئاً لو وجب أن يعلم كونه عالماً بكونه عالماً. لزم في المرتبة الثالثة أن يعلم كونه عالماً بكونه عالماً به. وإذا كان كل ما يصح في حق واجب الوجود لذاته يجب أن يكون حاصلا بالفعل، لزم أن تكون هذه المراتب التي لا نهاية لها، حاصلة بالفعل في حق واجب الوجود، بحسب علمه بكل واحد من الأشياء، فيلزم منه حصول علوم لا نهاية لها، لا مرة واحدة، بل مراراً لا نهاية لها، ويلزم منه أيضاً حصول علل ومعلولات لا نهاية لها في تلك العلوم دفعة واحدة. وأنه محال. فهذا تمام السؤال على هذه الطريقة. وبالله التوفيق.
Tenth Question: We concede that He, exalted, is knowing of other than Himself. Then why do you say that He must be knowing of His own essence? His saying: Because everyone who knows something can know that he knows that thing. And that thing necessitates his being knowing of his own essence, because every affirmation is preceded by the conceptualization of the two terms. We say: The objection against it is from two aspects: First: This is problematic because we can judge this person as human and not a horse. Our saying: 'This person is human' is a proposition. Its subject is our saying 'this person' and its predicate is our saying 'human'. The first is particular, and the second is universal. So if the judge of something by something must be conceptualizing them both, then there would be a single power that perceives both the particular and the universal meaning. This, according to the Master, is impossible, because the perceiver of particulars must be corporeal, and the perceiver of universals must be immaterial according to the Master. Second: If one who knows something must know that he knows that he knows, then in the third degree he must know that he knows that he knows it. And if everything that is permissible for the Necessary Being in Himself must actually exist, then these infinite degrees must actually exist for the Necessary Being with respect to His knowledge of each thing. This would entail the actualization of infinite knowledges, not once but infinite times, and also entail infinite causes and effects in those knowledges all at once. And that is impossible. So that completes the objection on this method. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth objection (السؤال العاشر) against the claim that God must know His own essence. The objector concedes that God knows other things, but questions why He must know Himself. The objector quotes an argument: if one knows something, one can know that one knows, and that entails knowledge of self because any judgment (تصديق) requires conceptualization (تصور) of the subject and predicate. Then the objector raises two counterarguments. First, by analyzing a simple judgment 'This person is human', he shows that the subject is particular (شخصي) and the predicate is universal (كلي). According to the Master (الشيخ, likely Avicenna), the faculty that perceives particulars is corporeal, while that which perceives universals is immaterial. So a single faculty cannot do both, challenging the assumption that knowledge of self is automatic. Second, if knowing something requires knowing that one knows, this leads to an infinite regress of levels of knowledge (e.g. first-order, second-order, etc.). Since all possible ranks must be actual for the Necessary Being (واجب الوجود), this would entail an infinite number of simultaneous knowledges and causal chains, which is impossible. Thus the objection concludes that the claim leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
شخصي
كلي
واجب الوجود
علل ومعلولات
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that knowledge of an object automatically gives knowledge of one's own knowledge without leading to infinite regress.
Correction: The objection shows this assumption leads either to a problematic unity of perceptual faculties or to an actual infinite regress, both of which are rationally untenable.

Checkpoint

B) It leads to an infinite regress of knowledge
C) The soul cannot perceive universals
D) Knowledge is not an attribute of God

Key Takeaway
The tenth objection uses the infinite regress argument and the duality of perception to challenge the claim that self-knowledge is necessarily implied by knowledge of other things.

?
The objection mentions an infinite regress that would be impossible for God. Have you ever caught yourself assuming that God's knowledge must work exactly like human knowledge? What does this lesson reveal about the limits of human analogy when thinking about the Divine? Commit to one area where you will stop projecting human limitations onto Allah.
?
The passage speaks of a single power needing to perceive both particular and universal. In your own life, when you make judgments (like 'this person is good'), are you aware of how you combine your perception of specific individuals with universal concepts? Third Proof: Soul's Potential and Actual Knowledge
Avicenna's third argument for God's knowledge via soul and fire analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Avicenna's argument from the soul's potential and actual knowledge leads to affirming God's actual knowledge through self-subsistent forms.

Core Idea
If a form makes the soul knowing when indwelling, a self-subsistent form must be actually knowing.

This passage presents a philosophical proof for God's knowledge, drawn from Avicenna's work al-Mabda' wal-Ma'ad. It uses an analogy with the soul's acquisition of knowledge and the heating of fire to argue that the forms of knowledge, if self-subsistent, must be actually knowing. By following this reasoning, we see how the First Principle's knowledge is necessary and actual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the parallel structure: soul:form :: fire:heat.
2
Identify the core analogy
The argument draws an analogy between the soul becoming actual intellect through an abstract form and fire becoming hot through heat. Identify both sides.
The form (صورة) and heat (حرارة) play analogous roles.
3
Understand the conditional reasoning
If the form in the soul makes it potentially knowing, then a self-subsistent form would be more deserving of being actually knowing. The same applies to heat: if self-subsistent, it would be more deserving of being hot.
The conclusion points to God's knowledge as self-subsistent and actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث من الوجوه التي تمسك الشيخ بها في إثبات كونه تعالى عالماً بالمعلومات: أنه قال في كتاب المبدأ والمعاد: إن جوهر النفس قبل حصول الصورة المجردة فيه يكون عاقلاً بالقوة، فإذا حصلت الصورة المجردة فيه، صارت النفس بسببها عاقلة بالفعل. فلما صار جوهر النفس بسبب حلول هذه الصورة فيه عاقلاً بالقوة، فلو قدرنا هذه الصورة جوهراً قائماً بالنفس كان أولى أن تكون عاقلة بالفعل، كما أن الحرارة لما حلت في جوهر النار، صار جوهر النار بسبب حلول تلك السخونة فيها مسخناً. فلو فرضنا نفس تلك الحرارة قائمة بنفسها، وجب أن تكون أولى بكونها مسخنة.
The third way from the arguments that the Master clung to in proving that He, exalted, is knowing of known things: He said in the book 'al-Mabda' wal-Ma'ad': The substance of the soul before the acquisition of the abstract form in it is potential intellect; when the abstract form is acquired in it, the soul becomes actual intellect due to it. Thus, since the substance of the soul becomes potential intellect due to the indwelling of this form in it, if we suppose this form to be a substance subsisting in the soul, it would be more deserving of being actual intellect. Just as when heat indwells in the substance of fire, the substance of fire becomes heating due to the indwelling of that heat in it. So if we suppose that same heat to be self-subsistent, it would necessarily be more deserving of being heating.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the third way (الطريق الثالث) from the arguments of the Master (الشيخ, likely Avicenna) to prove that God is knowing of all known things. It is taken from his work 'al-Mabda' wal-Ma'ad'. The argument uses an analogy with the soul and fire. The substance of the soul (جوهر النفس) in its potential state becomes actually knowing when an abstract form (الصورة المجردة) comes to inhere in it. If that form were itself a self-subsistent substance (جوهر قائم بالنفس), it would be even more fitting to be actually knowing, since it does not depend on a substrate for its existence. Similarly, heat inheres in the substance of fire, making it hot. If heat were self-subsistent (قائمة بنفسها), it would be more deserving of being hot. By analogy, the forms that cause knowledge in the soul, if they are self-subsistent (as in the case of God's knowledge), then the entity that is the form itself must be actually knowing. This argument aims to show that God, who is the pure intellect and the source of all forms, is necessarily actually knowing.

Key Terms

الصورة المجردة
عاقلاً بالقوة
عاقلاً بالفعل
حرارة
جوهر النار
قائماً بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing potential knowledge (in the soul) with actual knowledge; the passage shows that the form itself must be actually knowing if self-subsistent.
Correction: The soul only becomes actually knowing due to an indwelling form. A self-subsistent form, like God's knowledge, is inherently actual and does not need a substrate.

Checkpoint

A) The soul and the body
B) Heat and fire
C) Water and stone
D) Light and shadow

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is actual and self-subsistent, unlike the soul's acquired knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider the fire analogy: heat makes fire hot. Are you, like fire, dependent on something external to be truly knowing? Or are you seeking to connect with the One who is knowledge itself?

The Infinite Regress Objection to Reflexive Knowledge
Second objection that knowledge requires infinite levels of meta-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument of infinite regress refutes the claim that knowledge requires knowing that one knows.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot require knowing that you know—it leads to an impossible infinite chain.

This passage presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum against the idea that knowledge entails reflexive awareness. If knowing something means you must also know that you know it, then an infinite series of higher-order knowledges would be required. For God, who must possess every possible perfection as actual, this would entail an actual infinity of distinct knowledges and causal relations—which is deemed impossible. Thus, the initial assumption must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the logical flow: a conditional premise, a regress, an application to God, and a conclusion of impossibility.
The argument uses a hypothetical 'if... then' structure.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the premise that triggers the regress: 'if someone who knows something must know that he knows it'. This is the assumption being refuted.
This premise is about necessary reflection or self-awareness in knowledge.
3
Trace the infinite regress
The argument says that if knowledge requires knowledge of knowledge, then there is a third level (knowledge of knowing that one knows), and so on ad infinitum. Understand why this regress is problematic.
The regress is not just infinite but 'infinite times'—endlessly repeated for each object of knowledge.
4
Apply the Necessary Existence axiom
The argument then invokes a key principle: everything possible for the Necessary Existence (God) must be actual. Since all those levels of knowledge are possible, they would have to be real in God.
This principle is based on God's perfection—no potentiality in Him.
5
Grasp the impossibility conclusion
Accepting the regress leads to an actual infinity of knowledges and causes/effects, which is considered impossible (muḥāl). Therefore, the original premise is false.
This is a standard reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن من علم شيئاً لو وجب أن يعلم كونه عالماً بكونه عالماً . لزم في المرتبة الثالثة أن يعلم كونه عالماً بكونه عالماً به وإذا كان كل ما يصح في حق واجب الوجود لذاته يجب أن يكون حاصلا بالفعل ، لزم أن تكون هذه المراتب التي لا نهاية لها ، حاصلة بالفعل في حق واجب الوجود ، بحسب علمه بكل واحد من الأشياء ، فيلزم منه حصول علوم لا نهاية لها ، لا مرة واحدة ، بل مراراً لا نهاية لها ويلزم منه أيضاً حصول علل ومعلولات لا نهاية لها في تلك العلوم دفعة واحدة. وأنه محال . فهذا تمام السؤال على هذه الطريقة .
The second: If someone who knows something must know that he knows it (i.e., know his being knowing), then in the third level he must know that he knows that he knows it. And if everything that is possible in relation to the Necessary Existence in itself must be actual, then it necessitates that these infinite levels be actual in relation to the Necessary Existence, according to His knowledge of each one of the things. Thus it entails the occurrence of infinite knowledges, not once, but infinite times. And it also entails the occurrence of infinite causes and effects in those knowledges all at once. And that is impossible. This is the complete objection according to this method.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against a certain view of divine knowledge. The objection argues that if knowledge of something requires knowledge of that knowledge (i.e., reflexive awareness), then an infinite regress follows: for every level of knowledge, there must be a higher-level knowledge. Since everything possible for the Necessary Existence must be actual (because He is perfect), all these infinite levels of knowledge would have to be actually present in God. This would entail an actual infinity of knowledges, and also infinite causes and effects, which is deemed impossible (muḥāl). The conclusion is that the initial assumption (that knowledge requires knowing that one knows) is false. This is a typical reductio ad absurdum argument.

Key Terms
عالم (knower)
مراتب (levels)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existence)
علل ومعلولات (causes and effects)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God has knowledge or self-awareness.
Correction: It refutes a specific view that knowledge requires reflexive meta-knowledge, not knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise that leads to an infinite regress in this argument?
A) That God knows all things.
B) That knowing something requires knowing that one knows it.
C) That the Necessary Existence must have all perfections.
D) That knowledge must be of universals.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress argument refutes the claim that knowledge requires knowing that one knows, because it would lead to an impossible actual infinity in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself trapped in a cycle of needing to confirm your own certainty, always questioning whether you truly know? How does this argument reveal the futility of such endless self-verification? What step can you take today to trust the knowledge Allah has granted you without demanding further proof?
?
If you require a sign that your faith is genuine before acting on it, you might wait forever. How does the infinite regress taught here mirror the way you sometimes overthink your own commitment to Allah? Will you commit to one act of obedience today, not because you feel certain, but because you trust that the knowledge of the truth is already present in your heart?
?
The argument shows that for God, knowledge is simple and direct. How does your tendency to complicate matters of faith—by seeking endless explanations or signs—actually distance you from the trust that He has placed in you? What is one belief you currently doubt that you can simply accept as true today, based on the certainty that Allah does not need to prove Himself to you?

Soul and Form Argument for God's Knowledge
The Shaykh's proof from the soul's potential to actual intellect through forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from the soul's potential and actual intellect as a proof for God's knowledge, and its epistemological limitation to weak conjecture.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is proven through the soul's journey from potential to actual intellect, but only by weak analogy.

This lesson explores the third method from the Shaykh for proving that God is knowing. The argument uses the soul's transformation from potential to actual intellect through abstract forms as a model, then applies the principle of priority to posit God as the most deserving of actual intellect. However, as we will see, this proof is critiqued for yielding only weak conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Read the Source Passages
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overall sense of the argument.
Notice the structure: a premise, an analogy, and a critique.
2
Understand the Soul's Transformation
Focus on how the soul moves from potential intellect (ʿāqil bi-l-quwwa) to actual intellect (ʿāqil bi-l-fiʿl) through receiving an abstract form.
This sets up the key analogy.
3
Analyze the Fire-Heat Analogy
Examine the analogy: just as fire becomes heating due to indwelling heat, the soul becomes actual intellect due to the form; if the form were self-subsistent, it would be more deserving of actual intellect.
This is the core analogical move.
4
Apply the Principle of Priority
Recognize how the argument uses bāb al-awlā (priority and worthiness) to conclude that a self-subsistent form—God—is most deserving of being actual intellect.
5
Consider the Critique
Note the author's own criticism: the proof yields only weak conjecture (ẓann ḍaʿīf) because it relies on analogy, not certainty.
This is important for evaluating the strength of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث من الوجوه التي تمسك الشيخ بها في إثبات كونه تعالى عالماً بالمعلومات : أنه قال في كتاب « المبدأ والمعاد : ( إن جوهر النفس قبل حصول الصورة المجردة فيه يكون عاقلاً بالقوة ، فإذا حصلت الصورة المجردة فيه ، صارت النفس بسببها عاقلة بالفعل . فلما صار جوهر النفس بسبب حلول هذه الصورة فيه عاقلاً بالقوة ، فلو قدرنا هذه الصورة جوهراً قائماً بالنفس كان أولى أن تكون عاقلة بالفعل، كما أن الحرارة لما حلت في جوهر النار ، صار جوهر النار بسبب حلول تلك السخونة فيها مسخناً . فلو فرضنا نفس تلك الحرارة قائمة بنفسها ، وجب أن تكون أولى بكونها مسخنة ) . فهذا حاصل هذه الطريقة إلا أنه طول في تقسيمات هذا الدليل في كتاب المبدأ والمعاد. وهذا الدليل كما نراه مأخوذ من باب الأولى والأخلق ، وأنه لا يفيد إلا الظن الضعيف. فهذا تمام كلمات الناس في إثبات كونه تعالى عالماً .
The third method among the ways that the Shaykh adhered to in proving that He, exalted, is knowing of known things: He said in the book 'al-Mabda' wa'l-Ma'ad': 'The substance of the soul before the occurrence of the abstract form in it is potential intellect. When the abstract form occurs in it, the soul becomes actual intellect by virtue of it. When the substance of the soul becomes potential intellect by virtue of the indwelling of this form in it, if we supposed this form to be a substance subsisting by itself in the soul, it would be more deserving of being actual intellect, just as when heat occurs in the substance of fire, the substance of fire becomes heating by virtue of the indwelling of that heat. If we supposed that same heat to be subsisting by itself, it would be more deserving of being heating.' This is the gist of this method, except that he went into lengthy divisions of this proof in al-Mabda' wa'l-Ma'ad. And this proof, as we see, is taken from the principle of priority and worthiness (bāb al-awla wa'l-akhlāq), and it yields only weak conjecture (ẓann ḍaʿīf). This completes the statements of people in proving that He, exalted, is knowing.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the third method (al-ṭarīq al-thālith) used by the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sīnā or al-Rāzī) to prove God's knowledge. The argument is extracted from Ibn Sīnā's 'al-Mabda' wa'l-Ma'ad'. It begins with the soul: before receiving an abstract form, the soul is potential intellect (ʿāqil bi-l-quwwa). Once the form occurs, it becomes actual intellect (ʿāqil bi-l-fiʿl). The key move: if we imagine that form itself to be a self-subsisting substance (qāʾim bi-l-nafs), it would be more deserving (awlā) of being actual intellect, because the form is the principle of actuality. An analogy is given: fire's substance becomes heating due to heat indwelling in it; if heat were self-subsisting, it would be even more deserving of being heating. Thus, by the principle of priority (bāb al-awlā), a self-subsistent form would be most deserving of actual intellectuality. This is applied to God, positing Him as the self-subsistent intellect. However, the author (the commentator) critiques that this proof only yields weak conjecture (ẓann ḍaʿīf) because it is based on analogy and the principle of priority, not certainty.

Key Terms
جوهر النفس (substance of the soul)
صورة مجردة (abstract form)
عاقل بالقوة (potential intellect)
عاقل بالفعل (actual intellect)
الأولى والأخلق (priority and worthiness)
ظن ضعيف (weak conjecture)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that the soul itself becomes God or that the soul's intellect is the same as God's.
Correction: The argument uses the soul as an analogy to illustrate a principle; it does not equate the soul with God. God is the self-subsistent form, not the soul.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the source of the author's main critique of this proof?
A) It is not from the Quran.
B) It is based on weak analogy and yields only weak conjecture.
C) It is too long.
D) It does not prove God's existence.

Key Takeaway
The soul's potential-to-actual intellect argument for God's knowledge is based on analogy and yields only weak conjecture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to prove God's knowledge to yourself or others, do you rely on analogies like the soul-form argument? How does knowing that even Ibn Sina's proof yields only weak conjecture affect your confidence in such intellectual proofs?
?
If this argument is only probable, does that disturb your certainty about God's attributes? Are you willing to accept that some aspects of the divine may exceed definitive demonstration and require a different kind of conviction?
?
Reflect on a time you presented an argument for a theological belief with full certainty, only to later discover its limitations. How does that experience shape the humility you bring to discussing God's nature?

The Perfection Argument for God's Knowledge
Perfection argument for God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the perfection argument for God's knowledge, which holds that since God is the most perfect being and knowledge is a perfection, He must possess it.

Core Idea
Perfection demands knowledge; absence of knowledge is deficiency.

The first proof presented here argues from God's perfection to His knowledge. The author states that God is the most perfect of all existents, and knowledge is a quality of perfection. Therefore, its absence would be a flaw, which cannot be attributed to God. This argument uses the principle that the perfect being must have all perfections. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice how the argument moves from perfection to knowledge.
2
Identify the key premises
The passage states: God is the most perfect of existents. Knowledge is a perfection. Absence of knowledge is deficiency. From these, the conclusion follows.
3
Understand the logical necessity
The argument uses the term 'fawajaba al-jazm' (one must affirm). This indicates a rational necessity: a perfect being cannot lack a perfection.
This is a typical kalam approach: from attributes of perfection to divine attributes.
4
Reflect on the scope
The passage says God knows 'all known things' and is exalted above ignorance. This argument establishes universal knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الناس من ذكر في إثبات كونه تعالى عالماً وجوهاً أخرى نذكر منها : الأول : إن الباري تعالى أكمل الموجودات ، وصفة العلم صفة كمال وعدمه نقص فوجب الجزم بكونه تعالى موصوفاً بهذه الصفة . وبهذا الطريق تبين كونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات ، تنزيهاً له عن الجهل . والوجه الثاني : وهو أن الواحد منا عالم فهذا العلم إنما حدث من الله تعالى ، إما بواسطة أو بغير واسطة . وكل كمال حصل للمعلول فإن ثبوته للعلة أولى ، فوجب الحكم بكونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات . والله أعلم .
And among people are those who mentioned in proving that He, exalted, is knowing other ways, among which we mention: The first: That the Creator, exalted, is the most perfect of existents, and the attribute of knowledge is an attribute of perfection and its absence is deficiency, so one must affirm that He, exalted, is described by this attribute. And by this way it is shown that He, exalted, is knowing all known things, exalting Him above ignorance. The second way: That each one of us is knowing, and this knowledge only originated from God, exalted, either directly or indirectly. And every perfection that occurs in the effect, its existence in the cause is more fitting (awlā). So one must judge that He, exalted, is knowing all known things. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents two additional proofs for God's knowledge. The first is from perfection (akmaliyya): God is the most perfect of existents; knowledge is a perfection; therefore God must possess it. The absence of knowledge would be deficiency (naqṣ), which is incompatible with God's perfection. This argument is based on the principle that the most perfect being must have all perfections. The second is from causality: human knowledge originates from God, either directly or indirectly. Since the effect (human) has knowledge, the cause (God) must have it even more fittingly (awlā). This is the principle of priority: the perfection found in the effect is more appropriately attributed to the cause. Both arguments aim to establish that God knows all things (bi-jamīʿ al-maʿlūmāt). The passage ends with 'wa-Allahu aʿlam' (And God knows best).

Key Terms
أكمل الموجودات
صفة العلم
كمال
نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof assumes God's existence, but here it is a proof of His attribute of knowledge based on His perfection, not a proof of existence.
Correction: The argument is from God's perfection, which is taken as given. It is not a standalone argument for God's existence, but for His knowledge.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, why must God possess knowledge?
A) Because knowledge is necessary for creation.
B) Because God is the most perfect being and knowledge is a perfection.
C) Because humans have knowledge, so God must have more.
D) Because the Qur'an says God is knowing.

Key Takeaway
The perfection argument deduces God's knowledge from His being the most perfect: since knowledge is a perfection, He must possess it, and His knowledge encompasses all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God knows every single thought you have, even the ones you hide from others, would you still think that thought? What would change in your inner life today if you lived with that certainty?
?
The passage says ignorance is a deficiency. When you choose not to learn about your faith or ignore a command of God, you are acting as if ignorance is acceptable. Are you content with that? Or does God's perfect knowledge demand that you seek knowledge?
?
You claim God is perfect, yet do you live as if He knows everything about you—your motives, your secrets, your failures? If yes, why do you still try to hide from Him? If no, what is the lie you are believing?

The Causality Argument for God's Knowledge
Causality argument for God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the causality argument for God's knowledge, which uses the principle that perfections in the effect are more fittingly attributed to the cause.

Core Idea
Perfections in creation point to a greater perfection in the Creator.

The second proof takes a different approach, starting from human knowledge as an observed fact. Since our knowledge originates from God, who is the cause, and every perfection found in the effect is more worthy of the cause, God must possess knowledge in a superior manner. This argument relies on the principle of causality and the axiom that the cause has a higher degree of perfection. Let's examine the steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the second proof.
Notice the conditional: 'if we have knowledge, it comes from God'.
2
Understand the causal link
The passage states that human knowledge originates from God, either directly or indirectly. This is an assumed premise: all contingent knowledge has a divine source.
This is based on the idea that God is the ultimate creator of everything.
3
Apply the principle of priority
The key principle: every perfection in the effect is more fittingly (awlā) attributed to the cause. If humans have knowledge, then God, as the cause, must have it even more.
This is a classic kalām principle: the perfection of the effect is derived from the cause, so the cause must have it in a superior way.
4
Conclude the argument
Therefore, God must be knowing all things. The argument establishes universal knowledge.

Source Text

Critique of the Priority Argument for God's Knowledge
Author concludes and critiques a proof for God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of a specific proof for God's knowledge and why he judges it to yield only weak conjecture.

Core Idea
Not every proof yields certainty—some only give weak conjecture.

The author concludes his discussion of a particular method for proving God's knowledge. He notes that while one scholar expanded this proof at length, it fundamentally relies on a principle of priority that only produces weak conjecture. This lesson examines that critique and its implications for theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words come through.
Notice the brevity and concluding tone of the passage.
2
Identify the Passage Type
Recognise that this is a concluding and critical passage. The author is not providing a new proof but evaluating an existing one.
3
Understand the Critique
The author says this proof is taken from 'the category of priority and what is more fitting' (bāb al-awla wa al-akhlaq). This is an argument that God must have an attribute because it is more fitting for Him. The author judges that this yields only weak conjecture.
The term 'weak conjecture' (al-ẓann al-ḍaʿīf) is key.
4
Reflect on Certainty in Theology
Consider why the author considers this insufficient. For theological claims, certainty is required. Arguments based on mere fittingness do not provide certainty.
This connects to broader debates about methodology in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا حاصل هذه الطريقة إلا أنه طول في تقسيمات هذا الدليل في كتاب المبدأ والمعاد. وهذا الدليل كما نراه مأخوذ من باب الأولى والأخلق ، وأنه لا يفيد إلا الظن الضعيف. فهذا تمام كلمات الناس في إثبات كونه تعالى عالماً وبالله التوفيق
This is the gist of this method, except that he lengthened the divisions of this proof in the book Al-Mabda' wa Al-Ma'ad. And this proof, as we see it, is taken from the category of priority and what is more fitting, and that it only yields weak conjecture. So this is the entirety of people's words in proving that He, exalted, is knowing. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of a particular method (tariqa) for proving God's knowledge. The author states that the gist of this method is presented, but its proponent (who wrote the book 'Al-Mabda' wa Al-Ma'ad') was overly lengthy in dividing this proof. The author then critiques that proof, noting that it is based on the principle of priority (al-awla wa al-akhlaq), meaning an argument from analogy of what is more fitting. He judges that this proof only yields weak conjecture (al-zann al-da'if), not certainty. Finally, he states that this completes the discussion from people on this matter, closing with 'And with Allah is success.'

Key Terms

الظن الضعيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects all proofs that God is knowing.
Correction: The author only critiques the specific proof from priority as weak, not all proofs for God's knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) It is certain.
B) It yields weak conjecture.
C) It is a definitive proof.
D) It is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The proof from priority (al-awla wa al-akhlaq) yields only weak conjecture, not certainty, and is therefore insufficient for proving God's knowledge.

First Proof: God's Perfection Necessitates Knowledge
Author presents the first proof that God knows based on His perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perfection of God necessitates that He has knowledge, and how this proof establishes that He knows all things.

Core Idea
God's perfection demands perfect knowledge.

In this passage, the author presents a straightforward yet profound argument: since God is the most perfect being, and knowledge is a perfection, God must possess perfect knowledge. This proof not only affirms that God is knowing but also establishes that He knows everything, exonerating Him from any ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the key premises
Notice that the argument rests on two main premises: God is the most perfect existent, and knowledge is an attribute of perfection (its absence is a deficiency).
These are treated as self-evident truths in this proof.
3
Understand the conclusion
From the premises, the author concludes that God must be described with knowledge, and that this knowledge encompasses all known things, purifying Him from ignorance.
4
Reflect on the proof's strength
Consider why this is called a 'way' (wajh) and how it differs from other proofs for God's knowledge. The simplicity makes it intuitive.
The proof relies on the widely accepted premise of God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And by this way it becomes clear that He, exalted, is knowing of all known things, as an exoneration of Him from ignorance.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the first additional proof for God's being knowing (alim). The author introduces it as one of the 'other ways' people have used. The argument is straightforward: God is the most perfect being (akmal al-mawjudat). Knowledge is a perfection (kamal); its absence is a deficiency (naqs). Therefore, God must be necessarily described with knowledge. The author adds that this proof also shows that God knows all things (jami' al-ma'lumat), because the perfection of knowledge implies knowing everything, and it serves to exonerate God from ignorance (jahl).
