The Causal Argument for God's Knowledge
Author presents the second proof using the principle of cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of knowledge in humans leads to the conclusion that God is all-knowing, based on the principle that perfections in effects are more fitting for the cause.

Core Idea
Perfections in effects are more fitting for the cause.

This short but powerful proof starts from something we all experience: our own knowledge. How did we get it? It must come from God. But why does that mean God knows everything? The answer lies in a key causal principle: whatever perfection exists in an effect must exist even more fully in its cause. Let's see how this reasoning works.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation. Notice the logical flow: human knowledge exists → it comes from God → perfections in effects belong more to causes → therefore God is knowing.
2
Understand the key principle
Focus on the sentence: 'every perfection that occurs in the effect, its existence in the cause is more fitting.' This is the backbone of the argument.
This principle is intuitive: the source of something good must have that good in a higher degree.
3
Apply the principle to knowledge
Knowledge is a perfection. Humans have it. Therefore, God (the cause) must have it in a superior way: all-encompassing knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : وهو أن الواحد منا عالم فهذا العلم إنما حدث من الله تعالى ، إما بواسطة أو بغير واسطة . وكل كمال حصل للمعلول فإن ثبوته للعلة أولى ، فوجب الحكم بكونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات . والله أعلم
And the second way: That each one of us is knowing, so this knowledge only originated from Allah the Exalted, either through an intermediary or without an intermediary. And every perfection that occurs in the effect, its existence in the cause is more fitting, so it is necessary to judge that He, exalted, is knowing of all known things. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. It starts from the evident fact that humans possess knowledge. This knowledge must have originated from God, either directly or through intermediaries. The author then invokes a causal principle: any perfection found in an effect is more fitting to be present in its cause. Since human knowledge is a perfection, the cause of that knowledge—God—must possess that perfection in a superior way. Therefore, God must be knowing of all known things. The invocation ends with 'Allah knows best.'

Key Terms
الواحد منا عالم
حدث
الله تعالى
بواسطة أو بغير واسطة
كمال
معلول
علة
أولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof is simply that God created knowledge in us, so He knows what He created.
Correction: The proof actually relies on the principle that perfections in effects are more fitting for the cause, not merely on the fact of creation.

Checkpoint

A) The effect is greater than the cause.
B) Anything in the effect is more fitting in the cause.
C) Ignorance is impossible for God.
D) Knowledge is created.

Key Takeaway
The existence of knowledge in humans proves that God must be all-knowing because perfections in effects are even more fitting for the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If every perfection in you ultimately comes from Allah, what does that say about your own talents? Are you attributing them to yourself or recognizing them as gifts from the All-Knowing?
?
The principle says perfections in the effect are more fitting for the cause. What does that mean for your knowledge? Do you truly believe that Allah's knowledge is infinitely greater than yours? How does that affect your trust in His plan?

Objection and Answer on God Knowing Himself
Objection and answer on God knowing Himself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a philosophical objection to God's self-knowledge and how the response relies on the distinction between human composition and divine simplicity.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge requires composition; God's simplicity prevents it.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the attribute of God knowing Himself. The objector argues that knowledge is a relation requiring two distinct entities, so a single entity cannot know itself. The response cleverly distinguishes between the human self, which has inherent composition allowing relations within itself, and the Divine Essence, which is absolutely simple. Understanding this argument clarifies the importance of divine simplicity in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: Knowledge is a specific relation between knower and known that requires distinction. Therefore, a single thing under one consideration cannot know itself.
The key term here is 'relation' (نسبة) and 'distinction' (التغاير).
2
Identify the anticipated responses
The objectors anticipate two counterarguments: (1) human self-knowledge refutes the objection, and (2) the aspects of being knower and known provide the needed distinction. Note these before reading the answer.
Only the first response is answered in this passage.
3
Analyze the answer to the first response
The answer explains that the human self is not absolutely simple; it has composition, so relations can obtain within it from different aspects. Thus human self-knowledge is possible, but this does not apply to God.
Composition (التركيب) is what makes self-knowledge possible for humans.
4
Apply to God's simplicity
God's essence is free from all composition and absolutely simple. Therefore, relations (including the knowledge relation) cannot occur within it. Hence the objection stands: God cannot know Himself by His essence.
The conclusion is that divine simplicity precludes self-knowledge as understood here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس
المسألة الأولى: من الناس من قال: إن كونه تعالى عالماً بنفسه. واحتج عليه بأن قال: علم الشيء بالشيء نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم، وحصول النسبة مشروط بحصول التغاير، فالشيء الواحد، بالاعتبار الواحد، يمتنع حصول النسبة فيه، فيمتنع كونه عالماً بنفسه. قالوا: وليس لأحد أن يجيب عن هذا من وجهين: الأول: إن هذا يبطل بعلم كل واحد منا بنفسه. والثاني: إن اعتبار كونه عالماً مغاير لاعتبار كونه معلوماً، وهذا القدر من التغاير يكفي في صحة حصول هذه النسبة. لأنا نجيب عن الأول فنقول: إن نفس الواحد منا، ليست فردة منزهة عن جميع جهات التركيب، بل لا بد وأن يحصل فيها جهة من جهات التركيب، بل لا بد وأن يحصل فيها جهة من جهات التركيب والتألف، فلا جرم أمكن حصول الإضافة والنسبة فيها من بعض الوجوه، فلا جرم صح كونه عالماً بنفسه. أما ذات الحق - سبحانه - فإنها منزهة عن جميع جهات التركيب فردة من كل الوجوه، فيمتنع حصول النسب والإضافات فيها، فوجب أن يمتنع فيه كونه عالماً بذاته.
Chapter Five. The first issue: Some people said: He, exalted, is knowing by Himself. They argued by saying: Knowledge of a thing by a thing is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and the occurrence of the relation is conditional upon the occurrence of distinction. So a single thing, under a single consideration, cannot have the relation occur in it, so it cannot be knowing by itself. They said: No one can answer this in two ways: First: This is invalidated by each one of us knowing himself. Second: The consideration of being knowing is different from the consideration of being known, and this amount of distinction suffices for the correctness of the occurrence of this relation. We answer the first by saying: The self of each one of us is not a simple entity free from all aspects of composition; rather, there must occur in it some aspect of composition, rather there must occur in it some aspect of composition and combination. So it is possible for addition and relation to occur in it from some aspects, so it is correct that it knows itself. As for the Essence of the Truth - exalted - it is free from all aspects of composition, simple from all aspects, so the occurrence of relations and additions in it is impossible. So it must be impossible for it to be knowing by its essence.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning whether God knows Himself. The objection is based on a definition: knowledge is a relation between knower and known, and relation requires distinction (taghāyur) between the two relata. Therefore, a single thing under one consideration cannot have that relation; thus God cannot know Himself. The objectors anticipate two possible responses: (1) This is refuted by the fact that each of us knows himself, and (2) The consideration of being knowing is different from being known, providing the needed distinction. The author then answers the first objection by explaining that the human self is not absolutely simple but has some composition (tarkīb), so relations can obtain in it from certain aspects, allowing self-knowledge. However, God's essence is absolutely simple and free from all composition, so relations cannot occur in it. Therefore, God cannot know Himself by His essence. This refutes the objection's premise that self-knowledge is possible for all beings; it is only possible for composite beings like humans.

Key Terms

التغاير
التركيب
فردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human self-knowledge shows that a simple being can know itself.
Correction: Human self-knowledge is possible only because the human self has composition; God's absolute simplicity prevents such relational knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Because knowledge requires composition.
B) Because knowledge requires a relation between distinct things.
C) Because the self is too simple.
D) Because knowledge is impossible.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute simplicity prevents the relational structure required for self-knowledge in the same way humans have it.

The Objection to Divine Self-Knowledge and Its First Answer
Objection to divine self-knowledge and refutation of first answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God cannot know Himself due to simplicity, and why the first attempted refutation by analogy with human self-knowledge fails because the human self is composite.

Core Idea
Knowing requires distinction; God's absolute simplicity blocks self-knowledge.

This passage presents a philosophical challenge to divine self-knowledge: if knowledge requires a relation between distinct things, how can a simple God know Himself? The author first entertains two potential answers, then refutes the first by distinguishing human composite nature from God's absolute simplicity. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines: knowledge is a relation requiring distinction. A single thing in one respect cannot have a relation to itself, so God cannot know Himself.
This is the core challenge of the passage.
2
Identify the two proposed answers
The opponents list two ways someone might try to answer this objection: (1) human self-knowledge refutes it, (2) conceptual distinction between knower and known provides the needed duality.
Note that these are not the author's own answers yet.
3
Follow the author's response to the first answer
The author argues that the human self is composite (has aspects), so relations can occur within it. But God is absolutely simple, so the objection stands for Him.
This distinguishes God from humans.
4
Reflect on the implication
The first attempted answer fails because it overlooks the difference between composite and simple beings. The objection remains valid for God.
The author does not address the second answer here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمسألة الأولى : من الناس من قال : إن كونه تعالى عالماً بنفسه . واحتج عليه بأن قال : علم الشيء بالشيء نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم ، وحصول النسبة مشروط بحصول التغاير ، فالشيء الواحد ، بالاعتبار الواحد ، يمتنع حصول النسبة فيه ، فيمتنع كونه عالماً بنفسه . قالوا : وليس لأحد أن يجيب عن هذا من وجوه : الأول : إن هذا يبطل بعلم كل واحد منا بنفسه . والثاني : إن اعتبار كونه عالماً مغايراً لاعتبار كونه معلوماً ، وهذا القدر من التغاير يكفي في صحة حصول هذه النسبة . لأنا نجيب عن الأول فنقول : إن نفس الواحد منا ، ليست فردة منزهة عن جميع جهات التركيب، بل لا بد وأن يحصل فيها جهة من جهات التركيب ، بل لا بد وأن يحصل فيها جهة من جهات التركيب والتألف ، فلا جرم أمكن حصر الإضافة والنسبة فيها من بعض الوجوه ، فلا جرم صح كونه عالماً بنفسه . فأما ذات الحق - سبحانه - ف إنها منزهة عن جميع جهات التركيب فردة من كل الوجوه ، فيمتنع حصول النسب والإضافات فيها ، فوجب أن يمتنع فيه كونه عالماً بذاته
Then the first issue: among people, some said that His being, exalted, knowing by Himself. And they argued by saying: knowledge of a thing by a thing is a specific relation (nisba makhsūṣa) between the knower and the known, and the occurrence of the relation is conditional upon the occurrence of distinction (taghāyur). So a single thing, in a single respect, the occurrence of the relation in it is impossible, so it is impossible for it to be knowing by itself. They said: And no one can answer this in several ways: the first: that this is refuted by the knowledge of each one of us of himself. And the second: that the consideration of his being knower is different from the consideration of his being known, and this amount of distinction suffices for the soundness of the occurrence of this relation. But we answer the first by saying: indeed the self of each one of us is not a simple entity free from all aspects of composition; rather, it must have some aspect of composition, rather it must have some aspect of composition and combination. So without doubt, it is possible for relation and attribution to occur in it in some respects, so without doubt, it is sound for it to be knowing by itself. As for the Essence of the Truth—glorified be He—it is free from all aspects of composition, simple in every respect, so the occurrence of relations and attributions in it is impossible, so it must be impossible for Him to be knowing by Himself.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against God's self-knowledge and the author's first refutation. The objection argues that knowledge requires a relation between a knower and a known, and relation requires a distinction between the two. Since God is one, no such distinction can exist, so He cannot know Himself. The opponents then offer two possible answers to this objection: first, that it is refuted by our own self-knowledge (since we are one yet know ourselves), and second, that the conceptual distinction between being knower and being known provides enough duality. The author responds to the first answer by distinguishing the human self from God's Essence. The human self is not absolutely simple; it has composition of parts or aspects, which allows relations to occur. But God is absolutely simple, free from all composition, so relations are impossible. Therefore, the objection stands against God but not against humans. The author concludes that God cannot know Himself by His essence according to this first answer's implication.

Correction: The author shows that the human self is composite, allowing internal relations, whereas God is absolutely simple, preventing such relations. Thus the objection applies differently.

Circularity in the Distinction of Being Knower and Known
Sanusi refutes the second attempted solution by exposing circular priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why using a conceptual distinction between 'knower' and 'known' to explain God's self-knowledge leads to circularity and is therefore invalid.

Core Idea
A conceptual distinction alone cannot ground Divine self-knowledge without circularity.

In this passage, the author refutes the second attempt to answer the original objection about God knowing Himself. The attempt claimed that a conceptual distinction between being knower and being known provides the necessary duality for knowledge. The author shows that this leads to a vicious circle, because the distinction itself depends on knowledge. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premises
Read the passage and note that knowledge is defined as a specific relation, and relations require a prior distinction between the relata.
This is a key principle in kalam: no relation without duality.
2
Identify the proposed distinction
The second attempt uses the conceptual distinction between the meaning of 'knower' and the meaning of 'known' as the required duality.
This distinction is only conceptual, not real.
3
Follow the logical consequence
If that conceptual distinction is the necessary distinction, it must be prior in rank to knowledge. But knowledge itself is what makes the essence become 'knower' and 'known', so knowledge is prior to those concepts.
Thus we have two conflicting priority claims.
4
Realize the circularity
The distinction is prior to knowledge, yet knowledge is prior to the distinction. This is a vicious circle (dawr), which is impossible.
Circular priority is logically unacceptable.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since the second attempt leads to an impossibility, it fails. The objection remains unanswered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإجابة عن السؤال الثاني : فنقول : العلم نسبة مخصوصة ، وحصول النسب مشروط بحصول المغايرة ، فلو كانت تلك المغايرة هي المغايرة الحاصلة بين المفهوم من كونه عالماً ، وبين المفهوم من كونه معلوماً ، فحين تكون المغايرة الحاصلة بهذا الاعتبار متقدمة بالرتبة على حصول العلم، لكن حصول العلم لتلك الذات متقدم بالرتبة على صيرورتها عالمة ومعلومة ، فيلزم وقوع الدور ، وهو محال . فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الشبهة
As for the answer to the second question, we say: knowledge is a specific relation, and the occurrence of relations is conditional upon the occurrence of distinction. So if that distinction were the distinction obtained between the concept of His being knower and the concept of His being known, then when the distinction obtained in this respect is prior in rank to the occurrence of knowledge, but the occurrence of knowledge for that essence is prior in rank to its becoming knower and known, then circularity (dawr) would follow, and it is impossible. So this is the complete statement in establishing this doubt.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage answers the second attempted refutation of the original objection. The second attempt argued that the conceptual distinction between being knower and being known provides enough duality for the relation of knowledge. The author refutes this by showing that such a distinction leads to circularity. Knowledge is a relation requiring prior distinction. If we use the conceptual distinction between the concepts of 'knower' and 'known' as the required distinction, then that distinction would have to be prior to the knowledge. However, the knowledge itself is what makes the being become a knower and known. So the knowledge is prior to the state of being knower and known. Therefore, if the distinction is prior to knowledge, but knowledge is prior to the distinction (since the distinction arises from the concepts that depend on knowledge), we have a circular priority. This is impossible. Thus the second answer fails.

Key Terms
مغايرة
دور
رتبة
نسبة مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a conceptual distinction alone suffices for God's self-knowledge without requiring a real distinction.
Correction: The author shows that even a conceptual distinction leads to circularity because the concepts themselves depend on knowledge, making the distinction posterior to knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Circularity
B) Infinite regress
C) Contradiction
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The second attempt fails because it results in a vicious circle: the conceptual distinction must be prior to knowledge, yet knowledge is prior to that distinction.

Refuting the Doubt: Distinction Between Quiddity and Determination
Answering the doubt by distinguishing between quiddity and determination in God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the doubt about the lack of distinction in God's essence by distinguishing between quiddity and determination, and why this distinction suffices for knowledge to occur.

Core Idea
In God's essence, quiddity and determination provide the conceptual distinction needed for relations like knowledge.

We encounter a doubt: How can God know Himself if His essence has no internal distinctions? The author answers that God's essence is specific (muʿayyana), and in any specific essence we can conceptually distinguish between its quiddity (whatness) and its determination (individuation). This distinction, though not a physical separation, is enough to ground relations like knowledge. Let's see the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and the literal translation together. Notice how the answer is structured: first restating the doubt, then presenting the solution.
2
Identify the three aspects
In the answer, the author says that for any specific essence, there is a quiddity, a determination, and the totality of that determination. Underline these terms in the Arabic.
The three aspects are: ماهية, تعيين, and مجموع ذلك التعيين.
3
Understand the sufficiency
Reflect on why the author claims this distinction is sufficient for relations (nisab and idāfāt). Consider that it is a conceptual distinction within the same essence, not a real separation, preserving divine simplicity.
The distinction is not a division of parts but a mental analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة التي ذكروها . فالجواب عنها : أنا بينا أن ذات الله تعالى ذات معينة، وكل ما كان كذلك فهناك ماهية ، وهناك تعيين . وهناك مجموع ذلك التعيين ، فقد حصلت المغايرة من هذا الوجه ، وهي كافية في إمكان حصول النسب والإضافات لتلك الذات والماهية
As for the doubt (shubha) that they mentioned, the answer to it: we explained that the essence of God, exalted, is a specific essence (dhāt muʿayyana), and everything that is such, there is a quiddity (māhiyya) and there is determination (taʿyīn). And there is the totality of that determination, so distinction has occurred in this respect, and it is sufficient for the possibility of the occurrence of relations and attributions to that essence and quiddity.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage provides the final refutation of the original objection that God cannot know Himself because He lacks distinction. The author argues that God's essence is a specific, determinate essence (dhāt muʿayyana). In any determinate thing, one can distinguish three aspects: the quiddity (whatness, māhiyya), the determination (the individuation that makes it this specific thing, taʿyīn), and the combination of the two. This yields a genuine distinction—a difference among these aspects—which is sufficient to ground relations such as knowledge. Thus, even though God is simple in the sense of lacking composition of parts, the distinction between quiddity and determination within the same essence allows for the relation of knowledge to occur. Therefore, the objection that no distinction exists in God is false; there is a conceptual distinction that suffices.

Key Terms
شبهة
ماهية
تعيين
مغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's essence has no internal distinction at all, making knowledge impossible.
Correction: The author shows that a conceptual distinction between quiddity and determination exists within God's essence, providing a basis for relations like knowledge without compromising simplicity.

Checkpoint

A) By positing a distinction between quiddity and determination
B) By denying the objection entirely
C) By using temporal priority
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The conceptual distinction between quiddity and determination in God's essence provides a sufficient basis for the possibility of relations like divine self-knowledge, without compromising divine simplicity.

Are you willing to hold both absolute simplicity and real self-knowledge, or do you secretly reduce one to fit your comfort?
?

But God's self-knowledge rests only on a conceptual distinction. What does that silence demand from your intellect? Can you bow before a Truth your mind cannot fully grasp, or do you insist that He conform to your categories?

The Unity of Intellect, Intelligent, and Intelligible in God
Ibn Sina's doctrine that in God, intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that in God, the intellect, the intelligent, and the intelligible are identical due to His absolute simplicity.

Core Idea
In God, knowing, knower, and known are one and the same.

Ibn Sina presents a profound doctrine about divine self-knowledge: in God, the act of intellection, the subject, and the object are not three distinct things but one simple reality. This passage explains why that must be so, using the concepts of 'aql, 'aqil, and ma'qul. Let's unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the technical terms 'aql, 'aqil, ma'qul.
2
Understand the general principle
The passage begins with a general statement: any essence that intellects itself is both intellecting (active) and intellected (passive) with respect to itself. Grasp this principle first.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's specific argument
Ibn Sina answers the question posed: in God, intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are one. His reasoning is based on God being a simple substance. Identify the three aspects and see how they are identical.
The three aspects are: as a simple quiddity (intellect), as having a quiddity (intellected), and as apprehending that quiddity (intelligent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : اعلم أن كل ذات عقلت نفسها ، فتلك الذات عاقلة وهي بعينها أيضاً معقولة ، فحصولها بنفسها لنفسها . هل هو عين نفسها أم لا ؟ قال الشيخ الرئيس : إنه تعالى عقل وعاقل ومعقول ، والكل شيء واحد . وذلك لأنه ذات مجردة ، فيما هو ماهية مجردة، فهو عقل، وبما أنه حصل له ماهية، فهو معقول، وبما أنه حصلت له ماهية، فهو عاقل . فالعقل والعاقل والمعقول شيء واحد
The second issue: know that every essence that has intellected itself, that essence is intellecting (ʿāqila) and it itself is also intellected (maʿqūla). So its occurrence by itself for itself: is it identical to itself or not? The Chief (al-Shaykh al-Raʾīs) said: He, exalted, is intellect (ʿaql), intellecting (ʿāqil), and intellected (maʿqūl), and all are one thing. And that is because He is a simple substance (dhāt mujarrada). Inasmuch as it is a simple quiddity, it is intellect; and inasmuch as it has a quiddity, it is intellected; and inasmuch as it has obtained a quiddity, it is intellecting. So intellect, intellecting, and intellected are one thing.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage introduces the second issue concerning God's self-intellection. It begins with a general principle: any essence that intellects itself is both intellecting (active) and intellected (passive) with respect to itself. The question is whether the occurrence of itself by itself for itself (i.e., its self-intellection) is identical to its essence or not. The author cites Ibn Sina (the Chief, al-Shaykh al-Raʾīs) who answers that in God, intellect (ʿaql), intellecting (ʿāqil), and intellected (maʿqūl) are one single thing. The explanation is that God is a simple substance. In terms of its being a simple quiddity, it is intellect (the faculty or form of knowing). In terms of its having a quiddity (i.e., being an object of thought), it is intellected (the object). And in terms of its apprehending that quiddity, it is intellecting (the subject). Since all three aspects pertain to the same simple reality, they are identical. This doctrine emphasizes God's absolute simplicity in the act of self-knowledge.

عقل
عاقل
معقول
شيخ الرئيس

Correction: For Ibn Sina, they are one because God is absolutely simple and His self-intellection is identical to His essence.

But your heart often strays, your actions are distracted, and your intention is mixed. Will you repent from this fragmentation right now and ask Allah to grant you a glimpse of the oneness of which this lesson speaks?

The Necessity of Distinction for Divine Knowledge and Two Proofs
Explains the necessity of distinction for knowledge and gives two proofs that God knows by His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge requires a real distinction between knower and known, how conceptual distinction leads to circularity, the two rational proofs that God knows by His essence, and how the doubt is resolved through the composite of quiddity and determination.

Core Idea
God's knowledge requires a real distinction within His determined essence.

In responding to a second question, the author presents a sophisticated argument about the nature of knowledge and its conditions. He first shows that a merely conceptual distinction leads to circularity, then offers two proofs that God knows by His essence, and finally resolves the doubt by pointing to the real distinction within God's determined essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of its flow. Note the key terms like العلم, المغايرة, الدور.
The passage is dense; don't worry about understanding everything immediately.
2
Follow the argument about circularity
The author first argues that if the distinction were conceptual, circularity would follow. Trace why circularity is impossible.
This sets up the need for a real distinction.
3
Understand the two proofs
The author offers two proofs: (1) God is a simple substance independent of matter, so His essence is present to itself; (2) Anyone who knows something can know that he knows, requiring self-knowledge.
These are rational arguments for God's knowledge.
4
See the resolution of the doubt
The answer: God's essence is determined, having a quiddity and determination. Their composite provides a real distinction, allowing relations without circularity.
This resolves the doubt and completes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإجابة عن السؤال الثاني : فنقول : العلم نسبة مخصوصة، وحصول النسب مشروط بحصول المغايرة، فلو كانت تلك المغايرة هي المغايرة الحاصلة بين المفهوم من كونه عالماً، وبين المفهوم من كونه معلومات، فحين نكون المغايرة الحاصلة بهذا الاعتبار متقدمة بالرتبة على حصول العلم، لكن حصول العلم لتلك الذات متقدم بالرتبة على صيرورتها عالمة ومعلومة، فيلزم وقوع الدور، وهو محال. فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الشبهة ونقول : الدليل على كونه تعالى عالماً بذاته المخصوصة وجهان : الأول : ما ذكرناه في الطريق الأول أنه جوهر مجرد غني عن المادة، وكل ما كان كذلك فإن ذاته حاضرة لذاته، وكل ما كان كذلك فإنه يكون عالماً بذاته. وتمام الكلام في تقرير هذا المعنى قد سبق. الوجه الثاني : أن كل من علم شيئاً فإنه يمكنه أن يعلم كونه عالماً وذلك مشروط بكونه عالماً بنفسه على ما بيناه. وأما الشبهة التي ذكروها . فالجواب عنها : أنا بينا أن ذات الله تعالى ذات معينة، وكل ما كان كذلك فهناك ماهية، وهناك تعيين. وهناك مجموع تلك الماهية والتعيين، فقد حصلت المغايرة من هذا الوجه، وهي كافية في إمكان حصول النسب والإضافات لتلك الذات.
As for the answer to the second question, we say: Knowledge is a specific relation, and the occurrence of relations is conditional upon the existence of distinction. If that distinction were the distinction that occurs between the concept of being knowing and the concept of being known, then the distinction occurring in this respect would be prior in rank to the occurrence of knowledge, but the occurrence of knowledge for that essence is prior in rank to its becoming knowing and known, so circularity would follow, and it is impossible. This is the complete exposition of this doubt. And we say: The proof that He, exalted, is knowing by His specific essence has two aspects: The first: what we mentioned in the first way, that He is a simple substance independent of matter, and everything that is so, its essence is present to itself, and everything that is so, it is knowing by its essence. The complete exposition of this meaning has preceded. The second aspect: That whoever knows something, he can know that he is knowing, and that is conditional upon his being knowing by himself, as we have shown. As for the doubt that they mentioned, the answer to it is: We have shown that the essence of God, exalted, is a determined essence, and everything that is so, there is a quiddity, and there is a determination, and there is the composite of that quiddity and that determination. So distinction has occurred from this aspect, and it is sufficient for the possibility of the occurrence of relations and additions for that essence.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative exposition answering a second question about God's knowledge. The author first states that knowledge is a relation requiring a distinction (maghayira) between the knower and known. If the distinction were merely between the concepts of being knowing and being known, then that conceptual distinction would be prior to knowledge itself, yet knowledge is prior to the actualization of being knowing—leading to a circularity (dawr), which is impossible. This sets up a doubt (shubha). Then the author gives two proofs that God knows by His essence: first, because He is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) independent of matter, and such an essence is present to itself, hence knowing; second, because anyone who knows something can know that he knows, which requires being knowing by oneself. Finally, the doubt is answered: God's essence is a determined essence, comprising a quiddity (mahiyya) and a determination (ta'ayyun). Their composite provides a real distinction, sufficient for relations and attributes to apply, thus avoiding circularity.

Key Terms
العلم
النسبة
المغايرة
الدور
الجوهر المجرد
الماهية
التعيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A merely conceptual distinction is enough for knowledge to occur.
Correction: The distinction must be real, as shown by the impossibility of circularity. God's determined essence provides a real distinction via the composite of quiddity and determination.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that if the distinction were between the concept of being knowing and the concept of being known, circularity would result?
A) Because the distinction would be prior to knowledge, but knowledge is prior to being knowing.
B) Because the concepts are identical.
C) Because God's essence is simple and cannot have distinctions.
D) Because knowledge is a negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge requires a real distinction within His essence, which is provided by the composite of His quiddity and determination.

?
The lesson insists on a real distinction within God's essence for knowledge to be possible. In your own life, is there a real distinction between your professed belief and your actions, or are they merely conceptually aligned? What one concrete change can you make this week to bridge that gap?

Ibn Sina's Thesis: God is Intellect, Intelligent, and Intelligible as One
Ibn Sina's argument that God is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible identically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that God is simultaneously intellect, intelligent, and intelligible without multiplicity.

Core Idea
In God, intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are identical because of His absolute simplicity.

Ibn Sina presents a bold thesis: in God, the acts of intellection, being intelligent, and being intelligible are all one reality. He grounds this in God's nature as a simple substance (dhāt mujarrada). Before examining objections, we must grasp the reasoning behind this identity claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claim.
2
Identify the three aspects
Notice how Ibn Sina derives intellect, intelligible, and intelligent from the simple quiddity.
3
Ponder the question
Ask why the identity of these three is controversial—why might someone object?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : اعلم أن كل ذات عقلت نفسها، فتلك الذات عاقلة وهي بعينها أيضاً معقولة، فحصولها بنفسها لنفسها هل هو عين نفسها أم لا ؟ قال الشيخ الرئيس : إنه تعالى عقل وعاقل ومعقول، والكل شيء واحد. وذلك لأنه ذات مجردة، فيما أنه ماهية مجردة، فهو عقل، وبما أنه حصل لماهية فهو معقول، وبما أنه حصلت له ماهية فهو عاقل. فالعقل والعاقل والمعقول واحد. ولقائل يقول : السؤال عليه من وجوه : الأول : إن لفظ العقل والعاقل والمعقول إما أن تكون ألفاظاً مترادفة أو متباينة . فإن كان الأول لم يكن في ذكرها فائدة معنوية، وإن كان الثاني فهي إما أن تكون مفهومات سلبية أو ثبوتية. والأول باطل لأنا دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك ليس مفهوماً سلبياً، فبقي الثاني، ولا شك أنها ليست مفهومات متباينة بل هي صفات لهذه الذات، وحينئذ يبطل قوله : إن هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة لا تفيد كثرة في المعنى . الوجه الثاني : وهو أنا قد دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك معنى نسبي إضافي، فيمتنع كونه عين تلك الذات المخصوصة . الوجه الثالث : إن العقل يتفرع على حصول التعقل، وكونه معقولاً لنفسه، يتفرع على كونه عاقلاً لنفسه، فهذه المفهومات الثلاثة يتفرع بعضها على البعض، فيمتنع كونها مفهوماً واحداً . والله أعلم.
The second issue: Know that every essence that intellects itself, that essence is intelligent and it itself is also intelligible. So its occurrence to itself by itself – is it identical to itself or not? The Chief Master (Ibn Sina) said: He, exalted, is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible, and all are one thing. That is because He is a simple substance; insofar as He is a simple quiddity, He is intellect; insofar as the quiddity is attained, He is intelligible; insofar as the quiddity is attained for Him, He is intelligent. So intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are one. And one may object to him from [several] aspects: The first: The terms intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are either synonymous or distinct. If the first, then there is no meaningful benefit in mentioning them; if the second, then they are either negative or positive concepts. The first is false because we have shown that the meaning of awareness and perception is not a negative concept, so the second remains, and there is no doubt that they are not distinct concepts but rather attributes of this essence, and then his statement that these three terms do not indicate multiplicity in meaning becomes false. The second aspect: And that we have shown that the meaning of awareness and perception is a relative, relational meaning, so it is impossible for it to be identical to that specific essence. The third aspect: Intellect is derived from the occurrence of intellection, and its being intelligible to itself is derived from its being intelligent to itself, so these three concepts are derived from one another, so it is impossible for them to be a single concept. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents a second issue (mas'ala) concerning the identity of intellect (‛aql), intelligent (‛āqil), and intelligible (ma‛qūl) in God. The author first states a principle: any essence that intellects itself is both intelligent and intelligible; the question is whether the relation of the essence to itself is identical to the essence or not. Ibn Sina (ash-Shaykh ar-Ra'īs) holds that for God, all three are one because He is a simple substance (dhāt mujarrada): as a quiddity (māhiyya) considered in itself, He is intellect; as that quiddity is attained (ḥaṣala), He is intelligible; and as the quiddity is attained for Him, He is intelligent. Then the author raises three objections. The first objection: the terms are either synonymous (mutarādifa) or distinct (mutabāyina). If synonymous, no benefit; if distinct, they are either negative (salbī) or positive (thubūtī). Since awareness is not negative, they are positive. Yet they are not distinct concepts but attributes of the same essence, which refutes Ibn Sina's claim that they indicate no multiplicity. The second objection: awareness and perception are relative relational concepts (nisbī iḍāfī), so they cannot be identical to the essence itself. The third objection: each concept is derived from another (e.g., intellect derives from intellection), so they are distinct by logical priority and posteriority, preventing them from being one concept.

Key Terms
العقل
العاقل
المعقول
الذات المجردة
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina in this passage, what makes God intelligible?
A) His being a simple substance
B) His quiddity being attained
C) His being intelligent
D) His being intellect

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina identifies intellect, intelligent, and intelligible in God because all arise from the single, simple quiddity without real distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does your current understanding of God's attributes—like His knowledge or power—compare to Ibn Sina's claim that they are identical to His essence? Do you truly live as if God's essence is simple, or do you unintentionally compartmentalize His attributes?
?
Ibn Sina argues that God's self-intellection is not a separate act but His very essence. What does this imply for your own acts of worship? Can you offer them as an expression of your innermost being, or are they merely external actions?
?
If you accept that in God, intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are one, how does that challenge the way you seek to know Him? Are you content with a fragmented knowledge, or will you strive for a unified vision of His perfection?

First Objection: Synonymous or Distinct Terms?
The first objection to Ibn Sina's identity thesis using logical characterization of terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against Ibn Sina's identity thesis, which argues that the three terms must be either synonyms or distinct attributes, and that they cannot be identical in meaning.

Core Idea
If the three terms are not synonyms, they imply multiplicity, contradicting Ibn Sina's claim of unity.

The first objection attacks Ibn Sina's claim directly: the terms intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are either synonymous or distinct. If they are synonyms, there is no benefit in mentioning them; if they are distinct, they must be either negative or positive concepts. Since awareness is positive, they are positive attributes of the essence, which entails multiple attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the first objection and its translation.
2
Understand the dichotomy
Follow the argument: if synonymous, no benefit; if distinct, then either negative or positive.
3
Note the conclusion
The author concludes the terms are distinct positive attributes, so they indicate multiplicity.

Source Text

The second issue: Know that every essence that intellects itself, that essence is intelligent and it itself is also intelligible. So its occurrence to itself by itself – is it identical to itself or not? The Chief Master (Ibn Sina) said: He, exalted, is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible, and all are one thing. That is because He is a simple substance; insofar as He is a simple quiddity, He is intellect; insofar as the quiddity is attained, He is intelligible; insofar as the quiddity is attained for Him, He is intelligent. So intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are one. And one may object to him from [several] aspects: The first: The terms intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are either synonymous or distinct. If the first, then there is no meaningful benefit in mentioning them; if the second, then they are either negative or positive concepts. The first is false because we have shown that the meaning of awareness and perception is not a negative concept, so the second remains, and there is no doubt that they are not distinct concepts but rather attributes of this essence, and then his statement that these three terms do not indicate multiplicity in meaning becomes false. The second aspect: And that we have shown that the meaning of awareness and perception is a relative, relational meaning, so it is impossible for it to be identical to that specific essence. The third aspect: Intellect is derived from the occurrence of intellection, and its being intelligible to itself is derived from its being intelligent to itself, so these three concepts are derived from one another, so it is impossible for them to be a single concept. And God knows best.

Commentary
This passage presents a second issue (mas'ala) concerning the identity of intellect (‛aql), intelligent (‛āqil), and intelligible (ma‛qūl) in God. The author first states a principle: any essence that intellects itself is both intelligent and intelligible; the question is whether the relation of the essence to itself is identical to the essence or not. Ibn Sina (ash-Shaykh ar-Ra'īs) holds that for God, all three are one because He is a simple substance (dhāt mujarrada): as a quiddity (māhiyya) considered in itself, He is intellect; as that quiddity is attained (ḥaṣala), He is intelligible; and as the quiddity is attained for Him, He is intelligent. The third objection: each concept is derived from another (e.g., intellect derives from intellection), so they are distinct by logical priority and posteriority, preventing them from being one concept.

?

Second and Third Objections: Relational and Derivational Problems
Two objections: one based on the relational nature of awareness, another on the logical priority of concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two further objections: that awareness is relational and thus cannot be identical to the essence, and that the three concepts have logical priority relations making them distinct.

Core Idea
Relational and derivational arguments show that intellect, intelligent, and intelligible cannot be identical because they involve relations and logical dependence.

The second objection argues that awareness (shu‛ūr) is a relative concept (nisbī iḍāfī), so it cannot be identical to the essence itself. The third objection points out that the three concepts are derived from one another: intellect depends on intellection, and each concept presupposes another, making them distinct in order of priority.

Guided Path
1
Read both objections
Read the second and third objections and their translation.
2
Understand the relational argument
The second objection says relational meanings cannot be identical to the essence because they involve a relation.
3
Understand the priority argument
The third objection shows a chain of derivation, indicating distinctness.

المسألة الثانية : اعلم أن كل ذات عقلت نفسها، فتلك الذات عاقلة وهي بعينها أيضاً معقولة، فحصولها بنفسها لنفسها هل هو عين نفسها أم لا ؟ قال الشيخ الرئيس : إنه تعالى عقل وعاقل ومعقول، والكل شيء واحد. وذلك لأنه ذات مجردة، فيما أنه ماهية مجردة، فهو عقل، وبما أنه حصل لماهية فهو معقول، وبما أنه حصلت له ماهية فهو عاقل. فالعقل والعاقل والمعقول واحد. ولقائل يقول : السؤال عليه من وجوه : الأول : إن لفظ العقل والعاقل والمعقول إما أن تكون ألفاظاً مترادفة أو متباينة . فإن كان الأول لم يكن في ذكرها فائدة معنوية، وإن كان الثاني فهي إما أن تكون مفهومات سلبية أو ثبوتية. والأول باطل لأنا دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك ليس مفهوماً سلبياً، فبقي الثاني، ولا شك أنها ليست مفهومات متباينة بل هي صفات لهذه الذات، وحينئذ يبطل قوله : إن هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة لا تفيد كثرة في المعنى . الوجه الثاني : وهو أنا قد دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك معنى نسبي إضافي، فيمتنع كونه عين تلك الذات المخصوصة . الوجه الثالث : إن العقل يتفرع على حصول التعقل، وكونه معقولاً لنفسه، يتفرع على كونه عاقلاً لنفسه، فهذه المفهومات الثلاثة يتفرع بعضها على البعض، فيمتنع كونها مفهوماً واحداً . والله أعلم.
The second issue: Know that every essence that intellects itself, that essence is intelligent and it itself is also intelligible. So its occurrence to itself by itself – is it identical to itself or not? The Chief Master (Ibn Sina) said: He, exalted, is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible, and all are one thing. That is because He is a simple substance; insofar as He is a simple quiddity, He is intellect; insofar as the quiddity is attained, He is intelligible; insofar as the quiddity is attained for Him, He is intelligent. So intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are one. And one may object to him from [several] aspects: The first: The terms intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are either synonymous or distinct. If the first, then there is no meaningful benefit in mentioning them; if the second, then they are either negative or positive concepts. The first is false because we have shown that the meaning of awareness and perception is not a negative concept, so the second remains, and there is no doubt that they are not distinct concepts but rather attributes of this essence, and then his statement that these three terms do not indicate multiplicity in meaning becomes false. The second aspect: And that we have shown that the meaning of awareness and perception is a relative, relational meaning, so it is impossible for it to be identical to that specific essence. The third aspect: Intellect is derived from the occurrence of intellection, and its being intelligible to itself is derived from its being intelligent to itself, so these three concepts are derived from one another, so it is impossible for them to be a single concept. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents a second issue (mas'ala) concerning the identity of intellect (‛aql), intelligent (‛āqil), and intelligible (ma‛qūl) in God. The author first states a principle: any essence that intellects itself is both intelligent and intelligible; the question is whether the relation of the essence to itself is identical to the essence or not. Ibn Sina (ash-Shaykh ar-Ra'īs) holds that for God, all three are one because He is a simple substance (dhāt mujarrada): as a quiddity (māhiyya) considered in itself, He is intellect; as that quiddity is attained (ḥaṣala), He is intelligible; and as the quiddity is attained for Him, He is intelligent. Then the author raises three objections. The first objection: the terms are either synonymous (mutarādifa) or distinct (mutabāyina). If synonymous, no benefit; if distinct, they are either negative (salbī) or positive (thubūtī). Since awareness is not negative, they are positive. Yet they are not distinct concepts but attributes of the same essence, which refutes Ibn Sina's claim that they indicate no multiplicity. The second objection: awareness and perception are relative relational concepts (nisbī iḍāfī), so they cannot be identical to the essence itself. The third objection: each concept is derived from another (e.g., intellect derives from intellection), so they are distinct by logical priority and posteriority, preventing them from being one concept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that relational attributes can still be identical to the essence in a simple being.
Correction: The objection argues that relational concepts inherently involve multiplicity (subject and object), so they cannot be identical to the simple essence.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, why can't awareness be identical to the essence?
A) Because awareness is a negative concept
B) Because awareness is a relational concept
C) Because awareness is a composite
D) Because awareness is eternal

Key Takeaway
The second and third objections highlight that relational and derivational aspects of the concepts prevent them from being identical to the simple essence or to each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attribute of 'All-Knowing,' do you instinctively reduce it to a mere concept in your mind, or do you recognize it as a real, relational attribute that you can never fully grasp? How does this lesson expose your tendency to intellectualize God rather than stand in awe?
?
If logical priority shows that intellect depends on intellection, and you claim to know God through your intellect, are you willing to admit that your knowing is derived and limited? Will you let this humble your theological certainty?
?
The passage concludes 'And God knows best.' Do you truly live as though God's knowledge is greater than your own? In what area of your life do you need to surrender your intellectual pride and say 'Allahu a'lam'?
